THE RED PAGE.
THE KING OF CARABAS.
“ Wanted—A truthful epitaph for defunct royalty.”—
Morrison Davidson.
And so the King of Carabas is dead,
Bewept by other kings and queens in dozens ;
In grief profound the nation bows its head,
And pensions off his five-and-forty cousins,
While Sorrow slowly beats her muffled drum,
And bids the people weep in slimy lane and slum.
He loved not much, but many. In his day,
From high-born damsels down to Sue and Sally,
He ran the gamut in his princely way,
And knew each shapely ankle in the ballet
His judgment was superb on waists and wine,
The dainty “fatted calf ” he deemed a thing divine.
He loved the turf, and all the band of sport
Revered him as of festive life the patron ;
For much he lingered round each gay resort
And scandalised each pious ancient matron.
The Course and all its crew he made a Regal Ring,
Among the jocks he rose—aye, every inch a king.
He laughed at learning, but he lived for Art;
There was no man in all that mighty nation
Could better judge how tailor did his part,
Or fold a tie so sweetly on occasion ;
Was none could half so deftly wield a cue,
Or read in Beauty’s blush if Beauty painted true.
How free he was from pride ! He once confessed
To break the bars of rank was his ambition,
And, therefore, he preferred plebeians blest
With surplus cash to any poor patrician ;
“ I owe my people love ! ” his polished accents fell—
(How much he owed in coin no soul could ever tell).
And so the King of Carabas is dead,
And grief has rent the great heart of the nation,
And sundry butchers bitter tears have shed,
And paupers weep in endless desolation ;
While fellow Royal Vultures wail the Thing
That one time wore a crown and called itself a king.
in The Bulletin.
P. LUPPIG.
A Bushman’s Notion.
IT may be interesting to our local painters to
know how some of the pictures in the Aus-
tralian court of N.S.W. Art Gallery look to
a bushman’s eye. Of their technical quality
I cannot judge. But it seems to me that, in so
far as they profess to represent ouf' country’s life
and scenery, they should represent it truly. It is
possible that in years to come,, if these pictures
are preserved, they may be referred to for
authority on the way we live now, the way Aus-
tralia looks now. Any such reference would, in
many cases, become misleading—owing to the
unabashed distortion of facts which characterises
the pictures.
For example, “ The Ploughman Homeward
Plods His Weary Way,” by Percy F. S. Spence,
seems a good picture ; but one can’t help looking
at the position of the horses’ feet. Notice that
the near front foot of the first horse, and the off
front foot of the second, are raised to a position in
which they are held when the blacksmith shoes
them. The other feet are stationary, as though
the horses were standing instead of plodding their
homeward way. If you watch a moving horse, no
matter how slowly moving, you will notice that
the hind foot is lifted first, so that it comes down
on a spot only the fraction of a second
after the front foot has left it. Some
horses overstep, some step short ; but in
all cases the front foot is so tardy in lifting that at
times you would think the hind foot must strike
it. Nor do plough horses lift the foot as high as
depicted ; they are more inclined to drag them,
like a working bullock. The colouring of this
picture is dark and gloomy. It does not give me
the impression of approaching night; it presents
a nightmare in which there is night at midday.
The colouring in most of the oil-paintings comes
as a shock to one who has for years feasted his
eyes on the thousand natural tints of the Aus-
tralian bush. I, find it particularly repugnant in
Streeton’s “ Cremorne Pastoral”; in Sid Long’s
“Pan,” “ By Tranquil Waters,” and ‘'Midday.”
Compare these with Hunt’s “ Sydney from North
Shore —evening.” The colouring is not true; but
it is more in harmony with Nature than Sid.
Long’s; as it is also in “A Billabong on the
Goulbum River,” by H. J. Johnstone. This
painting gives a clear idea of the subject treated.
One can look at a picture like this and recognise
the spot at once; but, presented in Sid. Long’s
dress, all the truth is gone.'
Why not give us our Bush as she is ?—so that we
who were rocked in her cradle, and lulled by her
murmuring voices, if fettered to a city life, may
go at times to look and freshen our memories with
glimpses of the dear wild haunts of the long ago.
The Bush has colours enough for any brush. Look
at any group of trees, even the stunted growths of
our arid deserts. There are all shades of green
and gold ; leaves hanging with a deep-green sur-
face, leaves showing a lighter under-tint in the
breeze ; the mellowing leaves that are falling, and
the sere leaves that flutter lightly on to green
grass or russet herbage. These should be easy
enough to reproduce without the application of
foreign tints; and yet, in the whole court, there
is nothing approaching a natural presentment.
Looking at the works he has given us, I am
certain that if Sid. Long were taken to a water-
hole wherein a red cqw was bogged, and asked to
paint it, he would paint the cow blue; and if a
black turtle were at the time perched on a white
log, one would see on the eaavas a purple turtle on
a pink log.
I was looking at one of Streeton’s pictures
when I heard a German farmer on my left criti-
cising B. E. Minns’ “ Season of Mists.” “Vhat
a shocking colour in de foreground ! ” he ex-
claimed. “See dose turkeys—they vos vhite an’
pink. Und dose pumpkins ! Yell, I been farmin’
a long times now, but .1 never growed pumpkins
mit dat colour pefore.”
' For my part, I could pass the pumpkins, but
Enough of colour ! Let us turn to something else.
The most striking picture in the court is
“Across the Blacksoil Plains,” by George W.
Lambert. This is a picture which one must stand,
say, ten feet away from to admire properly. Good
as it is, it will not bear a too close inspection.
The driver, for one thing, is not a “ veteran.” He
has a bulky stick of uniform thickness for a whip-
handle, and his whip is tied on by the wrong end.
The bottom bale is down on the hind wheel, and
one notices that the ropes round the bottom and
third tiers have been twitched somehow. I looked
in vain for the rollers, which should protrude from
between the bales. The “ roller ” is a stout,
smooth stick, placed at right angles to the twitch -
stick, and around it the slack rope is wound. No
teamster would be foolish enough to destroy his
rope by attempting to twitch his load together
without the roller.
Again, in this picture, every horse with its head
down has it inside the chains. Horses in boggy
ground are generally reefing and plunging, and,
with the exception of the shatters, are more
likely to reach over the chains. ' Then —where are
the spreaders ! The only hoof that is lifted clear
of the bog has no mud on it; it is clearly outlined
—is clearer, in fact, than the bodies of some of the
horses. And what is the grey horse pulling by ?
The chain is two feet below the hook on the
names, and appears to be hanging to a tug-chain.
The breeching, too, is too far back for the position
of the horse.
“The Wool Team,” by F. P. Mahony, is to
some extent misnamed. It shows a cockie, or
station driver, in a square-cut coat, and station
bullocks—and a station load. “The Station
Team ” would be more appropriate. But even a
station driver would not be so absent-minded as
to forget to put cogles in his bows.
Mahony’s “ Rounding-up a Straggler ”is better,
though the straightness of the stockman’s whip
somewhat deadens the effect.
In “As in the Days of Old,” by the same artist,
one sees a misconceived idea, for the man with
the whip, instead of having the bushranger at his
mercy, is in reality at the mercy of his antagonist.
He has let go the reins—a dangerous thing to do
in any case—and has lost his Qff-side stirrup.
There is nothing to prevent the bushranger ward-
ing the threatened blow; at the worst, he could
let it fall on his shoulders; then, catching his foe
by the arm or shoulder, swerve aside and drag
him off. In fact, the horses are, if anything,
slightly diverging, and it is odds on the bush-
ranger winning the day. But that isn’t the idea
that Mahony intended to convey.
“The Golden Fleece,” by Tom Roberts, and
“Cradling in Golden Gully,” by A. J. Fischer,
are pictures that “ talk,” because they are true.
“ The Prospector, ” by J. R. Ashton, is faulty
m almost every particular. His dress is rather
that of a pack-horse-drover’s cook, who has come
down to sink a soakage for the night’s supply of
water. _ Allowing him his pouches—articles fast
becoming obsolete in the bush—a strong objection
must be made to leggings and long-sleeve guern-
seys. Miners don’t work with guernsey-sleeves
rolled across the muscles—they cut them off ; and
they certainly would not be encumbered with
leggings. It reminds one of the man who stood
in two empty kerosene tins to adze a rail. The
“boyang” is the badge of every man with a pick.
This prospector’s dish has a ridge round the
bottom, and the rim is turned over—that is, out-
wardly. It would be better were it turned in.
No doubt it is a good dish for a bushman’s toilet-
room, or for mixing up a damper in; but a miner
would n’t be seen carrying it.
The man who put the handle in the pick did n’t
finish his job ; and the blade of the shovel is rusty,
which does n’t say much for the man’s energy.
The next is “ A Sketch,” by J. Mather. This
apparently depicts the approach of a storm, with
a strong wind blowing. A large tree is being
blown over, and another giant alongside is also
bending ; whilst several tall, thin saplings, almost
in a line with them, and certainly in the direct
path of the wind, are undisturbed. This is re-
versing the natural order of things with a ven-
geance.
Arthur Streeton’s “A Surveyor’s Gamp” is
very unsatisfactory. It gives one no idea of a camp
in the bush, but a good idea of a dummy rigged in
a park. The pole has.no visible support—except
a post in front. One must imagine that the pole
is nailed to that—and surveyors, as a rule, don’t
carry nails. This is merely a fly ; the tents are in
the background. One would wish the picture
reversed. -
The structure in front is probably meant for a
galley, though it is too smudged to tell exactly
what it is. Anyhow, in our snake-ridden bush,
only new-ehums would utilise a big hollow log for
side supports. A man stands there with a theo-
dolite as though he had climbed over the log with
it, or lugged it all the way round, to oblige a
photographer. That effect of posing is at all
times ahominable.
One turns from this with pleasure to “The
Wounded Duck,” by N. Cayley. This is the most
naturally-painted picture in the Australian court.
It is to be regretted that the artist has spoilt the
general effect by one little blunder. The bird has
been winged, and the wounded wing is uppermost,
whilst the side supported by the uninjured member
is down. Any sportsman knows that, on winging
a bird, the injured side, having lost part, or whole,
of its support, is the first to drop. However, one
can overlook a little thing like that when tlm bird
itself is so faithfully painted. The pity is tha’t our
other artists do not endeavour to follow Nature
as closely, to paint her in her own garb—to paint
her as N. Cayley paints her.
N.s.w.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.
The Girl and the Island.
P'jM HEY have not left off stoning wizards
B vet, for all they may say. I have a friend
JL —he is a painter—who has given house-
room to one, and he tells me that every
time he brings the wizard’s wonder-work before
the public-, the school of the matter-of-fact make
fun of it with jibe and sneer. The missiles they
hurl are not exactly the same as those that bruised
and wounded dead, old wizards, but they hurt,
all the same. The wizard that my friend houses
has the power of transmuting the baser metals
into gold, and, of my own knowledge, I am aware
that he has healed and made whole again more
than one who was unwell —even when the wound
was at the heart.
When a wizard takes up his residence in your
house, be sure he will draw the blinds and dull
the place to twilight. Then your neighbours will
say: “ What a curious, unworldly, good-for-
nothing, rambling, old house is that across the
street there ! Why doesn’t the mah get rid of his
wizard : everyone is laughing at him.”
But it happens, now and again, that the world
grows strangely wrought with the wizard’s won-
der-work—it may be a hundred years afterwards,
when the house where he lived is in ruins. Then
the people come, and they put roses above the
ruins, never pausing to think that the place where
he resided was dften without food, whole days at
a time.
* » * *
As we sit smoking in his studio with rejected
pictures round about us, my friend, the painter,
opens his heart to me. He talks to me of one
thing and another, but mostly of a tall young girl
and an island. The tall young girl, I know, exists;
but I would not like to vouch for the island.
Any way, I have never told him that I doubted the
existence of either, there being many others in
the world to play the sceptic.
My friend does not sleep well, and the wizard
who resides with him, thinking, perhaps, that he
may have done something to cause these
periods of unrest, has given him an island
—as a thing to occupy his mind. Think! that
is its much as a king could do—but no king
in all the world has in his giving such an
island as this that ray friend got from the
wizard. And in this wise he speaks of it:
“Ig& to my island frequently. Would you like
to know where it is? Well, it is in the Coral
Seas, and it is built of coral—beautiful, branching
coral, white and pink. It has a reef all around to
keep away unruly seas, and it lies in the centre of
a blue lagoon. It is large, so very large that a
ship takes quite a long time to sail around it.
“It has mountains inland, and in the moun-
tains are savages, with black, blood-streaked eyes,
and teeth an inch long, and yellow, like a tiger’s.
These fellows are cannibals, and my island would
not be a pleasant place to live in, only that I
have another race of men there. This race
came from Greece in an ancient day. They were
beset by storm, but their gods were kindly, and
brought them at length to my lovelier island.
They never weep—they do not know what sorrow
is—and it is only when the cannibals come down
to war with them, and some of them are slain,
that they become grave.
“Inland, there are dense forests, damp, dripping
places, full of great, green serpents with yellow
eyes. These serpents have silver manes, and,
when they lift their heads to strike, I am minded
of beautiful, bad women. The sodden tracks are
trodden by lions and tigers and elephants and
jaguars. Anyone who says that this is impossible
does not know what he is talking about, because in
a certain realm all things are possible.
“When the tide is out the dugongs come up to
the salt marsh-meadows, arid they munch the
rank grass, and when they roll in the ooze the
air is full of sucking noises. There are sharks in
the lagoon—great grey fellows, with row after
row of teeth that dove-tail like interlocking
fingers, and, when they fasten on an arm or a leg, it
is no use in the world to struggle. But I have
seen men struggle all the same ; and I suppose
they could not help it, because we are not all
philosophers, and some of us love life very much.
“There is a fringe of palm trees round the coast,
and one of them is so lovely that I do not think
the world holds such another. When I look at it
I am reminded of the tall young girl.
• * * *
“I take friends to my island sometimes, although
they do not know it; and one man I take very
often, because he needs it. He is enchanted.
Let me tell you what I have seen.
“One day before I came to own my island, we had
been fishing, he and I, and when night fell we
went to our room and I slept. But there was no
sleep for him, and he sat beside a table and
wrote. Hours went by and I awoke. The pencil
had fallen from his hand, but there was no sleep
in his eyes, only enchantment. I knew it for en-
chantment all at once. How lam going to
describe what I saw in those eyes I do not know.
Have you ever seen smoke drifting across the face
of the moon when the moon is low and very red ?
Well, it was a sister thing to that. Shadows were
moving there, lifting, falling, drifting slowly
round and round about. I knew that these were
visions come from their secret places to look out
on the sleeping world. Some day he whom they
inhabit may give them a voice, and then they
■ shall startle you with their beauty.
“ Now it is not good that a man’s eyes should be
peopled by visions, and as my island is the half-
way house to slumber—the shrine where the mind
gets sanctuary from worry—l take him there. He
does not know, as he tosses to and fro in a hot
horror of wakefulness—part of which is begotten
of physical pain, and the rest of anxiety as to how
he is to play the man’s part, and face the morrow ;
as his eyes burn, and the kindly darkness becomes
hateful, he does not know that I have taken him
to my island, and that he lies sleeping under a
palm.
* * * *
“In my island are red flowers. They are large,
loose-leafed things that hold themselves up, like a
fawn’s ear, when.the wind purrs, them. They
grow on vines, a score on every vine, and the
women wind them round about, till their bodies
are an exquisite minglement of brown and red.
If you would know how beautiful brown and red
can be, consider a dark woman’s cheeks when she
-blushes. As I lie under the palm trees the women
dance. It is an old measure caught by them in
some mood of woodland merriment ; a joy-of-foot
bequeathed to them in some green moment by
nymph and faun. They have drunk of palm wine,
and such a glory leaps along their veins and
splashes from their eyes that trie sea and the sun
seem to dance, too—the palms and the flowers and
the paradise birds in the palm-trees.
“ Then I say to myself—‘ It is no wonder the
world was lovely when Greece was great.’
“ They bring me fruits of a keen relish when I
am hungry—fruits in which I can taste the scent
of the blossom. The pulp in some is blood-red,
and some have a coating of amber enclosing a
heart of cream, while others have a secret excel-
lence, captured from the sun, the moon or the
passing wind, and imprisoned in their cores.
They give me fish to eat —fish with flesh as white
as pearl.
m * * *
“When the mood is on me I go up into the dense
forest, and there my flesh is thrilled with a
pleasant sense of peril. All about green eyes
■gem the dark,—hungry eyes with malignant lights
in them. To and fro, on the sodden leaves, I
hear the pad, pad, of the tiger, and I fancy how at
times his claws sheathe and unsheathe with the
desire to rend flesh. A lion roars his challenge
down the wind, and from a distance comes a noise
of lust-anger, the trumpeting of elephants at their
midnight courtship.
“That forest is like no living forest in the world.
It is peopled with shadow-shapes that hide in the
darkness amongst the wet black trees, beings that
cheat the eye, but hurt the soul. The writer of
‘ Leonore ’ hunted in such a forest, and there are
others who have made strange captures in like
places, but who feared to show them to the world,
lest men should think them mad.”
Yesterday I found the artist jingling gold. He
had made money by the sale of a picture, and was
in the finest of moods.
“ Well, what about the island ? ” I asked.
He knocked the ash from a cigar. “Haven’t
been there lately,” he said.
He had a rose in his coat, and the reason flashed
in on me. I said, “You’ve been with ”
“Just so,” he interrupted; “the tall young
girl.”
“Well, it’s good-bye to the island!” I mur-
mured.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
I WOULD NOT BE A KING.
I would not be an idle king, to sit upon a throne,
And sway a golden sceptre o’er a nation all my own ;
To be the people’s'idol, ever worshipped by the throng,
With flattering incense to be served, and mercenary song ;
To be the envy of the rich, and wonder of the poor :
A situation that would be I never could endure.
To live on choicest dainties that the kitchen can produce ;
To quaff from golden chalices the grape’s fermented
juice ;
To see a thousand at my beck, ten thousand at my nod,
Low bend the knee in homage, and protest I am a god:
Though this to-morrow could be mine, and earth my
praises sing,
I’d rather be a working man than any idle king.
To ride within a chariot before the gaping crowd,
And hear the shout, “ God save the King ! ” repeated oft
and loud;
My smile to make a nation smile, my frown to make it
sad ;
And everything that kings may have to make my spirits
glad—
No : take your trappings all away ; for I can truly sing,
I’d rather be an honest man ; I would not be a King.
The sceptre is a bauble ; it might do for boys at play :
A man of dignity would throw the childish thing away.
The crown is but a dunce's cap, that silly people wear ;
And home-made bread is quite as good as any kingly fare.
Then I would not exchange my lot; such toys away I
fling;
I’d rather be just what I am ; I would not be a king.
The multitude that bow to him are only fools .or knaves ;
And those who guard his person but a host of armed
slaves :
And though they worship him to-day, to-morrow, for a
groat,
They’d throw away their loyalty, and cut his kingly
throat :
For it is to the office, not the man, they homage pay ;
They'd kneel unto a beggar were he made a King to-day.
If kings would rule their mighty selves, they’d have
enough to do ;
To put their families right, I fear, were more than they’d
get through :
Let each one mind his business, then, and wipe his
troubled brow ;
The people And that they can walk without assistance
now :
The drowsy earth has waked at last, and learned one use-
ful thing—
That men can rule themselves, without a fellow called a
king.
For kings are only paupers, who are by the nations fed ;
And, were the people wiser, they should labour for their
bread.
We need no kings to reign o'er us ; we can do well
without ;
And if we could but have our wish, we’d send them
“ right about.”
Each man should reign in righteousness, and rule o’er
his own heart ;
And kings and all their trumpery should evermore
depart.
William Denton.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Song of Darkness.
With the sound of smothered laughter—with the
tread of stealthy feet,
And the gleam of eyes where pleasant passions
shine ;
With the surge of sighs that echo to the lisp of
night-winds fleet,
You are here to light the darkness, Lady mine !
With the flash of satin shoes upon the green,
And the swing of hurried pulses where there never
pulse has been,
I have found you in the darkness, Lady mine !
You are here with me in darkness, with the star-
light in your hair—
Shining silver in your hair so silken-fine —
With a curve upon your lifted lips which spurs
my own to dare,
You are dazzling in the darkness, Lady mine !
With the mystery of midnight in your eyes,
And your dancing glances showing where the
path of pleasure lies,
I adore you in the darkness, Lady mine !
Though the lawn lies black beneath us, and the
grey boughs gloom above,
You have quickened in my heart the Flame
divine;
And the night among the myrtles offers time and
place for love —
You are here with me in darkness, Lady mine !
You are here with me at midnight, all alone ;
I can feel your quick heart throb against the
thunder of my own,
And —I’ve kissed you in the darkness, Lady
mine!
v.
FRANK HENTY.
The Flowers that Die.
Little, pattering baby-feet,
Where the violets bloom a-sweet!
Dirty, dimpled, dangerous hands
Where the gallant rose-bush stands I
(Was there infant known to live
Who was not inquisitive ?)
Delving down with sturdy will
Under phlox and daffodil,
Under shrubs and costly trees,
Crying to the budding peas—
“ Won’t you flowers let baby know
What it ig that makes you grow ? ”
But the flowers naught reply,
Only .droop their heads and die.
Children of a greater growth,
In the fancy fields of sloth,
Seeing flowers of ancient years—
Sprung from superstitious fears—
Blooming with a fragrance rare
’Neath the well-paid gardeners’ care,
Probe, with hands that know not ruth,
Probe beneath to seek the Truth ;
And the flowers naught reply,
Only droop their heads and die.
V.
MILKY WHITE.
The Harp: An Interpretation.
There stood my harp upon the breeze-blown deck,
And through it played'the briny breath of heaven »
Above there sailed nor cloud nor tiny fleck,
But through the sunbeams we were onward driven.
The idle strings, low moaning, mused of pain
Or some old memory of buried love,
Then rose into a wilder, clearer strain,
And falling, murmured like a mateless dove.
And now it ceased, as if to wipe a tear,
And Silence, dumbly speaking, brought relief,
When waking Memory wailed upon the ear
The piercing passion of a speechless grief.
And then it rose as from beneath the tomb,
Swift-soaring heavenward on the wings of Hope,
And music had the power to banish gloom
And make a harp of every mast and rope.
Then swifter blew the fairy breeze along,
And Hope led up to joyous Victory ;
Each trembling string thrilled with a rapturous
song,
Some bridal song it seemed of sky and sea !
A gentler breath now touched a dearer note,
And forth there thrilled the spirit of all Peace ;
Across some classic sea we seemed to float,
Or round the Blessed Isles of ancient Greece.
Once more there came the subtle trembling
change,
And Joy and Love blent into blissful calm ;
Then swelling, thrilling through its mighty range,
It fell asleep as some spent angel’s psalm.
T.
J. BOFTON.
To Nancy.
Fairer than the finest fancy
Of the love-lorn swain,
Nature formed thy face, Miss Nancy,
But forgot thy brain;
Wonder not, if all impassioned
At the beauty she had fashioned,
She forgot thy brain.
Yet, despair not! —Wisdom, Folly,
Shall thy victims fall,
Chase away thy melancholy,
Thou shalt conquer all;
By thy barren mental spaces,
By thy sweet and comely graces,
Thou shalt conquer all.
Death to vanity, to talent;
Maiden, from thine eyes
Hazel, laughter slays the gallant,
Platitudes the wise.
Nought may lay the spells that oharm us,
Morituri Salutamus!
We, the fools and wise.
V.

SATURDAY, JULY 13, 1901.
A Differential Tariff for Britain.
It is anticipated that the Federal tariff will
come up for discussion next month, and
when it does the advocates of a differential
tariff in favor of Great Britain and Ireland
will doubtless bring forward their little fad,
and push it for all it is worth. When they
do, all that can be asked is that it be con-
sidered intelligently, upon its merits, as a
plain business proposition, without any bel-
lowing, flag-flapping, or hysterics about the
“duty we owe to - the beloved mother
country.” As a matter of fact, Australia
owes just the same duty to Britain as Britain
ow r es to Australia, and there is no need for
hysterics. Years ago certain British people
decided that they would come out here
(those who were sent out here were such a
small minority that they may be left out of
the question), and some other British people
decided that they would remain where
they were. There is no special virtue
in remaining where you are—an oyster
can do as much as that; and the brother
who stayed at home has no right to expect
the brother who went abroad to worship
him for ever after, merely because of his
stationary character. Australians fought at
Crecy and Agincourt and Bannockburn and
Minden quite as well as Britons did ; they
might not be called Australians then, but
that is a mere detail. If Britain admires us
and feels grateful to us for our valuable
assistance at these and hundreds of other
battles, well and good. If Britain doesn't,
that is also well and good. It matters no-
thing either way. But a plain business pro-
position shouldn’t be confused by any absurd
theories to the effect that when two brothers
parted many years ago, the brother who
went away, and all his descendants, should
for ever afterwards be filled with humble and
prostrate reverence for the brother who
didn’t go, merely because he didn’t go, and
for all his descendants. Let us be business-
like.
• * * *
The next part of the theory of the dif-
ferential tariff crowd is that Australia owes a
debt of gratitude to Britain because Britain
is the only country which admits Australia’s
products free of duty, and that Australia
should give special concessions to British
goods in return as a pure matter of business.
Even if the basis of this theory were true—
which it isn’t—the deduction is wholly un-
true. This is really and truly the situation
which the differential tariffists lay before us:
(1) Britain is mostly Freetrade because it thinks that
Freetrade pays it best. Its motives are purely selfish.
It doesn’t propose to benefit anybody save itself.
(2) Australia is mostly Protectionist because it thinks
Protection pays it best. Its motives are purely selfish.
It doesn’t propose to benefit anybody save itself. So far,
the two are exactly level.
(3) Britain admits Australian goods on exactly the same
terms as it admits the goods of strangers, rivals and
enemies—France, Russia, Germany, and the world in
general. Australia treats British goods similarly.
(4) And it is proposed that Australia should give
British goods a preference in the Australian market, so
that Britain shall be able to annex all the trade of this
country. And on the other hand Australia is to get no
preference in the British market. Australian goods are
to scramble against the goods of all the world without
any assistance whatever. Australia is to charge 5 to 10
per cent, higher duties on French, German, Russian,
American, and other goods than on British goods.
Britain is not to charge 5 or 10 or anything else per cent,
more on French, German, Russian, or American goods
than on Australian goods. Britain is to get a vastly
better show than anyone else in our market without our
getting any better show than anyone else in its market.
The Briton is to get Protection in Australia that he
doesn’t enjoy in his own country, and which he de-
nounces in his own country. And the Australian Free-
trade push, which says Protection is utterly bad, is
mostly in favor of giving him the accursed thing.
Considering the assistance which our ancestors
gave to Britain in its battles, from Hastings
down to Waterloo and later, and further
considering that but- for their aid Britain
might have been beaten at Trafalgar and
Napoleon I. have poured his army into
London—there is no evidence to the con-
trary—this one-sided proposition doesn’t
show the gratitude on Britain’s part towards
Australia that might fairly have been ex-
pected.
• * * *
Britain hasn’t even done anything remark-
able in the way of letting in Australia’s pro-
ducts more cheaply than other countries do.
The idea that it does so is mainly a supersti-
tion. About five-sixths of Australia’s
exports to the outer world consist of
wool, gold and silver. Practically every
State of any importance in the world
admits our gold, silver, &c., just as
freely as Britain does. Quite a number
of important States admit our wool just as
freely as Britain does. In short, so far as
concerns the great bulk of our trade, there
are many leading States which treat Austra-
lia just as liberally as Britain ever did.
Some of them, in the small matter of wines
and tobacco, which may be a large matter
one day, treat this country better than
Britain does. And if this country, in a
frenzy of “ loyalty,” singles these countries
out for special and undeserved ill-treatment,
they will probably retaliate, with disastrous
results. Canada tried the differential tariff'
experiment, and Germany retaliated so
promptly that the Canadian wheat to
Germany dropped from 38,000 tons in 1898
to 900 tons in 1900. The year before
Canada provoked this ill-judged conflict
Germany’s purchases of Canadian goods,
though still small, had almost doubled, so it
was a very promising little field of commerce
wherein Canada went and hanged itself.
And it gained no compensating advantage.
British purchases didn't increase to make up
for the loss of foreign customers, and the
Dominion's exports decreased six million
dollars in 1899.
• * • *
It is customary to assume that our British
customer is so much more important than all
other customers put together that Britain
ought to receive special concessions and
special consideration. There are two or
three side-issues, however, to be considered.
In the first place, a very large proportion of
the goods which Britain takes from Aus-
tralia are really gold and silver which go to
pay interest on our numerous debts. Any
other country would take these imports just
as gladly, if it was offered the chance. A con-
siderable proportion consists of wool bought
from us by Belgium, France, Germany and
other countries, and which is simply sent to
London for transhipment because London is
the great transhipment port of the world.
When allowance is made for these matters
Australia buys more goods from Britain than
Britain buys from Australia—the money
sent to London to pay interest on our debts
isn’t an import “bought” from us by
Britain. And it is Britain which should
rush to show its gratitude to us by a differ-
ential tariff—not the other way about.
* * * *
Even apart from the fact that our real
trade with Britain is much smaller than it
looks, because tribute is included in trade,
and that our real trade with other countries
is a little larger than it looks, there is this
matter to be considered. In 1899 the
United Kingdom took from the Common-
wealth, in all, £26,560,000 worth of pro-
duce. Other countries took £20,860,000
worth. Deducting the money sent to Lon-
don to pay interest with, “ other countries ”
are really the better customer of the two.
Coghlan’s “Seven Colonies,” in giving the
results for a series of years, unfortunately
lumps the Commonwealth and Maoriland
together, but taking all Australasia he shows
(“Seven Colonies” for 1899-1900, p. 337) that
between 1891 and 1899 our exports to the
United Kingdom increased by £3,000,000;
exports to places outside the British Empire
increased by £7,000,000, and exports to
British possessions, other than Britain oi
Australasia, increased by £4,000,000. there-
fore it is proposed to severely kick the
countries whose purchases increased by
£11,000,000 in order to gratify (not even to
buy an advantage from, but merely to
gratify) the country whose purchases only in-
creased by £3,000,000.
• * * *
If Australia puts the trade of all the
world outside Britain at a special disadvant-
age, then the outside world will probably
retaliate. That outside world doesn’t really
need Australian goods very badly. It can
get practically everything Australia has to
sell—wool, meat, minerals, coal, and all the
rest—from South America and other coun-
tries. And it is a serious thing to jump
comprehensively on our best customer, lhis
is how the figures went for the Common-
wealth in 1899 (Coghlan’s “ Statistics of
the Seven Colonies ” for 1899, page 8) :
Exports of Imports of Balance
Common- Common- in favor of
wealth wealth Common-
to from wealth.
United Kingdom .. £26,564,000 £21,231,000 £5,333,000
Other Countries .. 20,862,000 11,389,000 9,473,000
The Commonwealth requires all that
£14,806,000 balance to pay interest
on its debts to Britain As a means
of raising it the trade with other
countries is about twice as valuable
as the trade with the United Kingdom.
It is the trade with countries outside the
United Kingdom which keeps Australia afloat.
So far as the mere exchange of goods (other
than gold, for which a market can always be
found) is concerned, the trade with Britain
leaves no balance, or none worth mentioning,
with which to pay our interest bill, and the
trade with other countries is everything.
Therefore, if the Commonwealth Parliament
deliberately declares war against the great
aggregate customer who keeps us out of the
insolvency court, it will be a very mad
Legislature indeed. Between 1891 and
1899, Australia’s exports to Germany in-
creased by over £2,000,000 —an increase
not far short of that shown by our exports
to the United Kingdom. To declare war
against that one customer alone would be a
tolerably reckless proceeding.
Federal L. fe Assurance—Again.
So far as concerns the House of Representatives
the question of establishing a Government Life
Assurance Department has apparently been hung
up on the nail of Procrastination that sticks out of
the back of the door of Apathy. It may recon-
sider the matter, or the Senate may take a bigger
hold of the opportunity than the Representatives
are doing, but so far the hanging-up tendency is
very conspicuous. The only visible product of the
debate so far has been a great mass of painfullv-
silly literature professing to explain why a
Government Life Assurance Department can’t
possibly succeed. For instance, William Lyne
(a director and shareholder of the Citizens’ Life
Assurance Co., with which a Federal Government
Life Assurance Department would necessarily
compete) offered these remarks (S.M. Herald
report) :
He found that in Maoriland, although the Government
some years ago introduced a general system of life assur-
ance, only 390 public servants assured with the Govern-
ment. As regarded the annuities principle, he found that
unless the Government subsidised the fund men entering
at, say, 25 years could secure an annuity of £152 at the
age of 60 only by the payment of £22 a year.
* * * •
The statement that only 290 public servants in
Maoriland have insured in the Government Dept,
may be true, or it may be a hideous blunder. It
certainly looks like a hideous blunder, for these
reasons: At the date of the latest returns, the
whole amount of ordinary life policies in Maori-
land was £21,762,155, divided among 10 institu-
tions ; and the Maoriland Government office held
£9,558,166 of this, or nearly half. In other words,
it does almost as much business as the other nine
institutions put together, and this despite the fact
that the A.M.P. was doing business in Maoriland
for eight years before the Government office was
founded. It seems highly improbable that an in-
stitution which does nearly half the business of the
State should only have 290 public servants among
its clients. But, if it has, then it must owe its
prosperity, even more than was commonly sup-
posed, to the support of the people at large, and
Lyne’s theory only further shows what splendid
scope there is for Government Assurance among
the masses. The proposal before the Federal
Parliament was that the Federal public servants
should be compulsorily insured in a Federal
Government office, and on that basis the said
office, it has been shown, would start with 1500
public servants among its customers —not 290.
There would be any number more later on, but
there would be 1500 to begin with. And in addi-
tion to this, according to Lyne’s figures, there is
such a brilliant chance to do business with the
public that the Maoriland Government Assurance
Department has 39,366 policies in force, of which
39,076 are issued to the general public; and over
£3,000,000 of funds. What more does Lyne
want ?
* * « *
Furthermore it appears that William Lyne has
struck the terrifying and insurmountable fact that
the compulsorily-insured public servant, if he
went in on the annuity plan, starting at age 25,
would have to pay £22 a year in order that he
might retire at age 60 on an annual allowance of
£152 per annum. The Sydney Evening News
said sorrowfully that these “ unfavorable statist-
ics” threw a wet blanket on the proceedings.
Seeing that neither William Lyne’s private office,
the Citizens, nor any other office that this paper is
aware of, could give the same annuity at any
lower price—in fact very few would give it at that
price—it is difficult to see where the moist blanket
comes in. Lyne struck the disheartening fact
that a Government Life Assurance Department
would have to charge the public servant almost as
much as he would pay any where else, while giving
him much better security. If that is such a per-
fectly new discovery that it falls like an avalanche,
it must have been a very unprepared person that
it fell upon.
„ » * •
A further reason advanced against a Federal
Government Life Assurance Department is that
it wouldn't have a revenue from lapsed policies
which other offices have, therefore its prosperity
would be less. Heaven only knows why this
should be. The Federal public servant certainly
wouldn’t be permitted to let his policy lapse while
he remained a public servant, but when he left
the service—as he occasionally does—taking his
policy with him, he might let it lapse now and ti en
As for that portion of the general public which
insured in the Federal Department, it would pro-
bably let its policy lapse in the same lament a tile
and slipshod way .which characterises it in its
dealings with other offices. Seeing that, by Wil-
liam Lyne s calculation, the slipshod general pub-
lic supplies 90 per cent, of the business of M.mri-
land’s State Assurance Department, this might
prove to be an item as regards the Federal office.
Anyhow, if it is a fact that the existence of
a large body of regular and compulsory Civil Ser-
vice policy-holders—a class which couldn’t let its
policies lapse—would reduce the profits, it would
be a mean thing to let these disastrous persons go
and insure in Lyne’s private assurance company,
or any other private institution, and thereby in-
jure it when it wasn’t looking. And, curiously
enough, one of the reasons of the A.M.P. board
for wanting to do business in England was that
life business there was more stable—there were
fewer lapses— and consequently it paid better.
* m • rn
Melbourne Age also has a theory on the sub-
ject, and a superlatively silly one :
To enter upon an innovation so important and far-
reaching as public service assurance with only 1500 sub-
scribers would not, in the opinion of the majority of
Ministers, be a wise step. The life assurance companies,
it is pointed out, have sources of revenue which would
not be open to the Commonwealth. Upon the establish-
ment of new branches large sums are set aside for invest-
ment, and the revenues include the earnings of this
money, as well as the premiums of the assured, upon
which the Commonwealth, for the present at all events,
would alone have to depend. Further than that, there
would be no safeguards in the creation of averages by the
successes in one sphere of operations being set off
against the failures in another. These difficulties may,
of course, be overcome, but the feeling of Ministers ap-
pears to be that in taking a new departure into the
realm of assurance the Commonwealth should not tinker
with the question, but should be prepared to act upon a
thoroughly organised and well-constructed plan that will
ensure ultimate success.
Yet, as The Bulletin pointed out last week, no
other life office started with anything like 1500
assured subscribers, and with a certainty of
issuing 1500 policies in its first year, apart from
all the business it might do with the public. It
took the A.M.P. more than 10 years to issue 1500
policies. It was in its twelfth year before it had
1500 policies on its books simultaneously. It takes
very few policies to make a large and prosperous
office if they are permanent and are for decent
amounts, as the civil service policies would be. The
great Equitable of England has nearly £5,000,000
of funds, and they are increasing at the rate of
£170,000 a year ; yet it only issued 1713 new
policies in the five years up to 1899. The London
Life (funds over £4,600,000, and increasing at the
rate of £120,000 a year) only issued 1585 new
policies in the six years up to 1899. The Federal
Department would start with about as much
certain business in its first year as these great
institutions get in five or six years. As for the
other point, every mutual life office has to depend
wholly on its premiums to begin with. The
Government Life Assurance Department would
begin just as the A.M.P. and every other mutual
institution did, only with the immense start it
would enjoy it must progress very much
faster. The A.M.P. had only £96 accu-
mulated at the end of its first year,
and £558 at the end of its second ; and it was
seven years old before it had £IO,OOO on hand.
The Federal Government Life Department would
accumulate funds, just as the A.M.P. did. And
it would have exactly the same chance in “ the
creation of averages by the successes in one sphere
of operations being set off against the failures in
another,” as the A.M.P. has, also the National
Mutual, Mutual Life Association, Colonial Mutual,
City Mutual, Citizens, Maoriland Government
Department, &c. In other words, its business
would spread all over the country just as theirs
does.
* * * *
To create the Department, it is only necessary
to adopt bodily the premium tables of the A.M.P.,
to make regulations for the investment of the
funds in State and good municipal securities, to
appoint a capable manager and instruct him how
far he can go in advertising expenses, and to get a
cheap office with some tables, desks and stationery.
Even an actuary wouldn’t be wanted till the first
triennial valuation fell due. The writer has
known two dissatisfied canvassers and a sacked
insurance clerk get together and found a life
assurance office, which is now a pretty large and
prosperous concern, and they did it in less time
than the Federal Parliament has already spent in
talking about the matter. Even then they were
delayed by troubles connected with raising the
money for furniture and preliminary charges.
* * "* *
As regards the compulsory insurance of civil
servants, the kind of thing which William Lyne
foreshadows is distinctly not what is wanted. The
old N.S.W. Superannuation Fund idea, whereby
the civil servant made provision for his own old
age, if he had any, but made none for his widow
and orphans, was a disaster all along. As a
specimen of the kind of thing that is wanted the
A.M.P. Society issues a form of policy whereby a
man may insure his life for, say, £IOOO, and on
attaining the age of 60 he ceases to pay premiums
and begins to receive an annuity of £SO per year,
which lasts his life-time. (It can also be
arranged that he shall collect the accumulated
bonuses subject to a discount.) The annuity
of £SO a year is continued to his widow.
At her death the annuity ceases, and the £IOOO
is paid over to the heirs. If the assured dies
before the age of 60 the annuity to his widow com-
mences immediately. That is the. ideal provision
for death and old age. Every public servant who
was not sufficiently insured before the Federal
Public Service Act came in force should be re-
quired, on reaching the age of 21, to take out a
policy in this or some very similar basis, for not
less than a certain fixed amount, though with full
permission to take more than that amount if he
is so inclined. If only insured for the minimum
he should be required to take out further policies
as his income increases. If he left the service he
would take his policies and all hi; rights and
privileges with him ; insurance would then cease
to be compulsory, but if he chose to emtinue pay-
ing his premiums, he would incur no loss by leav-
ing the service. If he didn’t so choose, he could
give up his policy and collect the su render value
like any other foolish priv ate citizen. There would
be no attempt to make him insure for sufficient
to provide him with an annuity of £152 in his
old age, as mentioned by William Lyne. The
average public servant can’t afford to insure to
anything like that extent in a private office, and
to suggest that a State office must either think
out a way of insuring him to this extent, or else be
a wet blanket and a failure, is an absurdity. But
it should be possible to so arrange things* that
every public servant, by the time he is 50 years of
age, shall have an annuity of £SO a year comino- to
him and to his widow, with £IOOO to divide among
his children afterwards. And if this annuity can
neither be sold, mortgaged, nor seized for debt
the Government Life Assurance Department will
have done as much for him as can be expected


Any provision beyond that must be a matter for
his own consideration.
* * * •
The reason why a Federal Government Life
Assurance Department could give better value
than any private office would simply be, as
pointed out last week, that its expenses would be
far less. It would pay no commission ; its busi-
ness as regards the civil service would cost nothing
to acquire, and it would take whatever other busi-
ness came to it on the same terms. It could dis-
pense with agents, branch offices, and the like—
the post-office would be its branch office every-
where. Its advertisement could be hung cheaply
outside every Federal public building, and it
could be advertised gratis in all Government pub-
lications. The chief British office which doesn’t
pay commission (the Equitable) works at an ex-
pense rate of 3| per cent. —-little more than one-
third of even the A.M.P.’s low rate ; and with a
great saving in branch offices and expenses a
Federal Life Assurance Department could do the
work even more economically than the Equitable.
The Clergy Mutual works for about 4 per cent.,
and the London Life at less than 4 per cent., ex-
penses—also on the no-commission plan. And
none of these offices started with 1500 sure cus-
tomers, with a prospect of an early increase to
10,000 or more.
* # ♦ *
The situation, at time of writing, is this. The
Federal Government, by the mouth of William
Lyne, says it approves of the idea of Govern-
ment Life Assurance, and is going to see about
the matter next year or later on. Meanwhile it
proposes to pass the compulsory assurance clause
of the Public Service Bill, and compel its present
1500 uninsured public servants to insure in pri-
vate offices, instead of making them the nucleus
of a big Government Life Office. Then, having
thrown away its 1500 available clients, it will
possibly start business. Meanwhile it is proposed
to exercise some kind of supervision over the pri-
vate office ; the public servants are only to insure
in approved institutions. Lyne had that system
in N.S.W. It works so well that the N.S.W.
Public Service Board some time ago approved of
a very small proprietary office with £4llO alleged
p lid-up capital (of which £OIO was visible assets
and £3500 was “ goodwill ”) as a good, reliable
place for public servants to put their premiums
into !
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Expulsion of Findley.
Feom one of Australia’s foremost, best esteemed,
and most cultured Radicals :
Editor Bulletin.— Do your readers know the true
meaning of the Findley expulsion from Vic. Parliamant ?
Findley is an active, vigilant, too strong-mouthed Labor
member ; hated by the Opposition ; a thorn in the side of
Peacock. He got into the House as representative of
Melbourne city at the general election, because the com-
mercial vote was split, and because no elector can now
vote in two constituencies on same day. But at a bye-
election the commercial voters of St. Kilda and Toorak
can pour into the city and swamp the Labor vote. This
is the kind of “ one-man-one-vote ” with which George
Turner wants democrats to be content ! Peacock and
his reactionary allies know that at a bye-election Findley
has not a show. They know that they can expel Findley
without ugly consequences-—such as happened when
London again returned John Wilkes. Ministry and Oppo-
sition forgot their jealousies in their hatred of the Labor
party and of Findley ; they have combined and succeeded
—for the present.
What did Findley do ? Nothing. He was the nominal
publisher of the Tocsin. He did not, he assured the
House, know what was in it more than old Mr Ebsworth
used to know what was in the Argus. He expressed
regret that the article of the Irish People had been copied.
Had the paper not been a Labor paper, the explanation
would have been accepted, and the extreme step would
not have been taken. But the as yet unlcnighted leaders
of the House wanted to get rid of him, to get him out of
.his seat for Flinders Lane, to show Chamberlain and the
Colonial Office how hysterically loyal w;e can be, to gain
eulogy from Jingo papers. The majority of the House
followed the leaders like sheep. They took the safe, but
not the noble, course to preserve their “ loyalty ” in the
eyes of the country. It is to be hoped that the advice
which Peacock gave Madden— or which Madden gave
Peacock— will be made public. We are entitled to
know it.
The resolution of the House declares Findley to be
guilty of seditious libel. The charge is false. To test it,
let any newspaper print the Irish People article in full,
without comment, in this loyal Australia, and let the
authorities prosecute the publisher. The cowards dared
not prosecute—dared not leave it to the courts. It would
have been time enough to expel Findley after he had
been convicted in due course of law. There might have
been some reason for expulsion if, in addition to being
convicted of libel, he had been proved to be a liar as to
his knowledge of the article. But Findley has been
expelled by a union of his political enemies, without trial
and without a stain on his character.
The Tocsin article is a protest against the interference
with the liberty of the press in the illegal seizure of the
plant of the Irish People. It disclaims belief in the
charges against King Edward ; it disclaims the “hardly-
veiled seditious sentiments ” with which the Irish
People winds up, treating the pretender Charles Edward
as being “little less obnoxious than Albert Edward of
the oath.” It points out that arbitrary suppression is
quite as capable of being used to silence truth as to stifle
falsehood", to choke reform as to preserve order, to
strangle the voice of opinion, or subserve party or per-
sonal interests,” &c. It reminds its readers of the grand
lesson taught by John Milton in his “ Areopagitica.” To
what will this foolish action of the Victorian House com-
mit us? Are our Australian democracies so blind to the
future as to permit Governments to punish minorities for
such articles ?
The article in the Irish People is an enraged attack
made by some ardent R.C. on Cardinal Vaughan and the
Duke of Norfolk and other English Catholics for present-
ing a loyal address to a king who had made the “ blas-
phemous” declaration against the doctrine of the Mass.
King Edward’s past is dragged in so as to point more
effectually the (alleged) unfaithfulness of the English
Catholics. The adjectives are the strongest part of the
article ; but they are nothing to John Norton’s. But if
Findley be punishable, why not criminally prosecute
him ? Why should the Government not come out into
the open, instead of shooting from the trenches of the
House ? Because they have a bad ease. We are' all en-
titled to have our own opinion of the personal character
of any man, even a king—even Charles II or Caligula ;
and we are all entitled to express our opinion—subject to
the liability to prosecution or action. As for “ seditious
libel,” it is absurd—at all events, in Australia. There is
nothing in either the Tocsin or in the Irish People calcu-
lated to provoke sedition (literally, going apart from, or
resisting constituted authority) in Australia.
Peacock has succeeded in getting the blessing of the
stock exchanges and the shire councils. So did Knight
Patterson, and he fell ignominiously. Shire councils and
stock exchanges will not avail Peacock when our people
get their eyes peeled. —Vindex.

What Happened to Lee.
T ’E present N.S.W. Ministry has come back from
the General Election about as strong as—perhaps
a trifle stronger than—it was before. Its direct
following and the direct Opposition are almost
exactly equal, but the Labor Party, 24 strong, is
with the See Ministry by reason of its Old Age
Pension and Compulsory Arbitration policy, and
the bulk of the 19 alleged Independents are mostly
with it. The Labor Party holds the balance of
power, as it has done almost continuously for the
last 10 years—in fact, it and the Independents, if
they combined together, would make a larger
force than either the direct Ministerialists or the
direct opposition. If the Labor Party took any
interest in honest finance (which, apparently, it
hasn’t done so far) it could insist that the See
Ministry, or any other Ministry, should face the
position and do something strong and definite
towards making ends meet in the future, and pay-
ing off the £5,600,000 or so of old shortages, and
stopping the Great Dismal Steal of loan moneys
for revenue purpose. Unfortunately, honesty and
solvency weren’t on the programme of any one of
the three parties which went before the electors
on Wednesday last week. The people were invited
to choose mainly from among the Lee crowd with
the worst and most shameless financial record in
Australia, and the See crowd with the second-
worst, and the Labor crowd with no financial
policy at all, and no visible desire for any, and
with no financial record save that it had connived
at the worst proceedings of both the other parties
in turn. In a general way, Premier See speaks
the solid truth when he says that N.S.W. has
enthusiastically endorsed his policy. It has en-
dorsed his deficits so cordially that he will proba-
bly go and have larger ones with a view to secur-
ing the country’s further approval. The country,
certainly, hadn’t very much choice in the matter ;
still it is noteworthy that hardly one candidate —
it is doubtful if even one—was asked to pledge
himself to a policy of raising enough revenue to
meet the State’s outgoings. The See Govern-
ment granted Old Age Pensions, which made
it popular, and it didn’t demand the
money to cover the expense, which might have
made it unpopular—it just trusted to Provi-
dence. It gained a certain bogus glitter by
sending liberally-paid contingents to Africa,,
and it didn’t ask the country for money
to pay for them, which would have badly smudged
the glitter—it just used the funds entrusted to it
for safe-keeping by the savings bank and other
institutions, as if it was a junior clerk taking the
loan of a pound out of the petty cash to put on a
horse. A Government which acts like that gets
its policy cordially endorsed in N.S.W. as a
matter of course. Both the old parties in
N.S.W. are built on these lines, and N.S.W.
has endorsed them both at different times.
Meanwhile, among many bad things, there is the
satisfaction of knowing that things might have
been worse. The efforts of the Lee party to keep
the dead-and-gone fiscal issue alive .in State
politics, and to attract votes by rousing the old
Kiama Ghost of sectarian strife, failed dismally.
The Telegraph tried vainly to wallop both these
defunct spectres into life; and lied to little
purpose, but with an incredible horse-power of
mendacity, over many other matters. But even
when it invented an imaginary break-down of the
new Government steamer, in order to show that
the public money had been fooled away on a
useless vessel, and told amazing snake-
narratives about a pallid crew drifting help-
lessly on the rooks, nobody listened much. Still
the Telegraph had its uses. It probably
lost its party a seat here and there; it was so en-
thusiastic in its assurances that the See Govern-
ment' was irretrievably damned that, no doubt, a
good few Lee supporters remained peacefully and
contentedly in the public-house when, if they
hadn’t been led into a state of false security they
would have rushed out and voted with all their
might. Anyhow, N.S.W. has managed to elect
125 men, mostly, judging by their speeches,
hideously dull and ordinary men, and the Parlia-
mentary machine will presently get going again.

The Live King and the Dead One.
The motion of Senator de Largie, that the Mel-
bourne Argos be required to apologise for calling
Britain’s monarch a fool and a blackleg, is
described by the heavy and respectable press as
evincing a great lack of humor. The kind of
humor that the aldermanic soul understands is to
see a person in large check pants sit down on a
pie, and everybody that doesn’t laugh when
somebody sits on a pie is supposed to be
of a grave and wrinkled disposition. Senator DE
Lahgie’s motion, as it happened, wasn’t humor—
it was satire. It was a bitter satire, if the Jingo
press would only abate its sonorous bray for a
moment and consider itself in all its own tawdri-
ness. The Argus led the way in hunting down
Vic. Labor member Findley, not because the
paper of which he was nominal publisher
called a British King bad nam.s, but merely
because it mentioned, with strong disap-
proval, certain bad names that he had
been called by another paper. The Argus itself
calls a British King far worse names straight out.
The only difference is that the Argus, being by
instinct a person who lies low behind the hedge,
takes the precaution to attack a dead King who
can’t defend himself. In the Jingo creed a dead
King is fair game because he has no more titles to
give away. The Jingo push attacks every monarch
after he (or she) is dead some time. It does this
in order to show its freedom and independence of
spirit, but it crawls humbly to every monarch
while he (or she) is alive. It dedicated the Word
of God to James I. (the man it now calls a fool
and blackleg) in language that put him really a
trifle higher than God. It called the unspeakable
George IY. “the First Gentleman in Europe”
while he was alive, and the worst scoundrel in
Europe after he was dead. It took to
calling Queen Anne “Brandy-faced Nan”
mostly when she was safely in her tomb.
Papers of the Argus stamp will take to
dropping offensive and brutal epithets on Vic-
toria, when she has been dead some years
longer. The Argus and the Irish People both
attacked the British Crown and threw mud at the
sanctity of British monarchy—but the loyal paper
kicked a dead man, and thedisloyal one had the cour-
age to kick a living one. The paper that hasn’t the
pluck to say unpleasant things about kings when
they are alive shouldn’t be so valiant against dead
kings. And it shouldn’t grieve so much over the
lack of humor shown by people who see the
absurdity of its attitude, even though it can’t see
that absurdity itself.

More White Elephant.
The party which opposes the taking over by the
Commonwealth of S.A.’s White Elephant, the
Northern Territory, makes altogether too much
use of the White Elephant aspect of the case. Its
plea is that, as the Territory has never paid its
way, it must be useless—which is a silly idea rest-
ing on a very silly misconception of the evidence.
The Northern Territory is one of the richest parts
of a very barren continent, and it hasn’t paid its
way for exactly the same reasons that have made
many other valuable parts of Australia un-
profitable-isolation, remoteness from any great
centre of population, and the fact that attempts
were made to settle it on a large scale before there
were people to put on it. It differs from the
other White Elephants principally in one point:
Because Britain refused to hand it over entirely
to S.A., and that State was merely in charge
as Britain’s agent, therefore a separate account
had to be kept of the Territory’s revenue and ex-
penditure, and its separate deficit became visible.
The other White Elephants had no separate ac-
counts kept, and their deficits were merged in the
general accounts and didn’t appear. It is practi-
cally certain that all the Monaro district of N.S.
Wales has been in the White Elephant business
ever since this country started; also, Eastern
Gippsland and the Wimmera district of Victoria ;
also, a good half of Queensland ; and a great part
, of Westralia. They only want the same system
of keeping the accounts to show the same results
as the Territory has shown. Still, they will
doubtless pay some day, and so will the Territory
—only more so. It is the most promising of eight
or nine White Elephants—provided it gets into
the hands of some authority with money enough
to supply it with means of communication,
which S.A. can’t do. The fact that it
has dropped behind is no proof that it isn’t
a great deal more valuable than N.S.W. or Vic-
toria, In America, California is far richer than
Vermont, but Vermont happened to be settled
first by an • active race, just as N.S.W. and
Victoria were in Australia, and the more valu-
able territory lagged 200 years behind as a conse-
quence. Some half-century ago Britain dropped
the Transvaal as a White Elephant just as a great
many people want the Federal Government to
drop the Northern Territory, and now it is spend-
ing £200,000,000 on the job of stealing the aban-
doned animal back again.

Our Assumed Soldiery.
A letter from the adjacent front: —
Dear Bulletin.— Concerning military matters in
Sydney : How is it that, when the splendid infantry-
training ground stretching from Kensington to Botany
can be reached as easily as Victpria Barracks, we are
treated to so many exhibitions of prehistoric tactics on
the old rifle range? What better configuration of ground
for training in attack and defence, outposts, advanced
and rear guards, defence of convoys, scouting, and the
use of cover, can be found than that provided by the
scrubby sandhills surrounding the Bunnerong-road ?
Twenty square miles of varied uninhabited country, un-
surpassed for military purposes, is neglected in favor of a
few acres of flat grassy field. I witnessed the following
“ instruction ” (?)
A battalion of five companies—250 men—(thanks to
the genius who organised partially-paid troops on a peace
footing, like regulars) scarlet clad, is halted uncomfort-
ably on the rubbish-tip at the north end of the range.
The old target-hill is “ assumed ” to be held by “ a strong
force of all arms.” Their flanks are protected by the
fence of the Agricultural Ground, and by Park-road.
Their front is about 200yds. The strong force of all
arms must be very crowded. Note the area of the battle-
field. It is 1000yds. long, does not exceed 250yds. in
width, and is enclosed. The O.C. explains the “special
idea ” to his officers, who stand round him in nigligg atti-
tude upon the remains of sardine-tins. The 0.0. speaks :
“ The distance to the position is 800yds., but we must as-
sume it to be 1200. It is assumed that the artillery duel has
shaken the enemy’s artillery so that the attack may now be
pressed. The flanks must be ready to meet any attack of
cavalry ” (posted, presumably, in large numbers in the
Cricket Ground clock tower). “ Cover must be fully
utilised. I hope to see a good attack.” Then the
serious nature of our preparation for war impresses itself
on all beholders. Each section of the firing line dis-
patches its ground scouts, who pay great attention to the
movements in rear. Each company sends a section
(about 10 men) into the firing line. These advance about
30 paces and open fire. The adjutant, with much fine
sarcasm, informs the subalterns in the firing line that,
though only 800yds. from the enemy, they are really, by
assumption 1200yds. distant. They blush, and make
sottovoce comments. When they are 80yds. ahead, some-
one inquires anxiously where the supports are. It is
elaborately explained to the officer in charge of the
supports that by assumption he is 250yds. behind the
firing line. This explanation has taken time, and, when
it is over, the supports are by assumption a quarter of a
mile in rear of firing party. This has to be rectified by
doubling. The major is sent hurriedly to liven the sup-
ports up. Meanwhile, the right flank has met cavalry (a
lancer riding-school having stopped its progress). The
firing line compliments a recruit \yho has fallen
from his horse, extricates itself from the cavalry,
and passes on. Supports now begin to pour in.
The centre of the attack is in disorder through meeting
entanglements (the engineers are raising a “ crow’s nest.”)
A football scrimmage is the next obstacle, but, finally,
after having disarranged, and been disarranged by, the
stretcher drill of the Army Medical Corps, our flushed
and stubborn soldiery arrive within 300yds, of the position.
Here they halt and pour in destructive volleys while the
supports and reserves come up. The Adjutant wants to
know why bayonets have not been fixed, and is told by a
rash subalternthat they are really by assumption 700yds.
from the position. The Adjutant gets angry (assumption
has got on his nerves) and he replies that men must not
get false ideas of distance, and that bayonets are to be
fixed at once. This time the subaltern’s comments are
not sotto voce. Bayonets are fixed, and finally the
mounted officers (still mounted) gallantly lead their men
on to victory after an attack lasting fully ten minutes.
The troops are formed-up and the O.C. makes inaudible
comments. He ends with a yell that “ the great lesson of
the Boer war is ” —etc., etc.
The band comes, the regiment marches off with the
wrong impressions conveyed by its former defective
training more deeply rooted than before. The farce has
cost our bleeding country £6O, and the soldierly men of
our forces are very weary of it all.—Yours (by assump-
tion) truly, Viking.

The Career of an Outcast Territory.
Ernest Faveno writes about S.A.’s White Ele-
phant, which the Federal Government has wisely
decided to take over, though it isn’t getting on
very fast in the arrangement of details :
The acquisition and administration of the Territory by
the Commonwealth seems only natural and proper, but
the question comes pertinently in, will the N.T. ever re-
pay the price S.A. asks for it—the amount of the accumu-
lated debt ? When we look at the natural
resources of the province there appears to
be no adequate reason why the N.T. should not
pay its way, and, in time, liquidate its back debt. In
point of size it is the third-largest State in Australia.
In variety and availability, the country within its
borders is, from a pastoral, mineral "and agricul-
tural point of view, suitable for occupation.
It possesses several navigable rivers, one of them
navigable for over 60 miles for ocean-going steamers ;
it has on its coast-line one of Australia’s best
harbors and several other first-class ones. How comes it,
then, that it has become such a scorn and a reproach
amongst its fellows ? There are several answers to this
question. In the first place, an attempt was made to
officially force and create settlement; and settlement
cannot be forced —it rftust grow spontaneously. Secondly,
when the country was first stocked, cattle and sheep were
brought over from S.A. at great expense and put on un-
suitable country ; the well-grassed, healthy tableland was
passed over and the stock taken on to the sour, poorly-
grassed coast lands. Naturally this was the cause of
great loss, both in stock and money, and gave the coun-
try a bad name. Then again, because the N.T. was in
the tropics, the idea was jumped at that tropical agricul-
ture must necessarily thrive and flourish there, indepen-
dent of other needful conditions, so sugar and coffee and
other experimental plantations were commenced, and one
after another came to a disastrous end. Thousands upon
thousands of pounds were again hopelessly lost and the
country was beginning to earn for itself the reputation of
being a financial sink, chiefly owing to exaggerated and
over-sanguine estimates having been formed of its capa-
bilities. The goldfields suffered in the same way : rash
and foolish speculation took place in 'Adelaide, the dis-
coveries were wildly exaggerated and the fields over-
rushed, and in consequence the inevitable reaction set in
and they have now become the spoil of the Chinaman.
Then followed a pastoral rush to the tableland country,
but dry seasons and the depreciatioh of station property
that set in just then throughout Australia acted as a
deterrent to the bona-fide pioneers, and allowed the
villain of the N.T. tragedy to strut upon the stage and
play his fell part in the drama. The Territory
became the exploitation - ground of the speculative-
syndicate promoter. A modest principality could
he secured for a nominal rental. In consequence
huge areas were taken up on the map by persons
who had the vaguest notion of the country and its
characteristics ; schemes were formulated and the affairs
placed before the public in the form of prospectuses
showing the possession of enormous territory. Some of
them reached London, some of them never even reached
the printer, but one and all served to add their quota of
damnation to the already bad name of the ill-used N.T.
Some of the schemes were for the construction of the
transcontinental railway on the land-grant system, others
for growing tropical produce by means of imported
colored labor, and others, again, for breeding cattle on a
most extensive scale and inducing the inhabitants of the
Malay Archipelago to become meat-eaters. So much for
the past.
When the Commonwealth takes over the Territory
and its debt, it will be essential to enter into a policy of
reconstruction as regards many things. The man who
wishes to take up 20,000 miles of country, and float it on
the London market as a first-rate property for breeding
giraffes and boobies on, must he coldly discouraged. The
Chinese element must be eliminated, and the stocking of
country, the formation of fresh stations and the rehabi-
tation of abandoned ones, be in every way encouraged.
There is splendid sheep-country all over the table-land
south of the dividing range. Some of it is even now
within a reasonable distance of port, and most of it will
be when that most necessary work, the trans-
continental railway, is finished, for this railway is
the most important element in the redemption of the
N.T. from its moribund condition. This is a big order,
but unless it is accomplished the N.T. will never be a
paying asset, good as its resources undoubtedly are.

Duggan, The Meddler.
“ Fkaulein ” on Victorian Lands Department
methods:
In a recent par you write of Vic. Lands Minister Duggan
as “ a well-meaning meddler.” The casual reader takes
this to mean that he interferes with those estimable in-
competencies, the State Lands officials. This is gross
injustice to Duggan. You might be as incompetent as a
dead Babylonian without any interference from him.
What is really needed in this State is a strong, “ meddle-
some ” Minister, armed with a barrow-load of bags and a
cheque-book, so that he could give the estimable official
the sack and his arrears of pay in one act. Business with
the Department is generally conducted thusly :
Certain lands are advertised as available. You apply.
Five, perhaps 50, or 400 others also apply ; ss. each
application. You are the alleged lucky person at the
board which allots the land. You will be told that your
survey fees must be paid within 28 days. The Land Act
of ’9l says you may pay one-third cash, balance extending
over six years. The Department says, in a way there is
no mistaking, Cash, or some other applicant wiil get the
land.
Your pay your survey-fees amounting in this (western)
Division to about .-£7 per mile for line surveyed (country
mostly open heath). Then you wait for the surveyor to
come. After about six months, you think you have been
overlooked, and write to the Secretary for Lands, draw-
ing attention to the fact, &c. In a week or two you
receive the startling information that your communica-
tion has been received, and will be considered in due
course ; this comes from someone who has the honor to
be your most obedient servant. Then you wait again.
Recommendations have been made and fees paid in this
district as long as two years ago, and the payees are get-
ting blear-eyed looking for the gentleman with the
theodolite. Also, present writer has had a lease (renewal)
in course of preparation for 18 months, and doesn’t know
if it is finished yet.
In Nov., 1900, certain lands (about 20,000 acres, known
as Blue Blocks) were gazetted as available for closer set-
tlement, in 20 odd blocks. About 200 persons applied.
All district land offices ran out of forms on the
second day, and no plans were available. In January
of this year the land was recommended to 34 ap-
plicants. This would amount to about £4OO survey
fees, which had to be paid at once. Then it was an-
nounced that the squatter’s lease of the land had been
extended for six months. The applicants, a lot of them
strangers to the Department (they came from S.A., where
they evidently do things) consoled themselves with the
assurance that at the end of the time they would get
possession. But it is now announced that the lease has
been extended for another six months, and in all proba-
bility there will be another and yet another extension.
And the applicants, or their descendants, may get
possession, if they are good, about 2000 a.d. But Duggan
isn’t a meddlesome'man—at least up to now it looks like
the very last offence that could be justly charged against
him.
