The last five years have been marked by an
enormous increase in the cheap foreign nigger in
Queensland. For the first half of the decade he
was practically stationary; then a boom set in.
Here are some results :
TCI.
1891 .. 8990 18,358 27,348
1895 .. 8745 18,658 27,403
1900 .. 9330 25,100 34,430
Within the last five years, therefore, the black
and brown and yellow alien population of Queens-
land has increased 20 per cent., while the rest of
the population has increased only about 10 per
cent. Fully one-seventh of Queensland’s increase
of population between 1896 and 1900 arose from
the influx of colored aliens. And these
figures don’t anything like express the size
of the evil. They only deal with the colored
alien. The colored British subject and the
half-caste British subject are not included.
There might, in course of time, be a million
Chows, Japs, and Kanakas in Queensland, and
not an alien among the lot; it is a mere matter of
waiting long enough till the imported brown man
dies out, and his son or grandson, the locally-born
brown man, grows up. The above figures merely
show that, despite deaths and departures, the new
nigger has been increasing for the last five years
at the rate of 1400 a year. And during these five
years the Whindykit Government has been in-
tensely impressive about the “efforts” it was
making to discourage this same coffee-coloured
invader. Apparently he comes in faster when
the Shindykit Government makes efforts than
when it doesn’t.
The most far-reaching piece of legislation passed
in Maoriland for some years is the Workers’
Compensation for Accidents Act, which came
into operation June 7th. In several important
respects the Act differs from the English law.
For instance, it will make no new pile for the
lawyers, as every claim for compensation is
deemed an industrial dispute, and as such will be
tried and determined by the labor courts under
the Industrial Conciliation and Arbitration Act.
No compensation is payable in any accident
brought about by the culpable neglect of the
worker himself, but it must be paid in all other
cases. The award of compensation is a first charge
on the employer’s property, takingprecedence even
of mortgages, thereforeevery mortgage executed on
and after 7th June is liable to become a second
mortgage. Hence not only employers, but all
owners and mortgagees, will necessarily have to
insure against the new risk, and the M.L. Govt,
fills the breach with a new system of accident
insurance in conjunction with its Life Assurance
Department. Only in one case can the new system
of compensation be avoided. If the labor court
is satisfied that other provision (equal to that
offered by the Act) is made for the payment of
compensation, it may issue a certificate, having a
maximum currency of five years, exempting the
employer from the Act. The rates of compensa-
tion are: In case of incapacity, half-wages—the
amount payable not to exceed £2 per week or
£3OO in the aggregate; in case of death, 156 weeks’
wages (provided the total is not less than £2OO or
more than £400) to be paid to those dependent on
the deceased, or, if he leaves no dependents a sum
not exceeding £3O to coyer medical and funeral
expenses. This Act is another example of
the Maorilander’s practical way of looking at
things. When the average workman meets
a sudden death leaving helpless dependents
behind him, there is always a large pro-
bability that they will become a charge on the

Sydney was glad right down to the ground
to see its Brough back again last Saturday, and it
said so at the Royal with an emphasis there was
no mistaking. It was a tremendous and well-
deserved ovation that fell on the returned wan-
derers. All the same, Sydney would like to know
why the “ Second in Command ” is described as a
comedy. It isn’t a tragedy, nor is it exactly a
drama. It is rather a broken-hearted society jest,
or a crushed smile in four acts, or a humorous and
well-written sorrow. The “ Second in Command ”
is well-staged, well-played on the whole, and it is
a good piece of work; but it isn’t a comedy.
Brough, as the hero, is an elderly major, with no
money and no prospects of promotion, and con-
sistently rejected by his best girl. So he is
sorrowful enough. The best girl’s brother, who
is in the regiment, wrecks himself utterly at
Monte Carlo, and is sorrowful too. The best girl
herself has the news broken to her (the thing is
done so suddenly that it is rather smashed than
broken) that the lady (a M‘Laughlin of the
Hebrides) with whom she lives is really keeping
her out of charity, and that she must hustle for a
husband, and that makes her sorrowful enough.
So she recklessly engages herself to the Second in
Command, which gladdens him for the moment,
though it makes her sadder than ever. Then she
falls madly in love with the rich colonel and he
with her, and they are both all broken up at the
impossibility of things. About this time her
brother implores her to marry the colonel and
borrow the money off him to pay his debts, and
he hears of the engagement to the penniless major,
so there is more woe all round.
m • * *
Then the lady breaks off her engagement
with the major, and breaks his heart, and she
becomes engaged to the colonel. And the brother
repents, and tells the colonel she has only done
this for his (the brother’s) sake, and she really loves
the weary major with all her soul, and he doesn’t
want to be saved at the price of his sister’s happi-
ness. So the man she loves throws her over
gently, and goes weeping miserably among the
baggage, in order that she may go back to the
man she doesn’t love, and be happy with him in a
miserable sort of way. She doesn’t go back, and
profound and misdirected wretchedness settles
down on everybody. Everything is wrong ; even
two men who nearly get a drink and one who nearly
gets a cigar have these refreshments snatched from
them at the last moment. At the remote end of the
story things clear up sufficiently to allow of the girl
marrying the colonel, but the hero is left to his
sorrow and his debts, though a huge royal duke,
with a stern countenance like a public injury,
gives him the V.C. at the last moment. This sort
of thing is very good society tragedy, but it isn’t
comedy.
• * * •
The fact that Brough is an artist of great
calibre has struck Sydney often before, and his
rendering of Major Christopher Bingham lends it
additional weight. The kindly, wrinkled cynic
on whom the hoof of misfortune treads so consis-
tently is a loveable character as Brough does him,
and any person with a Soul Would go a long way
to make his acquaintance. Mrs. Brough is rather
less successful than usual; there are probably not
many actresses in Australia who would do the part
so well, yet Mrs. Brough herself has often done
similar parts very much better. The best of the
feminine characters is Miss Susie Vaughan.
Her elderly lady who came from the Hebrides
isn’t a work of art —she rises to the rank of a work
of nature. Little Miss Chatteris is little more than
an outline, but she leaves a pleasing impression so
far as it goes ; and Cecil Ward makes about as
much as could be expected of the colonel. The
colonel is a character in whom the public takes no
great interest. A new man, Ernest Vere, is left
for more considered judgment later on (he has a
slim figure and an elegant pair of legs, anyhow).
Leslie Victor leaves a favorable impression;
Orlando Daly doesn’t. As for the royal duke, he
isn’t so much a duke as a Shock. Such a very
large duke with such a very small part is incon-
gruous.
#* # #
The “Casino Girl,” now doing nothing in
particular and doing it, on the whole, very well at
Her Majesty’s (Sydney), is a cleverer production
than “Florodora,” with catchier music and a more
sparkling libretto. Consequently it is extremely
probable, in the peculiar state of public opinion,
that it will make less of a success and run for con-
siderably fewer nights. Unless Mr. O’Sullivan
can place a sum of money on the Estimates for the
purpose of putting public opinion on a more
rational basis it is difficult to say what may
happen. The “Casino Girl” embodies a story
that is even hazier than most operatic stories. It
deals with a group of American citizens that meet
accidentally in an Eastern city, and strike up
musical friendships with the inhabitants thereof,
who are mostly married to the Pasha. The
opera, from the point of view of the libretto, is
pleasingly reminiscent of the “ Geisha,” and in
humor it occasionally comes almost up to the
Gilbertian standard, besides which there is a fair
amount of pasteboard sphinx, and camel with
human-being legs, and dancing brown girl, and
small black boy. Lauri, as usual, holds up the
performance —this time as a German brewer
turned Pasha, who has a hundred wives and is
faithful to them all. Some men, he virtuously
remarks, have only one wife and cannot be true to
her, but he is true to a hundred without any
difficulty, and could be true to more if lie tried.
He does try by annexing some dozen or so
American chorus girls to be faithful to ; and
when, still later on, he offers love to Carrie Moore
(who tries to make herself plain by wearing a
drop-off red dress that looks like a frilled lamp-
shade) his attachment to the fair sex reaches the
height of heroism. Like most of its kind the
opera tapers off considerably towards the end
of the second act. Though Carrie Moore
is saved by Kenningham from becoming somewhat
less than a hundredth part of a Pasha-ess, it is left
rather in uncertainty whether the gorgeous
American chorus-girls who agreed to take shares
in that Egyptian potentate remain a syndicate, or
are separately married to the various young men
in the red caps with whom they perform an un-
usually large number of dances.
» * » *
Hugh Ward takes the part of the sad-eyed
tenor who endeavors to dispose of his unpaid
chorus company in lawful, albeit limited, matri-
mony to the German brewer Pasha; and Carrie
Moore, once she gets out of the lampshade dress,
makes a fairly favorable impression all round,
more especially when she takes off Irene Franklin,
and also becomes a doll to order. One badly
wants a microscope to find Carrie on the stage,
and when discovered one wants most to find her
as some one else, though she is not amiss as
merely herself. This is a dark saying, but its
concealed meaning is perfectly friendly. Grace
Palotta possesses a voice not worth much alluding
to, and a face and manner worth a great deal, and
her little moral ditty on the fate of the fast little
clock that Btruck up an acquaintanceship with the
bold bad lamp that smoked and went out at
nights, is one of the best items in the opera. Also,
Lauri is Lauri. That’s all the public wants Lauri
to be.
** * *
Hawtrey’s comedy season at the Palace
comes to an end next week, and his departure will
make Sydney a less desirable place to live in than
it has been of late. If O’Sullivan can see his way
to put a sum on the Estimates for the further
retention of Hawtrey it would be a good work ;
there are few evils that O’Sullivan can’t alleviate
more or less by putting a sum on the Estimates.
On Wednesday the “Ivy Leaf,” by Bernard
Espinasse, is to take the place of the present
curtain-raiser. The Bulletin hopes to speak
kindly, yet more or less truthfully, about it next
week.
** # *
The early part of the “Power and the
Glory,” at Sydney Cri., before the scenery has
had time to ameliorate matters, is considerably
spoiled by the too-persistent gag of the baker-boy,
the butcher-boy’s allusions to his noble blood being
only a shade less dreary. The final peace and
blessing descend with less accuracy than usual in
this drama, the said peace falling just as much on
some of the bad people as on the good though
imbecile characters. The villainess isn’t hurled
from the flies of the Burlington Theatre, though
her old pal has threatened to do for her in that
way. Consequently it is evident that the lady in
question, who has continuously tried to demolish
tne heroine for no great reason throughout the
play, is likely to continue doing so for the same
reason all the rest of her life. And as the heroine
is the usual fool heroine who always tells her per-
secutors what she is going to do to them, thus
putting them on their guard, instead of doing it
and saying nothing, it is probable that she will
have further trouble as soon as the ordinary person
she has married returns to sea. The hero leaves an
impression of somewhat greater intelligence, ex-
cept where he stops, before rescuing the heroine,
to remark that he is a “common British sailor.”
There is nothing very deep or apposite in this
observation, but the gallery cheers it all the same.
#* * *
The amiable but nobbled race-horse will
probably run for some time longer at Sydney
Lyceum, his tin prototype jerking placidly along
the back scene just above the noses of the specta-
tors, who are watching his career in the far dis-
tance. The conduct of the heroine who sees her
brother-in-law bowled out on the racecourse for
fraud, and then authorises him, as a medical man,
to attend her little boy, looks as if there were only
one doctor in the district, or perhaps she took a
medical man related to the family for economical
reasons—which latter supposition does not har-
monise with the extremely gaudy evening-dress
which she wears to visit the stables. Considering
this elaboration of the horsey parts of the drama,
the scene where the heroine is nearly drowned
dryly in an empty cistern lacks fidelity to nature.-
But the big boy who sells newspapers in a gym-
nastic fashion remains a cheerful item, while there
are good points about the small boy whose identity
is discovered by a piece of ribbon. He is a
pleasant small boy, and makes a favorable impres-
sion when slowly dying of poison in the midst of
a leafy stage forest.
m# * *
Sydney Empire has a new first part called
“ Fun on the Jetty ” ; and the Dartos are at the
Tivoli. Otherwise the two variety shows are so
much as usual that it isn’t worth while for this
paper to waste the valuable time in which it
might be giving good advice to the Common-
wealth on any extended observations.
*# * *
In the absence of foot-warmers and other
antidotes to a low, vulgar temperature, the patron-
age at Her Majesty’s (Melb.) suffered severely
last week, but the opera itself was always* strong
and hearty. One night was given to ‘ Ernam,
and a meagre attendance heard Larizza, Signora
Benedetto, Travaglini, Cattadon, and the chorus
sing Verdi’s old opera with fine effect, the all-
round effort at the end of the third act being
something sweet and nourishing as well for the
body as the soul. “ Ernam is distinctly
improved by an alteration in the King s make-up.
His baritone Majesty has seen the folly of
his original straw berry-blonde hair and beard,
and is now a comparatively dark monarch and a
reasonable spectacular success. Also, his voice
seems to take a higher tone of authority when he
suppresses a weak-minded rebellion in the pre-
sence of a large party of ladies, who troop down
to the catacombs to see the conspiracy squelched.
They perform “Ernani” for the very last time
to-night (Thursday). For to-morrow the bill is
“Pagliacci” and “ Cavalleria,” the two bril-
liant, bloodthirsty little operas that have
been running five nights a week since they
were first produced. Last Saturday the
heavy tenor, Larizza, took up the name part
in “ Pagliacci,” previously sung by Dani. To say
that Dani, the tenor who resembles Phmmer, is
far surpassed by Larizza is not to find fault with
the taller artist. Dani was a clever Pagliacci a
bright reminder of Giraud whom Melbourne saw
at the Princess’s long ago. Larizza puts himself
right into the character, and the virility of his
singing and acting is great. On Saturday he
seemed to communicate some of his surplus force
to the sprightly little Nedda (Signorina Vaccari),
and the opera looked larger than before in all
respects.

** * *
“ The Bush King,” a drama with a lot of
typical Australian crime in its composition, is
raging for one week at the Bijou. “ The Bush
King” was probably put together some time
after “ Robbery Under Arms ” had inspired
the author to do this deed. The name
of the noblest marauder is Captain Mid-
night, who differs from old friend Starlight,
insofar that Alfred Rolfe plays the part, whilst
Dampier is limping around disguised as Humpy
Ned, a polished son of Beer. George Buller,
likewise Misses Lily and Rose Dampier and John
Forde are in the cast, and the Earl of Hopetoun
is the personage to whom “ The Bush King ” was
dedicated by permission. Which seems so unfair
to Hopetoun that the author ought at least to
balance the indignity by dedicating his next work
to the Jook.
mm**
At the Opera House, a large dumb animal
continues to earn a weekly salary for Herr Wins-
chermann, owing to Winschermann having got
him on a string. The animal in question is said
to be “the Sacred Bear of India,” but, unless his
wrongs have made him sacred, the title seems highly
inappropriate. Marie Lloyd and Hurley came
back to the Opera House on Saturday. Marie
is repeating her tale of the forty donahs and
blokes who painted Folkestone red. Moreover,
she has something very bright and Marielike to
say concerning baccarat and domestic bliss. A
wearisome manipulator of handcuffs fell heavily
into the bill last Saturday and was regarded with
scorn, but the familiar turn of Celina Bobe, who
now xylophones the “Turkish Patrol,” brought
almost as much applause as ever.
m* * *
The Philharmonic Society give two per-
formances of “The Damnation of Faust ” at
Melbourne Town Hall this week, and the second
performance will happen on Saturday. The great
cantata by Berlioz got worthy treatment from the
Phil, forces last year. The present revivals are
expected to go a little better. If they are only as
good as the original sample it will be a pleasure to
hear Faust d—d.
** * *
The Italian Opera season in Melbourne
has been suffering terribly from the cold these
last three weeks, and that’s all about it. Rickards’
new Opera House is a sufficiently cosy winter
theatre—upstairs anyhow. How it will feel in
the summer remains to be proved. The old
Royal, with its three galleries, is an inviting place
of refuge from the open air these nights. But
Williamson’s Her Majesty’s! “ The coolest
theatre in Melbourne” at Xmas time, and a polar
region in June and July. Nothing less than the
body heat of 2500 people can make the place
endurable on a sharp night. Doors have been
lately fixed at the street - entrance to the d.c.
passage, but the improvement will not suffice.
One gets numbed with the cold. _ If the place had
gas instead of the electric-light it would probably
permit of an ordinary - eyed audience feeling
happy, and then they would proclaim the good-
ness of the performance. As it is, they associate
Italian opera with cold feet, neuralgia, and
wretchedness.

follows Dampier at Melb. Bijou, but he is not due
before Aug. 24.
** * *
At Melbourne Princess’s a comedy com-
pany of somewhat unconvincing abilities have got
the run of Musgrove’s stage for a fortnight.
Their first programme comprises the two-act trifle,
“Nurse” and the mawkish little curtain-raiser,
“A Bit of Old Chelsea,” which Pattie Browne
played at the same house a while ago. “A Bit,
&c.,” is the tale of an inexperienced sculptor, who
usually sleeps in a hay-loft apartment at the back
of his studio, but who, on this occasion, provides
shelter for a strong flower-girl at 2 a.m., and in-
•sists on giving her the use of the loft whilst he
roosts in the studio. The damsel, being a credit
to her sex, grows restive under the arrangement;
and although the sculptor, after a brief wrestle
with his artistic conscience, falls into a virtuous
slumber, she decides to return to the outside
world and get frozen as before, leaving her tem
{jorary benefactor at liberty to occupy his own
ittle bed again. She doesn’t quit the premises
precipitately. She soliloquises on the social
question as regards genteel young men and unpro-
tected flower-gals, and ere she passes out into the
stilly, dew-spangled night, she lays a few stale
blossoms beside the dreaming sculptor, as though
he were indeed no more to her. Then the curtain
falls. Probably the eccentric young female will
take care to catch his admiring eye next day, but
meanwhile she wanders at large.
• * * •
“Nurse,” written by Miss Clo. Graves,
is palpably a woman’s work all the time. A
man and a maiden who have never met are
“intended for one another” by a rich aunt, there-
fore they object to aunt’s intention until they do
meet, without knowing that each is the other’s
intended, whereupon they fall in love. The hero
has gentle tendencies to betting and liquor and
other dissipations that endear the male sex to
lady writers, and the heroine is a nurse who
nurses for nursing’s sake. Even an intelligent
Clo. Graves can gull herself into the belief that the
penny-novelette kind of nurse is a sweet reality
rather than a popular feminine fraud. There
are several characters beside the ministering angel
and the Reggie who makes casual outlays of £5OO
upon gee-gees (perhaps Clo Graves didn’t know
that a “monkey ” was £500), but none of them
have a ring of truth in their dialogue. All the
same, it is bright, unorthodox dialogue at times,
and if the company were equal to all requirements
the result would be other than what it is. Car-
den Wilson, as a writ-server, makes a success of
his little part ; Frank Hawthorne, as an alleged
valet, is not unlike the waiter whom Boucicault
created for “The Pickpocket” of yore ; Henry
Ludlow is amateurishly pleasing, and Miss May-
nard plays the name character better than a Miss
Jean Barns impersonates a frivolous grass-widow.
But this is very modified eulogy. On Saturday
the same company produces “ Jim Belmont,” and
a week later the Vic. Operatic Society takes the
floor.
** * *
“A Million of Money,” at Melb. Royal, is
one of Bland Holt’s horsiest productions, wherein
the back-cloth defines 'the Little Bourke-street
boundary of the theatre, and the Derby candi-
dates have to cover half a furlong of public
thoroughfare on their way to the winning post.
Also it deserves to be noted by Isaac Moses 'and
the racing world in general as the first drama to
introduce a starting machine. The. field on
Saturday lined up to a primitive starting-gate—
under the management of two racecourse clowns,
who stood on very tall stilts for the purpose of
raising the rope barrier. It was an utterly
ridiculous novelty, and it limited the Derby
field to five horses because there wasn’t room
for any more to line up, but as a sign
of the times the ‘ ‘ gate ” provoked meditative
comment. In 1893, when “ A Million of Money”
was staged at the Princess’s with Myra Kemble,
Walter Howe and Lilia Wilde in the cast, and
Baker was a shiny villain instead of the soul of
honor, the starting-gate had never been brought
to bear upon a classic horse-race. The drama
which holds the mirror up to Turf practices has
reflected a great innovation within the space of
eight years. Now The Bulletin begins to hope
that Bland Holt will one day produce a Mel-
bourne Cup scene where the Tote will boom in
the foreground, and the winner will pay a divi-
dend of £73 10s.
* * • *
It is Baker who inherits a Million of
Money nowadays, and Albert Norman is the re-
lentless majah who takes him down with the
assistance of Miss Harrie Ireland. The lady
shakes her glorious auburn locks at Baker and
lures him to the baccarat table and leaves him
dead-broke for the present. Her stage name is
Stella St. Clair, but even that suspicious circum-
stance doesn’t prevent Baker from thinking her
pure, it appears. He scatters his ten hundred
thousand pounds in three acts, and nearly
breaks the heart of his wife (Miss Ross),
also he gets wrecked; but owing to a sudden
rise in the price of some wild-cat shares
which he got from the majah, he is restored
to health and happiness. The temporary
ruination of millionaire Walter Baker by Norman
and Miss Ireland is effectively acted. Charles
Styan, as the cast-off husband who used to drink
champagne before he came down to beer and dis-
grace, is a forcible incident, and the Bland Holts
supply relief of a rather superior kind. Likewise
Brown is a cheerfully dishonest character, who
turns respectable for a time, but finds that it
doesn’t suit him. Brunton’s scenery ranges from
good to beautiful, and the hippodrome features of
the show are kicksome in the extreme.
Recent pulpit utterance in a N.S.W
North Coast town :
Oh Lord, wilt Thou direct the pen of those who control
the press of this fair continent. Wilt Thou show the way
of truth to the editor of the Sydney Daily Telegraph,
and teach him and his subordinates the error of their
ways. Inspire them with Thy grace, and put between
them and Mammon the sword that defendeth right,
truth and light. Teach them the way to assist in the
development of the purification of the political atmos
phere, and save their pen from directing thoughts that
are calculated to suit the policy of the Evil One. Lord,
be Thou with all newspaper men in the execution of their
responsible duties. Be Thou also with the Premier of the
State, Mr. John See, and direct his footsteps in the paths
of duty, arid help him to rise above the antagonistic
newspaper men that are seeking to destroy his political
life. Amen.
So there now !

The Austral Otis Engineering Company have
now delivered one of the water-ends in connection with
their contract with the Melbourne and Metropolitan
Board of Works. The second water-end will be delivered
within a week or two. These water-ends are a very
massive piece of work and reflect credit on the makers.
They are made of a special mixture of iron, which was
subjected to severe tests before being put into the work
and gave highly satisfactory results. Each water-end
weighs about 70 tons.*

All Barrier Mines remodelling their mills on up-
to-date lines include a number of Luhrig vanners. The
South and Junction companies each have about a dozen,
no slime treatment plant being now considered complete
without these excellent machines. In Queensland tin
concentration is the latest accomplishment, the Irvine-
bank Mill having introduced Luhrig Vanners with success-
ful results, the example having been followed by the well-
known Coolgarra Tin Mining Co., who are installing a
number of Luhrig Vanners in connection with their new
Tin Dressing Mill. Messrs. W. &J. Lempriere, Melbourne,
are the general agents for the Luhrig Appliances. *

very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, andallsortsof iron-work.*

still wanting, but apart from that the revenue returns
are as follows:

Since the Federal Government took over various depart-
ments of revenue and expenditure, the State revenue
accounts haven’t anything like their old value for pur-
poses of comparison. Moreover, when the States settled
down to the new order of things it seems that they didn’t
all start on the same system which (especially in the case
of Victoria) makes the returns still less valuable. It is a
most urgent matter that the Federal Government should
promptly appoint a Statistician for all Australia, and that
he should be allowed to issue monthly revenue returns,
both State and Federal, on a uniform basis—if putting the
accounts in comprehensible shape wouldn’t be an outrage
on State rights. Pending more complete information, it
is impossible to fully figure out the year’s results, but a
few facts are available.
• • • •
The N.S.W. Government reckons that, despite
war and plague and Federal celebrations, it has ended
the year with a total deficit of only £169,929. This is
saying nothing of a sum (probably about £140,000) due
by way of interest on the purchase-money of the resumed
area, which would probably run the shortage up to about
£300,000. And it is saying nothing of the half-year’s in-
terest on a large amount of public debt, which, though it
is interest for the half-year from Ist January to 30th
June, on which latter date the financial year ends, isn’t
charged till next year. Nor does it take any account of
the great mass of other unpaid accounts —it is through
leaving over the June accounts till July that the expen-
diture for that last month of the financial year is made to
look, on Reid’s Cash Basis dodge, like about £400,000,
when the expenditure for any other month averages
about t'850,000. Also, it takes no account of the huge
amount of ordinary expenditure charged to loans. Alto-
gether, an honest, business-like audit of the N.S.W.
finances would show a lamentable spectacle. The N.S.W.
loan expenditure for the year just ended is set down as
£2,879,727, which is a fine large amount.
* » * *
Queensland spent £1,212,019 of loan money
last year, which is even worse than N.S.W.’s record. And
it ends the year with a tremendous deficit of £528,188 —
more than £1 for every man, woman and child in the
State. Queensland has already got rid of £1,575,000 or
thereabouts of deficits by the gentle processor adding them
to the permanent debt. It is interesting to know what it
will do this time. Premier Philp, at latest advices, hadn’t
made up his mind whether to tackle the difficulty by
increased taxation or by economies. When it is a case of
trying to save £528,000 out of an expenditure of £4,600,000
it" wants both measures, and wants them very badly.
Next year Queensland will have about £40,000 more in-
terest to pay, and there is no evidence that next year may
not be even worse than this. Even with all the awful
falling off in revenue Queensland collected more per head
in the year just ended than either Victoria, S.A., or Tas-
mania, and about as much as N.S.W., so its revenue is
just as likely' to fall again as to rise. And as Queensland
charges an enormous amount of ordinary expenditure to
loans, its shortage, on an honest and fair basis, is really
something over £1,000,000. To put things anything like
square, Queensland wants rigid economy, an income-tax
graduated from 6d. to Is., or Is. 3d., in the £, a land-tax
graduated from Id. up to 2d. in the £ according to the
size of the estate, and a sane, courageous, and strictly
honest Government.
Victoria has hopes, even after paying its old
age pensions, that it will end the year with a surplus of
£120,000 or so, but no definite information is available
yet. The railway' receipts increased by no less than
£303,000, which is likely to be the salvation of A. Peacock.
South Australia will probably come out about square, but
everything is guess-work yet. Anyhow there may be a
deficit, and it is tolerably certain that there won’t be
much surplus.
Tasmania is only half-way through its financial
year, but it is obvious that the glory of its boom has
departed. The State and Commonwealth revemua of the
island put together are £36,000 less than the State revenue
for the first half of last year. With the revenue dropping
at the rate of £72,000 a year, and Deficit Bird at the
Treasury, the prospects are gloomy.

half-yearly balance-sheet, to 30th June, 1901. The results
compare with some previous doings as follows:

The profits for the half-year just completed are at the
rate of £3 19s. 3d. per cent, per annum on the share-
holders’ funds, as compared with £3 17s. 9d. per cent, at
December, 1900, and £3 17s. 3d. per cent, at June, 1900.
The gross profits, too, are larger than they have been for
a very long time. The Royal Bank still sticks closely to*
its wise policy of paying small dividends and building up
a solid reserve. It is the only bank which, after losing
most of its reserves in the suspension year, has more than
made good the loss. The Royal’s reserve fund, including
£3OOO added this half-year, is now £63,000, all separately
invested in State securities, whereas its highest figure
before the great collapse was £46,000. The practice of
keeping the reserve separately invested in State securi-
ties is also a new and eminently sensible precaution
adopted since ’93. During the past half-year deposits
have increased by £32,916, and now stand at £932,178.
The reserve of cash and liquid securities has increased by
£5248, and is now £366,718 —equal to over 37 per cent, of
all liabilities to the public. Altogether the report of the
Royal Bank of Queensland looks highly satisfactory, as
usual.
• • « •
The Royal Bank, by the way, proposes to call
up £1 per share, by two instalments of 10s. The present
paid-up capital is £385,187, in 70,034 shares paid up to £5
10s. each. The call will give the bank another £70,000
with which to spread its business in the North. It has
such a solid hold up there that it should be able to greatly
enlarge its connection if it had only adequate funds to do
it with. At the same time, Queensland, just at present,
is a place where a bank needs to spread its connection
very cautiously.

April, 1901 .. 320 6 per cent. = 857 6186
The balance-sheet just issued is the company’s fourth,
but as the first and second reached this office as mere
statements of assets and liabilities, with no profit and loss
account attached, it is impossible to say with
certainty how things went in these two years.
The Co-operative Wool and Produce Co. has £15,352 paid-
up capital, and its reserve of £6186, as shown above, is
very solid for so young a concern. The year just finished
is, despite an increase of business, by far the worst in
the co.’s record. The great drop in wool prices seriously
diminished the commission receipts for one thing, and
owing to the lateness of the season, it is explained, the
returns from much of the wool sold, both here and in
London, could not be included in this year’s balance-
sheet. So the co. hopes for better fortune next year.

Most of the sums written off are described as “ Personal

reckons that the weary job of writing off depreciation is
about finished, and as it would only require £B4l to pay
a 5 per cent, dividend there should be a prospect for the
shareholders some day.

The reserves consist of ordinary reserve fund £475,000;
reserve for unexpired risks, £282,548; and undivided
profit, £27,186. The co. also includes amongst its reserves
a sum of £163,869 set aside to meet outstanding
claims, but this can hardly be called a reserve,
being already appropriated, or mostly so. In the strictest
sense of the term, the £282,548 set aside to cover
unexpired risks isn’t a reserve either, but it is generally
reckoned as such in considering the position of fire,
marine, and accident companies. If it wasn’t then the
co. which holds no reserve against unexpired risks might
often look better on paper than the one which takes this
essential precaution. The Ocean is an exceedingly strong,
progressive and well-managed co. with immense re-
sources. Also, it has an old-fashioned and exasperating
way of drawing up the balance-sheet. For instance, the
report for 1898 sets forth that the total dividend for 1898
(paid partly in September, 1898, and partly in March,
1899) will be 15 per cent., while the balance-sheet shows
that the last half-year’s dividend for 1897 and the first
half-year’s dividend for 1898, lumped together, absorb
£16,148. There is actually nothing to show how much
the 15 per cent, dividend declared for 189 S (and paid
partly in 1899) amounted to. Nor is there anything to
show how much per cent, the £16,148 actually paid in
1898 (though part of it was for 1897) represents. And the
constant alteration in the amount of capital through the
issue of new shares, and the fact that the amounts paid
are shown “ less income tax” which is also a fluctuating
quantity, make matters even more obscure. These things
don’t reflect in any way on the solvency of the office, but
they reflect considerably on its book-keeping.
» * * •
S.A. Govt, has granted a shilling a ton reduc-
tion in the carriage of Broken Hill ores and products to
seaboard. Though it means £20,000 a year, it is only a
flyspeck per ton of ore, because the mines send away
little except concentrates, and it takes from five to eight
tons of ore to make a ton of concentrates. The Govt., in
making the concession, gave the Silverton Tram Co.
(whose directors are mostly mining men) a nasty knock
for refusing to reduce their rates ; but it is since an-
nounced that the co. has conceded something—amount
unstated.
» * * *
Miners in a lot of places seem to have some-
thing approaching a mania for the appointment of Govt,
mining inspectors. Their spokesman at Broken Hill the
other day asked for six for that place alone ! The mining
co.’s representative present jumped at the chance—it
would take so much responsibility off the managers’
shoulders. But Chief Inspector Slee, with 40 years’
experience behind him, knew better. “ Make the
manager his own inspector,” he said, in effect, “ by hold-
ing him and his co. firmly responsible whenever anything
goes wrong.” And that is the sum and substance of the
whole matter.
The Royal Commissioner on the B.H. South
mining accident had to leave B.H. without learning any-
thing more than the coroner’s inquest disclosed. The
mine’s lawyer promised, when the Miners’ Association
wrote that the inquiry' was a farce, that no workman
would suffer for telling the truth. But as there is some-
times a difference of opinion as to what is truth, the
workmen took no risks and unanimously stayed away.
• * * *
British B.H. has picked up the lode at 500 ft. ;
hitherto all workings have been above that level. The
development (value not yet ascertained) was badly
needed.
* * * *
The Lucknow mines, N.S.W.: Another little
newspaper attempt from Orange to boom them —said that
Newman, who managed the properties so successfully up
to a few years ago, will soon have full control of all the
amalgamated mines, and that when he gets them going
again confidence in the stability of N.S.W. mines will be
re-established in London. Great Scott! The explanation
given to the writer of the failure to float a N.S.W. mine
in London recently was that the expert had unwittingly
compared the formation with that of the Lucknow mines.
That damned the scheme at once. London was not
taking any Lucknow formations. True, tons of gold have
been got from Lucknow mines, and tons of gold have gone
into them in wages, managers’ heavy salaries, houses,
and machinery. Reasonable also to suppose that where
tons of gold have been got, a ton or two may remain ;
but the fact can’t be overlooked that during the last five
years more prospecting work has been done in the Luck-
now mines than during the previous 15. It is a big
mining gamble, with the chances against it, all the higher
levels having been gutted out; the formation is the
patchiest known in the mining world, and exploring in
the deep levels is an expensive business. Should Newman
strike a bonanza, he will be as greatly praised as if he
manufactured it—if he fails, a new manager will probably
receive the same newspaper gush.
* * * *
Blayney Copper-mine: Last half-year’s report
proves this to be a low-grade and cheaply-run concern.
It produces ore at a trifle over 6s. per ton, and is turning
it into copper under 16s. Realisation and all other costs
would bring the total up to 265. for mining and smelting.
This is done by one water-jacket and several reverbera-
tory-furnaces, and it is estimated that the cost
will be further reduced when the second blast-furnace
is at work. There is another important feature of which
little or no mention is made in the report. The ore is
so silicious that it puzzles most metallurgists how direct
smelting takes place without any addition of iron flux,
and it was on high advice that a trial was made with
ironstone from Oarcoar, but no better smelting
result was obtained. As it is, the Blaymey mine is smelt-
ing an ore containing more silica than any other smelting
proposition in Australia, if not in the whole world, and
producing probably the cleanest of slag which sometimes
runs over 50 per cent, silica. This operation certainly
upsets all smelting text-books and metallurgical theories
and has awakened such an interest elsewhere that corres-
pondence on the matter has been received from different
parts of the world asking how it has been arrived- at. No
technical explanation is given, but the palpable fact re-
mains that it is done, and, after allowing for the fairly
large percentage of limestone used for fluxing, it can only
be accounted for by some mechanical action in the
furnace itself which cannot be- obtained in laboratory
tests. Metallurgists who have not seen the ore or mine
declare that the iron must be more and the silica less than
is generally admitted, although the contents of each
metal have been proved by repeated analyses and are demon-
strated every day by the percentage of iron in the matte
and the wonderfully heavy percentage of silica in the slag.
* * « *
It is about time that an attempt was made to
see if the experience with Blayney cannot be repeated
elsewhere. Probably had it not been for the success at
Blayney with an unusually high silicious ore those two
blast-furnaces would not have been erected at the Lloyd’s
copper mine, Burraga. They were condemned without a
trial when a change of management took place, in spite of
the Blayney success, and in face of the fact that 100 tons
of Burraga ore were put through the Blayney furnace,
and the report that it went through if anything easier
than the Blayney ore.

* * * *
Of the Cobar mining district the Warden says :
“I consider the mining industry is only in its infancy,
and the district is undoubtedly the most important
mining centre in N.S.W., and it only wants good seasons
and capital to prove it the best mining district in the
Commonwealth.” It is a good gold and copper mining
district no doubt, but it is not quite as good as that. Of
the North Nymagee, he says : “ It is a mine which the
directors and legal manager seem to think can be worked
with from seven to ten men, a whim, and a green-hide
bucket.” Writer has heard a great deal from time to
time of the North Nymagee, but this is the first he has
ever heard of the green-hide bucket.

» * • •
Victorian mining generally is in a bad way and
next to nothing doing on the Stock Exchange ; several
recent bitter disappointments and no mining surprises.
South Glenfine last crushing 7dwt., and as there are no
divs. in this there is a bigger prospect of calls next year
than divs. Birthday, near Ballarat, is the latest big mine
to suddenly slump—from 78s. to 40s. Has paid over 70s.
in divs. Plenty stone in bottom level, but it is much
poorer and the run of gold has become so much shorter
that the suspicion is entertained that the next hundred
feet will see the end of it. A big improvement has taken
place in South Birthday, and some mining men, not
usually sanguine, think that the run of ore found at the
600 ft. will go to the surface ; if so, the shares will more
than double in value. Will probably call up 2s. to erect
a crushing plant. New Moon, Bendigo, going back.
South New Moon coming forward. Golden Pyke getting
some rich crushings from a patch.
* * * *
Sydney E. News : “ It is now an open secret
that during the W.A. boom many of the glowing reports
telegraphed to the eastern States were simply a tissue
of lies. Agencies appear to be again at work transmit-
ting roseate intelligence in regard to several of the minor
W.A. stocks, the object sometimes being to ‘ boost
shares and sometimes to put the public in good heart to
pay a succession of calls. Say a piece of telluride the
size of a pea is found, immediately this is magnified into
a telegraphic assay going 300 z., 400 z., or 70oz. to the ton,
but the size of the find is withheld for London’s exclusive
information. The present state of the libel-law in the
parent state renders it impracticable for conscientious
scribes to be explicit in placing the staid section of
mining operators on their guard in regard to individual
mines in the far West.” Not a word as to the responsi-
bility of newspapers themselves which have been played
with by biassed and corrupt telegraphic correspondents
ever since the W.A. boom started. Writer has referred
to it for years past, and the public have so long been
sickened with these mining lies that they awake na
further interest, and even the truth is received with the
gravest suspicion.
• • • •
Great Boulder : Shares, 18s., and they touched
16s. 6d. last week—the lowest figure they have seen for
nearly three years. Many reasons given for the fall—that
the extensive rich chute which has long enabled the
ounce-and-a-half average to be kept up is exhausting ;
that the bottom levels, so far as developed, are the
poorest in the mine, that the underlay of the main lode
has changed, and is now dipping towards the Golden
Horseshoe. Says a Sydney Sabbath weekly :—“ It is
asserted that the ore body in the Great Boulder mine is
only a pipe going down vertically, and the bores put in
various directions at different levels have to some extent
proved this.” Ye gods ! and this of a mine that turns
out as much gold as, if not more than, all the N.S.W. gold-
mines (dredges included) put together—that has pro-
duced upwards of 20 tons altogether, and is now turn-
ing it out at the rate of nearly five tons a year ; is
developed to 1200 ft. in depth, and has more ore
reserves than any two other mines on the belt. The fact is
that the Boulder for years has been regarded as an invest-
ment mine ; “ bears ” kept away from it because of the
great number of shares and the heavy commission
charged on transactions. If the other mines were in the
same category they would only have about half their
present market value. Inflated values are given by
“ bears,” and if it were not for these so-called unscrupu-
lous persons Associateds would be down to £1 a share.
As it is we have one mine, a steady dividend-payer for
years, well equipped and managed, with a little greater
valuation than another, in the same neighborhood, which
hasbeen worked at a loss for nearly two years, and has been
gutted out of its rich ore with a very remote prospect of
ever being worked at a profit. Few Boulders are over-
sold, while the oversold Associateds probably largely
outnumbered the actual shares—soo,ooo.
* • * *
Bayley’s Coolgardie : One more reconstruction

tributors, some of whom are doing remarkably well.
Proposed to call up Is. 6d., which, from so many shares,
will give a substantial amount.

BRAMDY
Talk about mean men and mean Govern-
ments. An old Victorian lady got the full pen-
sion of 10s. a week. It cost her Gs. a week for a
room, and, finding that she couldn’t live on. the
other 45., she took in a little washing—she was
too old to take in very much washing—to eke out
the balance. But Govt, came to hear of it, and it
was so shocked at her duplicity—it having been
led to understand that she had no means of her
own —that it stopped her pension altogether ! There
is genuine humanity and common-sense in the idea
of the See Government to build decent little homes,
which will be let to the N.S. W. old-age pensioners
at 3s. a week, so that they may at least have 7s.
clear to live upon.

* • * *
i>. R. Wise was on the solidest of ground
when he commiserated N.S.W. Public-school
teachers on the wretchedness of their pay. In
their case, as the Public Service Board’s regrading
report shows, there are no plums for even the top-
notchers in the department. Inspectors in charge
of important districts containing from 100 to 150
schools receive £4OO a year—the prize for, pre-
sumably, the best among thousands after 25 or 30
years service ! And still they say that education
is one of the most important functions of the
State.
* * * *
Apparently admission to Victoria’s Public
Service is just as much a matter of political in-
fluence now as it was in the bad old days. An
attempt was. made at reform some time ago, but
a large hole was left in the bottom of the new law,
and the new idea fell through. For instance,
there are 1100 new railway officials wanted, and
this fact being announced as required by law
there are 14,000 applicants. The reformed law says
that all applicants must undergo an examination,
but in the innocence of its parchment heart it
quite forgot to say that those who passed the
exam, most successfully should have the billets.
So the examinations were held at different
centres ; and at great expense to themselves and
the country the 14,000 candidates were put
through. Yet, there is hardly a billet for any one
of them. The 1100 permanent vacancies are to be
partly filled by the promotion of casual hands now
in the service, and the rest are being kept for re-
turned cohentingenters. The successful examinees
thought, when they passed all tests triumphantly,
that their appointment was certain. It was only
after it was over that they learned there had never
been any real show at all, and their preparation
was mostly a waste of time. Now a very large
number of Victorian families would gladly rotten-
egg any returned cohentingenter at sight.
* * * *
Concerning a N.S.W. civil service trouble :
The action, early this year, of N.S.W. Public Service
Board in gazetting still another set of regulations dealing
with the question of annual increments has been placed
before the Attorney-General. Mr. Wise has held back
until after the elections, and is now to decide the point,
which point, in the opinion of the service, is simple
enough. In ’9B various regulations were given to the
service providing, in certain cases, for yearly increases.
Under these regulations, a confidential certificate on the
work, manners and customs, as well as personal appear-
ance of each individual clerk is furnished by the depart-
mental heads to the Board. Last Oct. or Nov. these
certificates, on which the responsible officers spent much
anxious time and thought, were duly demanded by the
Board and furnished. Next week the service was circu-
larised to the effect that, pending new regulations, these
certificates would be set aside. Early in Jan. these new
regulations appeared and calmly annulled all the old
ones. Not only this, but dated them back six months.
Thus the increment duly earned under the old regulations
was lost to the service. The action of the Board in
making the new regulations retrospective was imme-
diatelv challenged. But there was another point. The
Legislature has already voted £14,000 for increases in
salaries from July 1, 1900. The decision of the Board to
date them six months ahead is held to be ultra vires.
The Attorney-General has given a tentative opinion, up-
holding these views The whole thing is to be threshed
out wlth the Board and the Bush Lawyers in the
service are quite satisfied their beloved Board will go
down

The most pathetic petition presented to
Maoriland Govt, during the Yorks’ visit was one
from sundry persons, against whom grog-prohibi-
tion-orders had been made, praying that the
whisky-ban against them might be suspended pro.
tern.
m * * »
During the Yorks’ visit to Wellington
(M.L.) it was arranged that they should go
through the Old People’s Home. At midday the
old people were all assembled, dressed in their
best, while the trustees hovered round in blissful
expectancy. Till 2 p.m. they waited, but no
Duke. A telephone-message to Govt. House got
the response that he couldn’t come. Half-an-
hour later another message came that he was
coming. The inmates were again assembled, and
frantic ’phone messages were sent to the trustees.
Another agonising wait till five, and then a
mournful message that, after all, the Duke
wouldn’t come.
* * * *
All things come to those who wait. In
Auckland (M.L.) Dr. Campbell, a fine old pioneer
colonist, just tottering on the verge of the grave,
was recently dragged into the mayoral chair
because it was thought the expected knighthood
would sit better on his grey hairs and spotless
reputation than on another candidate who had
sprung more recently into popular favor. The
old doctor marked the event by presenting Auck-
land, through the Duke, with what is to be known
as Cornwall Park—a very fine property in the
suburbs. Yet “the numbers are up”—and the
veteran’s name is not on the list.
* * * *
The general opinion in Maoriland is that
Seddon and Mrs. Juke ran the whole show, Mr.
Juke and Gov. Ranfurly taking up positions con-
siderably in the rear. Seddon has quite made up
his mind as to the relative importance of himself
and the Governor. After the reception of the
Juke in Auckland, Seddon hurried South in the
new and up-to-date Govt, steamer Tutanekei.
Same day His Ex. also went South —in the old
Govt, boat Hinemoa.
* * • *
The intense loyalists of Greymouth (Maori-
land) badly wanted to show their consuming
respect for the Duke of York by humbly asking
bis permission to do something. Not being able
to think of anything else, they got fixed up, at
considerable expense, an illuminated scroll on
which they expressed at length their unalterable
devotion, and submissively implored permission
to put up certain lamps in the streets of Grey-
mouth at Greymouth's expense. The lamp-docu-
ment was enclosed in a silver casket. And York
was graciously pleased to let Greymouth put up
the lamps at its own expense, in the usual way.
Can’t someone go one better, and present, in a
gold casket, a humble petition praying that the
Duke will permit his bowed-down and abject sub-
jects to continue going to bed at night as hereto-
fore, and to arise in the morning at the customary
hour, without thereby incurring his wrath and
displeasure; with a further assurance that, if
H.R.H. objects in the least to this proceeding,
his subjects will gladly remain in bed all the rest
of their lives, and not get up for breakfast, being
amply sustained by the sense of his royal ap-
proval, and victuals, under such circumstances,
being quite superfluous?

manently tenanted by himself and Mrs. Hall-
Jones at Searle’s Hotel. Minister Hall-Jones
must certainly be accounted a coarse and vulgar,
not to say treasonable person ; it is absolutely
certain that in such a case Seddon and “ Sir
Joseph ” Ward would have cheerfully slept in the
dog-kennel or the fowl-hoase.

There was great wrath amongst society
“officahs” at Brigadier Gen. Gordon’s orders to
S.A. “army” to parade in plain kharki on the
Yorks’ arrival-day in Adelaide. Many high-toned
swashbucklers protested that they had gone to
much expense in buying dazzling uniforms, all to
no purpose, and their indignation is being re-
echoed by the servant-girls of the pious city.

Another statement sent to a Zeehan (Tas.)
publican by Regiment Sergt.-Major Cracknel!, of
the Ist Tasmanian Bushmen and printed in Zeehan
Herald of June 3 :
On entering Petersburg a little resistance was offered,
The Tassy’s (who were the advance guard) swept through
the town, and were advancing’ on to a ridge on the oppo-
site side of the town when the Boors opened fire on us,
killing five horses, and also wounding Lieut. Sale, and
shooting dead Lieut. Walter and Trooper Frazer, of the
Westraiians. Soon afterwards the man that did the
damage was sighted, and the men fairly riddled him
with bullet s, then got to work on.him with the bayonets.

Oh, a stick, a club, a waddy to assail the swaggering
swaddy
As he struts and lolls and poses in his ambles through
the streets,
As with lordly condescension (’tis an undeserved atten-
tion)
He bestows a victor’s smile on every maiden that he
meets !
Oh, a pole, a beam, a girder to perform dark deeds of
murder,
To besprinkle bits of flesh and shreds of kharki through
the air,
To dissever from the body politic the human shoddy
That in everlasting kharki turns to greet us everywhere.
Kingly Death ! Eternal Reaper—of Jove’s thunderbolts
the keeper—
Lo, the kharki crowd is on us, in its brazen grip we
writhe ;
Shall our khariu-cursed community be mocked at with
IMPUNITY
While You in dreary idleness stand fooling with your
SCYTHE ?

The other night, in a Vic. township, when
gold medals were being handed to returned
warriors on the platform, at a local concert, one old
fellow marched up from the back seats to welcome
his son. The erstwhile Boer-slayer had been back
several days, but had left the job of calling on
the old man to the last. Patriotism—love for
your fatherland. Not for your father.
» * » *
A Rockhampton (Q.) patriot (4th Con-
tingent), mournfully“ People forget that the
men who are returning now are just as good as
those who returned earlier.” Poor patriot ! He
forgets that champagne doesn’t fizzle for ever ;
that beer very soon gets flat; and that patriotic
enthusiasm is mostly froth with a residue of bitter
dregs. And above-mentioned contingenter is
down to the dregs.

sick—some of them, the writer unkindiy suggests,
of “pom-pom” fever.

* » * *
Go to the war, young man ! A Crimean
veteran named Jennings, 85, who lived in a
humpy at Gundagai, N.S.W., was, a few days
ago, found in a dying state by a policeman, and
was arrested as a “no visible.” After several
hours the Govt. Medical Officer rolled up and said
Jennings ought to go to the hospital—but that
institution was full. Then the Bench ordered
him to be sent to the gaol hospital, whereupon it
was explained that none but prisoners serving
sentence could be treated there. The magistrates
then ordered the poor old man to the lock-up for
eight days, with an order for his admission to the
gaol hospital when accommodation was available.
Room was at last found there, but directly the
ancient was admitted he died. Go to the war,
young man !
“S.”: Be the disinclination of the re-
turned cohentingenter to get down to business,
and his ability to swell round and have a rich
time: I have met a few in my suburb, and the
most remarkable result of the prodigal’s return in
each instance is the sudden blossoming into jewel-
lery of the sisters and cousins and aunts of the
noble boy, likewise his best girl.
* * * *
The widow of one of the victims of the
Broken Hill South mining disaster gave about the
only bit of fresh evidence supplied to the Royal
Commission appointed to inquire into the accident.
Her husband, she said, went home to her,
scratched and bruised, a few days before he was
killed, with the remark, “You were nearly a
widow to-day.” He added that more than one of
them would be killed there soon. To her ques-
tion, “But, then, why will you work there?”
there was a world of meaning in the answer —
“ Oh, the other chaps are willing to.”

Apropos late cables re the deadliness of the
Plague at Hongkong, thus a Bulletin corres-
pondent :
The other day I was jostled in Queen’s Road (Hong-
kong’s main thoroughfare) by two coolies carrying one of
the Sanitary Department’s dead-boxes.” Slowed down
to let the bearers and their unsavourj' burden get some-
thing like an offing, but they deposited it on the side-
walk, and sitting one at each end, coolly rolled and lit
cigarettes. No wonder the plague is finding victims
among white business employes. All Hongkong rejoices
at a prolonged spell of wet weather, as the drains get
some chance of a partial cleaning, but whole oceans
would be needed to shift the magnificent accumulations
of filth.
» » * »
Adelaide has, or had until recently, an
analyst whose business it was, among other things,
to overhaul the city milk-supply, and shut down
on diseased or adulterated milk. The analyst re-
signed the other day in disgust. For two years
the Corporation, or the Board of Health (it doesn’t
appear to be quite certain which) has prohibited
the city oTcials from forwarding samples of milk
for analysis, and as a result the person with the
consumptive or cancerous cow, or the person who
puts chalk and water in his milk, has for all that
time been carefully sheltered from interference.
As to why the local fathers should sympathise so
earnestly with the man who leaves murder lurk-
ing in the early-morning jug for the encourage-
ment of the death-rate, nobody seems to have
any coherent theory. The natural dirtiness of
elected persons may have something to do with it.
* * ♦ •
Chief Justice Madden’s constant railing
against the Shiels Divorce Act has not been pro-
ductive of much effect, for Tas. Senator Dobson is
now drafting a Federal Divorce Bill, which will
bring all Australia into line with the almost-
identical acts of Victoria and N.S.W., and without
doubt the measure will be accepted by the
Commonwealth Legislature. Dobson’s Bill cer-
tainly does not go quite so far as that of N.S.W.,
for he has omitted, at the suggestion of Barton,
the section permitting of a decree within three
years in the absence of the respondent. Desertion,
criminality and cruelty still stand, however, and
the logic which impelled C.J. Madden to advocate
the maintenance of the bond between a black-
guard husband and a suffering wife finds no place
in the measure.
Elsie Hubbard, a
small girl of 11, has been
exciting the curiosity of
Melbourneites by her
passion for travel. She
does not care much where
she goes so long as she is
going, and she has run
away so often, merely to
gratify her inordinate ap-
petite for wandering, that
when she does not turn
up for meals at her home
in Fitzroy her parents
take it for granted she
has stowed away on a
steamer or a train, and
await reports from neigh-
boring States without
much concern. Elsie’s
last escapade was a crip
to Sydney in the hold of
a steamer. When dis-
covered the captain took
her in charge, and shipped
her back to Melbourne,
c.o.d. She has stowed
away to Adelaide and to
Hobart, and has taken
dead hrad excursions over
all the main lines in the
State, always turning up
at her suburban home
again in good order and
condition. She tells a
Herald reporter that she
is meditating a trip to
England in a cargo boat,
and Port Melbourne
crews have got orders to
keep a sharp look-out for
a small freckled girl in
short frocks in any bundles
or bales that may come
aboard. “I love the
sea,” she said, “but my
brother there hates water.
He hasn’t got spirit
enough to wash his face.”
Somewhere in Maori-
land (North Island) the
Town Council lately came
to high words on the ques-
tion of cutting down a lot
of trees in the local parks,
for morality’s sake. The
purist who moved the
resolution had reason to
fear that amorous couples
used the thick foliage as
a cloak for deeds which
shun the light. Strange
rustling among the
branches had grieved his
spirit, and he had pro-
bably been struck by the
significant paleness of the
moon on these occasions.
Whereupon another civic
father, renowned for his
malapropisms, said
“ Mr. Mayor, I object to
Councillor Blank making
insertions against the cha-
racter of our young peo-
ple, which I’m sure he
cannot constantiate.” All
the same the unconstan-
tiated insertion threatens
to result in a considerable
chopping down of timber.
Foliage, no doubt, has a lot to answer for.
* * * *
Despite the lamentable smash-upat Sydney
station some years ago, when a number of people
were slowly steamed to death because they could
not escape through the carriage- windows, the
railway authorities still retain the window-
bars. There seems to be no sensible reason
why, on these long suburban saloon cars,
the windows should be barred. There is no
danger of intending suicides throwing them-
selves through a narrow window while they can
quite easily go out of the doors at each end. In a
certain class of accident, especially steam acci-
dents, these barred carriages become certified
death-traps, because people cannot even get
their heads through the windows. As the
actual cause of death in each case of the Redfern
accident was the inhalation of steam, if the vic-
tims could have got their heads out of the windows
their chances of escape would have been good.
The ordinary compartment carriages have no
window-bars, and passengers can take what risks
they like.
* * * +
Accidents caused by Sydney’s George-st.
electric trams now pass without comment, but
they have not decreased. Surely (writes “ Boko ”
to The Bulletin) the Commissioners can do
something. Here is a suggestion. The cars, as
built at present, have a bogey in the centre and
overhang greatly at each end, leaving a space
some 2ft. in height between the street and the
floor of the car. That is the death-trap, for a
man knocked down by the car gets easily under-
neath, and then the bogey must smash him.
Surely upon the front rail of the leading car could
be hung a light, easily-detachable sheet of steel,
cow-catcher shape, sloping from the rail to a pro-
jection of 3ft. at 6in. from the ground. Such a
contrivance would cost little, oidy one being neces-
sary for each tram, as it could be changed from
one car to the other as necessary. The steam
motors have a clumsy cow-catcher which, though
it has to be lowered when needed, has saved many
a life.

That Auckland servant - girl continues
to make additions to her penny-dreadful tale
about the three alleged conspirators who burnt
the Grand Hotel, together with a numoer of its
inmates. Her original narrative re the men who
were looking darkly and mysteriously in at the
window some days before the fire, and the men
who broke into the house at nights and wandered
around the premises carrying kerosene or dyna-
mite or something, is now decorated with this
further melodramatic frill:
Jessie Minns, the pahtrymaid, on being cross-examined,
repeated her former extraordinary statements about
three men burning the hotel. Under pressure the witness
further deposed that on the night of the fire she went
upstairs and found the men smearing some white stuff
over the walls and carpets on both floors. They forced
her to carry messages and a parcel from one floor to
another, and told her that a gentleman was paying them
well to burn the hotel, as revenge on Johnston, the
licensee. Since giving evidence On Saturday, the witness
said that she had been followed b.y these men, with three
confederates with them, on Tuesday night.
Little Jessie explains that, though these extra-
ordinary proceedings and breakings-in extended
over some days, she was too badly frightened to
tell anybody about them, though she was plainly
in a fair way to be burnt in her bed. This makes
her valour in fully explaining tilings now, when
the three men she was originally afraid of are re-
inforced by three others, all the mere remarkable.
The police think that Jessie is suffering from
“ hallucinations,” but it looks rather as if she had
a bad attack of London Journal, and had been
readingabout how Harold Plantagenet Fitzherbert
set fire to the moated grange with a view to com-
busting the beautiful heiress, Muriel Courtenay
Cavendish, whose golden hair hung down to her
knees, and securing her money.

When suddenly was blotted out the light mysteriously,
And a voice of simple sweetness in a cadence of caress,

“Shellback” to The Bulletin:—
The result of the Federal inquiry by Vic. Marine Board
was almost a matter of course. What good, in fact,
has resulted from any of the inquiries held during the
last few years ? Plenty of evidence is always on hand to
prove that the lost vessel was well found and manned and
in every way perfectly equipped for a fight with the ele-
ments. Vessels having large f reeboards making the same
passage, through the same gale that foundered the
Federal, had a terrible time, and even one of the mighty
Liverpool White Star steamers was seriously delayed ; so
what chance had the small, deep-laden, comparatively-
under-powered collier ? Fact is—the present freeboard
limit is still on the dangerous side, as anyone will allow
who has seen the little weak-engined deep-loaded iron-ore
steamers wallowing their way across the Bay of Biscay
for Swansea, with their decks full at every roll. Tramp
steamers, also, are continually leaving Australian for over-
sea ports, loaded down to their marks, with decks lum-
bered up, fore and aft, with cattle and horse stalls and
piles of fodder, and in a condition generally that
must render them almost totally unmanageable in
emergency. Say such a vessel gets into collision,
it’s 10 to 1 that the boats and gear are unwork-
able, by reason of the stall-fittings built around
the davits and chocks. Yet who is there to see that
this gear is in proper working order before leaving port.
No cattle-stall or other obstruction should be permitted
within at least 10ft. of any boat or its hoisting gear,
under any circumstance. It is a shameful thing that the
sailor-man’s chances of escape should be thus stolen from
him because some long-shore shark sees a chance of
making a few extra hundreds of freight on deck-space.
It is often—or seldom—a failing of bright,
clever girls to at less than their
face —or form—vaftte. This should be guarded
against. The best remedy is the P.D. Corset,
which, whilst giving a graceful mobility to the
figure, stiffens the character with a consciousness
of self-value. A pretty girl beautified by a P.D.
is too wellaware of her great charm to throw herself
away on any Adolphus who may be first to offer
himself.*
