A WOMANS LETTER.
Sydney, J uly 8, 1901.
My Dear Artni, —
State elections, a big ball, and the productions
of “Casino Girl” and “Second in Command”
are pretty good provender for one and the same
week. The crowd opposite the Herald office
looked the same old, wet, silent, smoking, straw-
hatted crowd that we’ve viewed so often from the
windows of the Empire Hotel. One woman-
only one, I think—stayed in the liquid mud and
crowd’s centre. A pair of upstanding blue bows
betrayed her. The crowd opposite the Telegraph
had lungs, and smelt of onions. In the dining-
room of the Empire, where the Progressives had
fed, was the same old after-dinner fervor, hand-
clasping, speeching, and reading of congratulatory
telegrams.
Squaw-like, a few women came in after the feast,
and there was a demand for menus and auto-
graphs. One lady, whose husband had been re-
turned, asked for a menu to be autographed by
the “distinguished” persons present, as she
wanted it “for her boys.” She was confessedly
not far in the twenties, but the excited chairman
asked, “ Are your sons also in politics? ” and got
the reply, “They’re not even in knickerbockers ! ”
Some of the candidates proved that “the readi-
ness is all.” At Ingleburn, where solicitor Black-
more was addressing a gathering of hen-farmers,
during his hopeless assault on Camden and Poli-
tician Kidd, the speaker was roasting Ministers
and holding them up to scorn for their photo-
graphic freak at the Town Hall, where, to catch
the flash-light flare, they turned their backs on all
present—a forecast of future action. “ Mr. Kidd
wasn’t there !” shouted a young man. “ How do
you know?” asked Blackmore, “are you hiskeeper?
The Telegraph says he was present. Who are
you?” “Kidd’s son,” shouted the audience.
“ It’s a wise Kidd that knows his own father ” re-
torted Blackmore. Collapse of the Kidd.
Here’s another proof. A Sydney sider, at-
tempting the capture of a Western stronghold,
where Beer and the Bishop are all-powerful, was
orating. “ I hear,” he said, “ the local publicans
intend boycotting me unless I swallow their plat-
form whole. I warn the intended deputation
that if they come along with that threat I’ll show
the deputation the door.” “What!” yelled the
local boss-publican, whose height was just four-
feet-six, shaking his fist; “Would you show me
the blanky door?” “No, my little man,”
answered the orator, “ I’d showyou the keyhole.”
There has been great indignation in Dunedin
over the Duke’s visit to the Hawkesbury (_N.S. W.)
on Sunday. A local church-paper has five solid
columns of severe reprehension ; and the “ bun ”
for pious twaddle is certainly taken when, under
the sub-heading of “A Blow at the People’s
Purity,” it is apparently argued that the number
of M.L. illegitimate births will increase because
the Dook broke the Sabbath in Australia!
Ten maidens debuted at University Sports Club
Ball. They had the usual snowy, girl-from-up-
there appearance, with their white silks and
chiffons and bridal-flowered hands and hair. Miss
Jane Stephen (now let us rejoice that the sweet
name of Jane yet abides with us) was one of the
July fledglings. Miss Purves was another, a cata-
ract of narrow ribbon falling from the front of one
shoulder over the white frock. Miss MacCallum,
a daughter of Prof. Mungo, also came out of her
shell, and one of the jokes of the evening was the
mistaking of this young lady’s mother for ,a
just-come out chick by persons who aren’t
short-sighted, either. Miss Hinton, Miss Baker
and Miss Armstrong also “arrived.” A girl
with a big green poppy on either side of
her face, close to her eyes, carried off the daring
decoration capitally. Her hair was really black—
the kind that if an artist were painting he’d use
green for its shadows. Miss Littlebridge Harris,
who was there with her niece, Miss Blanche
Harris, wore a beautiful gown with a molten-
bronze look about it. Miss Hilda Caro was in
ro3y pink. Miss Mary Johnson left a memory of
white silk and chiffon and pink roses. The two
Misses Pitt were white and fluffy aud Dresden-
chinaish. A girl got a pretty effect out of her
arm-straps. They were black velvet bands over-
laid with little pink rose-buds. It was a lovely
ball, and all the damsels wished the U.S.U. would
do it again next evening.
Sydney Comedy Club plays “School” next
Friday night at Palace in aid of Free Kinder-
garten of N.S.W. The Eieut.-Gov., Mrs. Beau-
mont, and the Mayor and Mayoress will be pre-
sent. Mrs. F. A. Davenport and Mr. F. L.
Hodgkinson are the executive.
A correspondent from Orange writes : “ Which
of the four named artistes has the best natural
contralto voice—viz., Kate Rooney, Marian
Llewellin, Hetty Holroyd, and Eva Mylott ?
Also their relative positions?” First of all,
Orange flower, Hetty Holroyd is a soprano, has
always been a soprano, and unless a big tax is put
on high C’s to help pay the expenses of the Royal
Visit, she will stick to her present vocal arrange-
ments, and remain a soprano. Kate Rooney is by
right a mezzo-soprano, Miss Llewellin has pro-
bably the best contralto voice. As to their relative
positions, they stand thus—(struck out, by order).
Sydney University Amateur Dramatic Society
plays, “ She Stoops to Conquer,” next Thursday
night in Palace Theatre. This is the first time
the Society has taken such an ambitious play-
ground as the Palace. And as Tyrone Power has
been coaching the club it ought to conquer with-
out much stooping.
Broughs opened to a packed and fashionable
house. Sixteen persons in the vice-regal box, in-
cluding Miss Darley and some A.D.C.’s with high
collars. No Brough first-night would be at its
best without a host of Allens, and the host was
there in its very best clothes. Frocks are changed
three times in “ The Second in Command.” Mrs.
Brough comes out in a white serge tucker-skirt
and a pretty bolero and vest arrangement, but the
deep heliotrope street-dress in which she sees the
soldiers off suits her best. Miss Susie Vaughan
dresses her psirt perfectly. Her dinner-dress of
1 ilac brocade is a lesson in how to seem slender
1 lough stout. Little Miss Chatteris, who looks
bke a twin-sister of our late visitor, Mrs. Mac-
kenzie Fairfax, wears a very fetching frock of red
cloth with a bolero, white chiffon vest, and black
tie. Just the sort of get-up that will be copied by
a lot of fair, short girls.
“ Casino Girl ” was to me reminiscent of
“Belle of New York,” “LaPoupee,” “Trial by
Jury,” divers Xmas pantomimes, and some Bible
Pictures. It blazes with color, goes like an ex-
press automobile, and is lightly, pleasantly musi-
cal. The eight tall, handsome, stranded mem-
bers of Signor Hasbeni’s co. wear in the first
act dresses of yellow silk, with square collars of
white tucked satin and yellow hats, six-deckers,
with white quills run through the front under the
brim, and black buckles. There’s some very
narrow black velvet about the corsage of the
dresses. And they and the hats would do splendidly
for garden-party or race-meeting wear. The hats
reminded me in lightness of puff paste, the silken
layers were so flaky. I was sorry when that
yellow wave rolled off, for it was beautiful. Grace
Palotta’s first frock is just indescribable. I have
a memory of a short, white and heliotrope skirt
and a short cloak, worn Hungarian-hussar fashion,
of heliotrope velvet and gold gauze, a big hat with
a huge white feather straying away from the
wearer’s face, and another peeping over the brim
towards it. Of course, this is the kind of dress an
American girl wears when touring in Egypt.
Miss Palotta’s hats are noted and copied con-
siderably. One Melbourne house alone made 100
copies of a certain yellow hat she used to wear.
Later in the play she gets into a shorter, brightest
poppy-red frock with an oval, black, glittering
apron, and, though the stage is crowded with
color, this frock makes all the rest look drab. But
Grace is robbed of a tenth of her charm in having
no train to manipulate.
One of Miss Carrie Moore’s short frocks is
cerise silk flounced to the waist. At the square-
cut neck is a wide band of black velvet that makes
a background for a splendid diamond star. Later,
Carrie puts her star in her hair under the brim of
her hat. Another of her frocks is a white satin
with pastel blue chiffon, but she looks best in her
Oriental robes. In the Egyptian ballet there is
a strong flavour of flesh, the little zouave of black
and silver seems to be worn over—nodings else.
By the way, it does not appear now that Miss
Carrie is going to be married —just at present.
London Sketch recently printed portraits of
the Marquis of Headfort and Marchioness
Rosie, nee Boot?. The Marquis, who looks
a school-boy despite his military uniform,
has the eyes of a child and the mouth of
a noodle. His bride wears a large toque
and very undraped shoulders. Her portrait is in
profile. From below the forehead the face pro-
trudes, nose, mouth and chin seem bunched
together —a bit monkey-like. If the full face is
better, one wonders why we see Rosie in profile.
If it is no better you may conclude that the Head-
fort boy married her for her moral qualities, or
“charm,” anything but her looks. Perhaps the
portrait is a libel. Talking of portraits, I often
look over an M.L. weekly— The Triad —and am
always “fetched” by the beauty of the young
singers and budding musicians grown in M.L. and
pictured by the paper named. The South Island
of M.L. seems to produce pretty girls as lavishly
as autumn fields grow mushrooms.
Don’t some good managers know how to get the
worth of their money out of, say, a bunch of
violets ? A woman of my acquaintance has been
wearing a two-and-sixpenny bunch of silk violets
for the last five months ! Sometimes they are in
her toque, sometimes in her coat, last week they
were divided between her hair and her evening
dress, to-day she’s sick in bed with bronchitis and
the violets are in the low, flap collar of her
dressing-jacket. The doctor noted that they
matched her eyes so she is happy, even if there is
a eucalyptus kettle steaming at her.
In the matter of veiling : Soon, unless you wish
to be mistaken for a Boggabri girl, you must let
your mouth go uncovered. Veils to the nose are
due. Rendered necessary by the side-boards of
hair, and ruffled necks. This is bad news for most
mouths, for with women their mouths are the
crux. (Note how they come out in photographs).
Yet the mouth almighty will, you’ll see, short-veil
itself equally with the Cupid’s-bow.
I’ve just been looking for the fiftieth time at a
serge, so-called tailor-made, five-guinea frock that
I can’t wear and wondering if it’s worth an action-
at-law. The sleeves hang like concertinas, and
the lapels of the coat stand out and up till the
Eoints tickle my chin. Would an Australian jury
e moved to tears at sight of these defects as they
were the other month in Paris, when La Belle
Otero pleaded in person about the misfitting of a
dress ! The waist was a quarter-inch too wide,
and two pieces of lace were an inch out of place,
and out came the jury’s handkerchiefs. She got a
verdict, and as she left the court gave the dress-
maker a gaily malicious head-wag. The very
latest laugh that Paris is having is over a fur
cloak. A severe magistrate on the Bench has a
lady friend ; his wife has a gentleman friend. The
wife wished to possess a certain fur cloak—a
beauty worth 3000 francs, and her gentleman
friend would have gladly presented it to her if it
could be accounted for to the magistrate, whose
small salary would not allow of its purchase. So
he (and she) worked a little trick. He pretended
to have shares in a lottery and got the magistrate
to buy a ticket. That ticket, of course, won the
fur cloak. The severely-moral husband received
the prize, hid it from his wife —and gave it to his
lady friend!
It was recently announced in Perth (W. A.) that
the public school children were to attend a
biograph display at the expense of certain public
personages. On the day before the performance,
the children were called to attention in the school
to listen to an announcement, and naturally
imagined that they were going to hear something
in reference to the coming treat. Imagine their
disgust when the head teacher informed them
that it was intended to make a present to the
“dear little children of the Duke and Duchess,”
and that every scholar was to bring THREEPENCE
for the purpose on the following day.
Our time must be sterile in taste when such
press spawn as the “Visits of Elizabeth” is the
book of the day in London. Young, lovely,
innocent Elizabeth (not yet “ out ”) goes visiting
amongst her kindred, who are duchesses, mar-
chionesses, &c., and writes home to her “ Dearest
Mama ” accounts of these visits. A forty-odd-
year-old swan writes with a cygnet’s quill and the
artlessness is a bit overacted. Perhaps Mrs.
Glyn, the author of the book, is pulling the
middle-class British reader’s limb whilst affecting
to show pictures of aristocratic life in England
and France.
An English Marquis—we are given to under-
stand that he’s a very fine fellow, no word that
he’s drunk or “bounding” kisses Elizabeth a
few hours after he’s introduced. She is insulted and
angry, and he apologises so: “The dimple in her
left cheek misled me as to her character.” What
would this noble lord think of, say, Grace Palotta,
if he took dimples for indices of friskiness?
One doesn’t like to think of Grace’s danger.
Another coroneted person whispers to Elizabeth
to “drop her glove outside her door that night as
a sign that it would be all right.” And Miss Baby
Face writes “it was a funny thing to say, wasn’t
it, dearest Mama ? ” Nearly everyone is intoler-
ably vulgar, and looks and demeanor are on the
top rung of exaggeration. The maids are
“ furious ” over some trifle. Titled women “glare
like tigers ” at others not in their set and “ burst
with fury ” when a nouveau-riche calls on them.
A lady “ looks green-rhubarb-juice ” at a gentle-
man and he “ has the expression ‘ damn ’ all over
him.” Eliza meets her Marquis in a train and they
‘ ‘ glare at each other like two wild cats. He
wanted to slap me and I wished to scratch him.”
She talks “fleas ” with a French Count. “ Lady
Diana is pretty but has big ears, and all the
people have the look in their eyes as of persons
with tight boots.”
The poor, cheap words (when applied to humans)
“pig’ and “cat” appear often, and there’s con-
siderable talk about “lying in a hammock when
men are around if your frillies are all right.” It’s
hard to say which is the worse—Elizabeth’s
vulgarity or her grammar. But the craze for
books of letters is at its craziest and Mrs. Glyn’s
mark in “ literature ” is made.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The sudden sunshine of Saturday not only
attracted an immense crowd to the Flemington
Meeting, but called for the most flippant and
summery frocking. Those who, for want of some-
thing smarter, donned their Midwinter serges,
compromised by hats revelling in Midsummer
roses and Spring airiness. The two slim Clarke
girls were tricked forth in cream costumes and
bird-nest toques. Nothing so tricky has been
seen in the walks of Fashion since the trellis necks
of the blistering season. The Dowager herself
was more engaging than the girls in a dove-grey
voile, avowing the curves of her juvenile figure with
delicious candour. The Madden contingent
helped out rustling Winter or postscript Mourn-
ing gowns of black with the merriest
millinery—a garden of roses, jasmine and sweet-
pea. But the startling Paddy-green rig-out of
S’Rupert’s better-half struck the shrillest note in
the inordinately-gay gathering. Thes Hibernian
outfit was helped to sartorial greatness by the
trimmest revers and vest of white glace silk, and
a mushroom hat of dead black run over by sundry
lines of scarlet silk. Round the wearer’s neck
hung numerous chains and trinkets of an Egyptian
character. The black coats of many cavaliers
provided an admirable foil to the verdant toilette.
Besides this, all other frocks of would-be gaiety
faded into insignificance.
“ A stir was caused in the galleries of the House
of Reps, the other night when a round, very
familiar, corpulent figure and a round face on a
smaller scale to match, and a pair of round, mild
eyes embedded in the too, too solid flesh of the
face were seen in the place reserved for the Elect.
‘ Mr. Reid,’ whispered one to another. But,
just then, Georgie Porgie bowled into the Cham-
ber, and took his seat at the table. Then the
gallery occupants decided it was a twin brother
of the Dry-Dog Politician. And when after
chaffing the Ministry to a point of considerable
anger, the gentleman of the eye-glass rose and
approached the visiting rotundity, everyone was
more convinced than ever of the relationship.
They were wrong, the visitor was the Hon. Dr.
Montague, of Canada.
“ In town the likeness of the pair of talking-
machines (the Canadian one is gifted that way) led
to many queer mistakes. But Dr. Montague, who
is as serious as an owl, did not laugh himself and
thus help the mistaken one out of the predica-
ment, but glared, listened to the apology (!) for
thinking him Mr. Reid, and passed on with a
sodden look.
“ The old supernumeraries of Vic. Parliament
have gone over to the Federal service. There is a
new Usher of the Black Rod with a small voice
and lead-pencil legs which fail to fill his black-silk
stockings. An Usher without plump calves can’t
‘ ush ’ with any sort of dignity. The new young
man will have to take to cycling, or else buy pads.
There is also a new Sergeant-at-Arms, bearing a
new Mace that has cost £2OO. It is being jealously
guarded against the designs of Solomon’s lilies.
“There always is a queer mixture of the re-
spectable, the professional, and the small social
fry at 4th July celebrations. This year was no
exception to the rule. A flag from the sky-scrap-
ing top of the Equitable Building bade the Yank
and all his sympathisers to come and rejoice at
Massah Bray’s show, and the crowd came—pro-
miscuously. There were all sorts in the gather-
ing. The hand-shaking of Consul Bray (an ada-
mant bachelor) was hurriedly rushed through, the
visitors being determined on a foraging expedi-
tion, and making no dark secret of the matter.
They dashed out of the reception-room, where a
few flags, starred and striped, hung dismally
enough, to the tea and candy apartment. In one
devoted to tea and weak wine - cup, several
queer American confections decorated with
woollen girdles and corner tassels reared their
iced heads and set all the tea - drinkers
wondering what woollen comforters had to do
with celebration cakes. In another room only a
moving sea of silk hats could be seen through a
cloud of smoke as thick as the fog outside. One
heard of an American bar and a bona fide
‘ Bronson ’in charge of the taps. That accounts
for the majority of males at an afternoon recep-
tion. A band played ‘Airs of Columbia’ and
‘ Swanee River,’ to which all the ready reckoners
and Uncle Sam’s sons kept time with sticks and
goatee beards. Mrs. Charles Ryan as smart as
possible in black. Miss Muriel Curtain in blue,
and Miss V. Singleton (of Conservatorium lustre)
in purple and pink-tipped hat were amongst the
naicest. Prime-Minister Barton came late, had a
scramble and an iced American drink, and saved
his coat-tails from the fray.
“The hostess of Rupertswood at present has
the house on the Sunbury Hills filled with girls,
who have a roaring good time with the baronet’s
bright wife. Rumor hath it that she contemplates
a merry hop shortly, when a special train will be
turned on and Melbourne’s smartest make the
Rupertswood ball-room, where the life-sized por-
trait of S’William hangs in solemn state, ring
with revelry. Late reports advise S’Rupert’s
health to be better since his landing in London.
“ A mixed marriage that has excited much con-
tentious comment among the Jewish relatives of
the Rebecca in question and the Anglican family
of the young man, was quietly negotiated at the
Registrar’s office last week. When the Hebrew
maiden (marrying a Christian) can resist the
panoply of wedding-bells and confetti, a suburban
registrar is sought to tie the knot. True love has
a decided leaning to the registrar, although
Heaven knows he is a most unromantic creature
as a rule.
“The fashionable nuptials of Miss Winter
Irving and Mr. Staughton were fated to be post-
poned from this week. The bridegroom-elect fell
ill, and, just as that contretemps was causing
alarm, the sudden death of the bride-elect’s father
brought the preparations to a full stop.
“In a ‘Million of Money ’ Frances Ross looks
very young and pretty as a parson’s daughter,
who marries millionaire Baker on the quiet. In
the average parsonage of real life it’s waste of
time looking to Pa for finery—ask him for a new
hat and he’ll chestnut you re ‘ the adornment of a
meek and quiet spirit’—but in the Blandoltian
rectory the Apostle’s one ewe lamb prances round
in swagger flowered muslin, all chiffon frills down
the front, pale-pink and lilac alternately. Later,
as the wife of a rich husband, she sports a silver-
bordered, purple cloak with rich white tassels—a
picturesque garment of novel shape, rather like
an Arab’s bernouse. Under this is worn a girlish
short-waisted, short-sleeved dinner-frock of white
satin.
“In ‘The Million,’ Harrie Ireland (the sinner)
and Frances Ross (the saint) should exchange
roles. The latter’s honeyed sweetness and decora-
tive innocence would sit well on an ‘ adventuress,’
who needs to look lamblike, even though she feels
wolfish. Now, Harrie Ireland, all stage-swagger
and loud haberdashery, would scarcely wheedle a
wide-awake babe-in-arms into mistaking her for
an injured innocent. When she lounges into a
bachelor’s snuggery in tight fawn lace polonaise,
transparent sleeves, and hat-feather hanging into
her eyes, she gives herself away as completely a 8
if ‘ baggage ’ was branded on her brow. Not that
Harry Dunstable (Baker) sees through her. But,
then, they’re so unsuspecting in Stageland ! That
old parson, for instance, who goes to races and
illuminated fetes, gets His fortune publicly told by
a gipsy, and sanctions the addresses paid by a
welsher to his (parson’s) niece ! What would a
real-life congregation say to this erring shepherd ?
From Brisbane:—
“ Little Salvi, of the Grand Opera, can’t get his arm
round the plump Lorraine, so he pats her shoulder help-
lessly. Faust took a fall out of the Devil the other night
by rushing the trap-door and leaving for Hell alone.
‘Himself’ was left clinging for dear life to the sides of
the flooring, but high and dry and with a 113th psalm
expression.
“ ‘ Herod ’ was read at a recent meeting of our Literary
Circle by a society tea-merchant member. By varying
positions he was the entire cast of the tragedy. By the
filter, the Queen ; elbow on the piano, Herod ; behind the
spittoon, the rabble ; under the hat-rack, Salome ; and so
on. The head of our educational system made some
remarks: ‘He looked upon Othello and Herod as black
savages, and was always sorry that such very charming
women as Desdemona and Marianne were in their power.
He had seen Beerbohm Tree act Herod in London, and to
see a woman with such very, very white skin embraced
by an Asiatic savage was revolting to him.’
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

. JOHN DOWD.

gravel, neuralgia, general debility, and Bright’s
disease all arise from defective kidney or liver
action, and are cured by Warner’s Safe Cure.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The Peacock’s eyes are in its tail;
Tho’ many, they’re of small avail.
Yet quickly treason he descries,
Who always looks with Arous eyes.
J. P. Butler, S.A. Treasurer, is easily the
thinnest politician in the local Legislature, now
that shadowless Holder has departed. Butler,
like Playford, is a farmer with a taste for finance.
Seems that a man can publicly announce
his own death. Melb. Arc us, 17/6/’Ol, reports
the death of its late publisher—whose name,
nevertheless, appears in the “imprint” of the
same issue.
Elector at political meeting to B. It.
Wise: “ Mr. Wise, do you know why you are
“he a kitten ? ” Wise said he didn’t know.
Well,” continued the elector, “it’s because
you’ve so often been licked ! ”
Wood-chopping champion Bryan will wield
the baton instead of the axe in future, he having
been accepted for the Vic. “foorce.” He hails
from Healesville and Gippsland, where wood-
jamming is a popular sport.
If Providence felt the least interest in the
health of the Anglican Church in Victoria, poor
old Bishop Goe would have been moved by the
Holy Spii-it to throw up his billet long ago. There
never was such a long unbroken failure in Mel-
bourne’s Episcopal gaiters.
“ Gentlemen ; though defeated, I am more
than proud of my thousand odd votes. I will be
with you again at the first opportunity, and I ask
you to pray to your Saviour that I will be suc-
cessful.”—One of the Chosen returning thanks for
his defeat at a recent election.
It is freely printed in Maoriland that the
be-knighted democrats, Ward and MKenzie,

John Colton, the S.A. Premier who first
gave Kingston Ministerial office, is still alive,
though nearly 80 years of age. In that famous
Ministry formed m 1884, were Kingston, Baker,
Playford, and Coles, all of whom subsequently
occupied foremost position in S.A. politics.
Findley, the Expelled, is a printer
and learned his trade on Mortimer Franklyn’s
long-defunct World. His next frame-holder and
great “ pal ” of those days was a young man who
inherited the bulk of the fortune of one of Aus-
tralia’s richest squatters, «-nd now pays income-
tax on £20,000 yearly.
Findley’s name, as publisher of the Tocsin,
has been replaced by that of J. A. Andrews.
Andrews, like Eugene Aram, is equal to either
fortune —Gaol or Plaudits. It was J. A. Andrews
whom G. It. Dibbs prosecuted in Sydney on a
matter of imprints, and sent to prison—a mean
pretext for an unjust act.
Now this is something like a testimonial
for a man to take to Parliament! Laborite
Scobie, who succeeds N.S.W. ex-Speaker Abbott
as member for far-out Wentworth, has lived for
25 years in Menindie. There are 74 voters there ;
of these 72 voted, and all but one went for Scobie,
who is the village saddler.
For the second time this year the order of
St. Michael and St. George has proved fatal to an
Australian recipient. Defence Minister Dickson
died immediately after being made a K.C.M.G.,
and now a brother of Defence Minister Forrest
has succumbed to a C.M.G. In spite of these
warnings, however, Australian public
men are pushing themselves into the
circle of infection.
A CHIP OF THE OLD BLOCK.
ISAAG : “ They, my poy, how many apples are
eleven apples and thirteen apples?”
I KEY : “I don’t know.”
ISAAC : “ Veil, can you tell me how many shillings
are thirteen shillings and eleven shillings? ”
IKBY (quickly ): “ Twenty-four! ”
On the suggestion of Keating, the Laun-
ceston boy-politician, the knighthood title has
been dropped in the Senate, and members no
longer turn verbal somersaults in endeavoring to
say “ Senator Sir Josiah Symon.” Keating’s pro-
posal was hailed with such a chorus of “Hear,
hears”—Symon and Downer themselves being
among the applauders—that Sargood had not the
temerity to speak against the proposal, but cast
himself back in his seat with a deep breath, as if
to say, “ What are things coming to ? ”
A certain Mayor, who has just been re-
turned to the State Parliament, was once deputed
to apologise to Toby Barton for the then local
member’s absence when Barton visited the town
on Federal business. “’E’sbeen called away to
examine a corpse,” said the civic magnate. “ A
corpse,” exclaimed Barton —“what corpse?”
“ Oh ! ” said his worship, “ the Mudgee corpse of
infantry.”
Poor little York was very anxious to speak
to Maorilanders in Maori, and invoked the assist-
ance of a well-known M.H.R. to teach him a few
words. H.R.H. was advised to wind up his
speeches with the Maori “ Kia ora ” (pronounced
Kee-ora) —“Live for ever.” Somehow or other
the wires got tangled, and the Duke said “ Kai-
ora” (ky-ora), thereby inviting all present to eat
with him !
Died recently in the U.S., Louis Janin,
jun., a well-known American metallurgist and
pressman who for some years resided in this
country. Australia has known few men more
competent in their profession than the deceased,
who, however, was temperamentally doomed to
wreck his own career. He was formerly celebrated
as a sprint-runner and was a son of Louis Janin,
the editor of New York Mining and Engineer-
ing Journal.
W. J. Lyne is much disgusted that the
Duke did not lay the foundation of a Hall of
Fame, as suggested by him. Just think of the
tinpot gods that would have been set up by the
politician in response to newspaper-organised
bursts of mob-howl! Better first get some well-
established heroes, then build Parkes’s Dead-
house and Lyne’s Hall of Fame. No man should
have a statue until he has .been dead 50 years,
any more than any man should be hanged until
he has been convicted six months.
Editor Bulletin. —If the late Mr.
M‘Kean, as stated in a recent paragraph,
“ always maintained that the greatest forensic
effort made during his long experience of
the Vic. bar was Dawson’s summing-up for
petitioner in the Molesworth divorce-case,”
he was much in error. Dawson did not appear
for petitioner in the case named. My late father,
Archibald Michie, Q.C., appeared for the peti-
tioner Robert Molesworth, then a Judge of Vic.
Supreme Court; Mr. A. Dawson appeared for
Mrs. Molesworth. Petitioner was successful. I
have the brief somewhere. —A. D. Michie.
The Times (Wellington, M.L.) actually
printed this (in a leading article) during the Royal
visit:—
“An element of romance hangs round the story of the
handsome and winning Princess May, because of her
engagement to the late Duke of Clarence, and the subse-
quent transference of her affections to the Duke of York
on learning that the younger of the brothers had con-
ceived an attachment for her. Affairs of the heart,
whether the actors in them occupy lowly cots or lordly
palaces, have a perennial charm for the fair sex, and the
women of Maoriland will, therefore, be glad to place them-
selves in some sort of personal relation to the Royal pair
whose love-story has been illumined by that fierce light
from which exalted personages cannot escape.”
At the Commonwealth gathering in Syd-
ney, Colonel Wyndham, commanding the Im-
perial troops, collided with burly Dick Seddon,
Maoriland’s Premier. Their introduction started
the trouble. Wyndham put two fingers in Dick’s
large paw, and remarked, “ Haw ! I have heard
of you.” Seddon detests a two-fingered shake,
and the colonel got to thoroughly know this
when he had to go to Seddon for infor-
mation as to the future destination of his show.
Seddon gave it to him in plain blunt colonial— “ I
have a cable from Lord Roberts, and you and your
crowd can pack your swags, and get ready to go
to Maoriland at once.”

Brainy Wallace Nelson, editor of the
People’s Newspaper, Rockhampton, is off to edit
a Democratic journal at Kalgoorlie. Nelson had
loads of ambition for political honors but was most
unlucky. Twice he fell-in through informalities
in his nomination paper, and Capricornia just
failed to elect him as its Rep. in the Federal Par-
liament. Under Nelson the P.N. was, for a while,
the brightest Labor paper in Queensland.
Just as the jury, at the third and final
futile trial of Jane Smith, of Talune notoriety,
retired to consider their verdict, Robert Howard,
the Botany Bay hangman, appeared on the lawn
at Darlinghurst Court-house. “Looking for a
job?” inquired a reporter, who had described
many a gruesome scene enacted within the grey
sandstone walls of the gaol. “No ; 1 don’t expect
to handle her,” replied Robert—who appeared
quite resigned and even smiled magnanimously.

The Federal Parliament-house Committee
has appointed genial George Upward to the sole
management of the show in its club and pub.
aspects. Upward labors under the cruel appella-
tion of Usher of the Black Rod, which would
imply that his chief mission in life is to wave a
wand. But the brawny ex-oarsman clerks for
select committees when required, and has been
the usher of radical reform into the refreshment
department. The Black Rod is a mere ancient
British foolishness, whereas the Black Bottle is a
great political force.
Many “ t’othersiders ” will keep green
the memory of the late Alick Forrest by reason
of his exertions to obtain employment for them.
When the western goldfields broke out nearly
every one from the other States who could
manage it brought with him a letter of intro-
duction to the deceased. They all wanted work,
and Alick wasted many days in finding berths for
needy new-comers who had not even a vote to
give him in return for his kindness. Brer Alick
was, with all his failings, one of the most genial
and kind-hearted men who ever lived.
There are two solicitor-politicians in Syd-
ney who object each to the other bearing the
name of John Hughes, and one of them is
generally writing to the press to explain that
lie isn’t the other fellow. Each is at a loss
whether to change his home, apply for an in-
junction, or emigrate. Whenever one John
Hughes climbs to fresh fame, he finds the multi-
tude lifting its collective hat to the other J.H.,
who on his part is wearied explaining that the
bowing and scraping are before the wrong man.
And when John Hughes passes John Hughes in
the street, there is ice in the atmosphere.
“Kiwi,” from Maoriland : — Apropos the
be-baublement of Ward and M‘Kenzie. Seddon’s
Radical admirers are delighted that he once more
resisted the Royal blandishments (as to the
acceptance of a title). But, if I mistake not, a
rude shock is in store for them. Seddon is not
yet quite clear of the crowd whom he knows how
to humor. Soon, however, I expect to see him
farewell Maoriland to take up a high official
position abroad ; he will then accept a title Con-
sidered to be as much above a K.C.M.G.-sbip as
Mt. Cook is above Seddon’s Terrace. As for
Ward and M‘Kenzie, the “honor” will be the
former’s political death ; the latter, poor fellow,
is too dead politically to be hurt by anything.
Mayor Graham’s defeat by photo.-maker
Eden George is generally alluded to as the surprise
of the N.S.W. elections. Yet it was not quite
unexpected. A stronger man than George would
have won the seat easily, instead of skimming in
by a few votes —possibly influenced by his alleged
timely donation to the Balmain Strike Fund. Dr.
Graham won the seat first when he was a highly-
popular medical practitioner in Belmore Division,
which includes Wexford-street, Belmore Park and
some of the poorest portions of Sydney. His
professional advice was always at the disposal of
the poor around him, and he troubled them little
about the bills. But now he is a Knight, Mayor
of Sydney, Lecturer at the University, &e., &c.,
and has little time to spare for the rheumatism and
colic of Belmore Division. So the poor ungrate-
fully deserted him for Eden George. Added to
this the land’ords and property-holders vented
their spite on the City Mayor for raising the City
Rate m order to place the City Council in a
solvent position. The Assembly now has not a
titled person in it, and if Speaker M‘Court can
manage to escape the distinction it seems likely to
remain without one.

John Anderson, long meinher for E.
Melbourne in Vic. Assembly, who died the other
day, was a good-natured old hotel-keeper who
owed his Legislative position mainly to the
possession of a brogue on which you could hang
your hat. John’s speeches were marvels, and on
one occasion when he had questioned the accuracy
of some Argus report that journal retaliated by
printing one of Anderson’s oratorical efforts verba-
tim. The thing was hopelessly unintelligible.
John was not, as generally supposed, an Irish-
man. He was English-born, but had spent nearly
all his life among sons of the ould sod. It was
Jack Murray, of Warrnambool, who described
him as “an Englishman with a Scotch name and
an Irish accent.”
Mr. Edward Dumaresq, of Tasmania, who
lately celebrated his 100th birthday, is chiefly
remarkable as having sat longer on the magisterial
bench than anybody else this paper knows. He was
appointed a J.P. 12th July, 1828, and after 73
years at the game is still capable of fining the
local larrikin 255. for “farin’ me uniform.” That
is, if he could find the necessary larrikin ; for in
a land where a man may serenely grow older than
Adye Douglas, the air is too soporific for larrikins
Mr. Dumaresq saw the division of Tasmania into
police districts in 1827 ; and knows the sad history
of Vandemonia from Ato Z. His son, H. R.
Dumaresq, was appointed a J.P. 30 years ago
and being an inoffensive agricultural M.P., quite
unlikely to fire the Derwent, he also may live
100 years.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. —In the case of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
THE PRESENT STOCK-INSPECTOR IN THE WEST.
“ Inspecting his stock."
“F.A.R.” : Apropos the fact that at
Rotorua (M.L.) recently the Maoris presented the
Duke and Duchess of York with native curios,
&c., worth, say, £2OOO. In Queen-street, Auck-
land, t’other day, I met a splendid specimen of a
Maori. Asked him if he had been one of the
recipients of the medals given by the Duke to the
leading chiefs at Rotorua. On his replying in the
affirmative I asked him if it were a gold medal.
“No! No!!” he said, “Te Tuke not gif gold
medal ; Maori gif everyting; Te medaHe Duke
give me same as penny —no plutty goot 1 ”
“Philef”: Re “Ben Sun” (8.,
6/5/’01). Where does “Ben Sun” come
from ? I have spent two years —swag up
—on bush routes between Brisbane and
Bundaberg, Brisbane and Roma, and uf
and down Yeulba and Condamine Rivers,
and have camped with drovers and
travellers of every description; with
Kanaka-working eockieson coastal ranges,
sugar hands in mill and in cane, coastal
fishermen, cedar-getters high in the
divide, rail and road navvies ; and fail to
remember a single instance where the
talk at the fire—and elsewhere too —was
not of an incredibly detestable description.
Have no hesitation in saying that around
nine camp fires out of 10, two hours out
of three, the talk evinces a bestiality of
mind, a loathsomely dirty depravity, as
sickening as it is senseless. The cause?
A kind of humor peculiar to Australians,
born of ever-hopeless, ever-heroical stoic-
ism, and perhaps the inadequacy of the
Anglo Saxon language—the language of a
mild, moist clime—to express the drear
desperateness, the iron, silent pathos, of
the momad’s life.

A N.S.W. up-country municipal
council recently invited applications for
the combined positions of council clerk
and inspector of nuisances at the magni-
ficent salary of £5O per annum. There
were 20 applicants. It costs from 16s. to
18s. per week for board up there. Pre-
sumably the rest of the official’s keep is
intended to be embezzled out of the petty
cash.
“A.P.R.”: If Tattersall’s is to be sup-
pressed, why not also the N.9.W. system of land-
balloting ? Between legal, travelling and hotel
expenses the out-back land-applicant has often to
pay £5 for a 50 to 1 chance of getting £2OOO worth
of drought-stricken desert. Even after drawing
the lucky marble he is sometimes disqualified.
Writer knows of one man who attended nine
ballots last year, and spent £25 in travelling
expenses alone.
* * • *
Many intending settlers complain of the
clumsy regulations re land-balloting in N.S.W.
Every time a man applies for land he has to attend
the local Land Court for two days to have his bona-
fides inquired into, notwithstanding that such has
been approved of ten times before by different
Land Boards, thus incurring considerable expense.
What is desired is a certificate asserting the fitness
of its holder to select land.
♦ » » *
“Jagra”: Almost incredible is the
amount of gambling carried on in the country
districts of N.S.W. Writer knows of “schools”
where men think little of losing £2O or £3O at a
sitting, and where farmers have knocked down in
a few nights the price of a year’s crop, just sold.
I have known two Hawkesbury men to have £7O
on the toss-up of a coin.

muddy water. Now Rockhampton Harbor Board has
got a splendid Bates dredge, which will soon enable the
biggest boats to come right up, and 0. Queenslanders
would like to know what that line was built for.

A NICE DERANGEMENT OF EPITAPHS.
MRS. MALAPROP (fat party with nice daughter and smart young man attached): “ Friends,
allow me to interpret you to my daughter's financier.”
“Hermes”: Be Maori etiquette. When
writer went surveying in the King Country, Pig
Island, some years ago, the first Maoris we met
came down and examined us suspiciously. They
then requested us to keep close in our camp till the
day after to-morrow, when they would receive us.
We kept close accordingly, wondering whether it
meant a feast or a massacre. When the day came
every Maori for 20 miles around was on the spot.
A hoary chief stood up on his short but redundant
legs, and made a long speech, and insisted on
rubbing noses with “ Wiremu ” (that was our
boss), and said that. when we all went to Te
Reinga (Hades) we would go hand-in-haud. Then
he waved his hand, and the brawny-haunched
tribesmen staggered forward loaded with flour,
potatoes in sacks, bags of sugar, and, in fact,
every scrap of food in the district, and made us a
present of it. It was enough to feed the whole
party for 12 months, and I wondered how it was
that Wiremu had the reputation of not paying his
debts; for, at this rate, his profits must
have been enormous. But Wiremu knew
his Maoris. He accepted the gifts with
great dignity, and made a long speech to the effect
that his father and his father’s father had told him
of the generosity of the Maori, and now, lo ! he
beheld it with his own eyes and knew it for him-
self. He squeezed out a tear and was too affected
to speak, and each Maori wept copiously. Then
Wiremu waved his hand, and said to us (in
Maori): “Take these things that my brothers, the
Maoris, have given me within your tent, lest the
rain come and destroy them, and drown my heart
in grief.” We took them. Wiremu went on
spouting, and as we came Out again to see the rest
of the show he hissed at us in English : “Take all
those blanky things out through the back of the
tent into mine (which was next door), and don’t
let them see you doing it. It wrings me innards
to give ’em' back, but it’s got to be done.”
So we did it. Then I came out and told
Wiremu that it was finished, and Wiremu
waved his flipper majestically, and burst forth
(in Maori) : “Hear ye, men of the tribe of
Hapuka. I, your brother Wiremu, am not a
mean man but generous, even as yourselves ; and
because I love you, and that there is amity
between us, I make you a gift.” (To us): “ Bring
forth the gift from my tent, and give it to my
brothers, the Hapukas.” We did. It was the
same heap of provender. All we got was the
back-breaking job of carrying it in and out again.
Later on, the Maoris presented Wiremu with a
fowl for his tea—and it was a very small fowl,
too. This was all Etiquette. In six weeks’ time
the press of Maoriland will probably rise as one
rag and say that Seddon is to blame for not
having carefully tutored Dook George who, when
the Maoris laid all their treasures, some of them
of priceless sentimental value, before him, incon-
tinently grabbed the lot. He should have picked
out one or two very small ones, and then have
handed the great bulk of them back with the in-
timation that a father may not rob his children,
and the Royal minions should have scattered gifts
—good substantial gifts—and shaken hands with
every chief personally. As it is, the Maoris will
probably be soon heard advocating a South Sea
republic, or something like that. They haven’t
started yet, simply because they can’t quite credit
the impossible circumstance that the Duke has
really gone off with the whole heap of wealth.
They feel as a white man might if you called on
him and he told you to take a chair, and you forth-
with gathered up the most comfortable chair and
went away home with it.
* * * *
“ P.C.” writes: Vic. Govt, has spent
£196,000 on the work of draining the great Koo-
wee-rup Swamp. Its assurances that the swamp
was properly drained induced many to select land
and settle there with their families. The Govt.,
be it noted, sold these people land—not sludge.
Since then they have struggled through flood after
flood, and more than once have seen their homes sub-
merged in an inland sea, and their crops and stock
swept away. Now Vic. Govt.—what humorists!—
has the cheek to present the Koo-wee-rup settlers
with a boat and life-saving apparatus, so that they
can escape from the allegedly perfectly-drained
settlement in time of emergency —or submergency
—and grappling-irons with which to fish for each
other’s dead bodies when they don’t happen to
get away fast enough. It is probably the best
the Government can do now, but the engineer
responsible for the awful fiasco should be sen-
tenced to spend the rest of his natural life on the
dampest part of Koo-wee-rup.
LITTLE CARRIE MOORE OF FIVE
YEARS AGO.
Photo, taken specially for THE BULLETIN by Mendelssohn ,
M elbourne.
A lecture recently given by Theodore
Bevan, in London, illustrates strikingly the con-
nection between missionaries and armed force as
it presents itself to the native mind. If the
Papuans were asked whose was the warship visit-
ing their coast the reply was always “ Tamate
lakatoi ” (Chalmers’ warship). Well they knew
the object of the big vessel was to protect or
avenge the missionary.
* * «
Abdul Wade, the Bourke camel-carrier,
has lately sent up 200 beasts to Winton (West
Q.), where another depot will be established.
But worst, the cheap brown man from the Punjab
(Abdul Wade scorns the suggestion that these
mild, long-suffering laborers are true Afghans) goes
with them.
WHIP MARKS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I’ve just put the bullocks to by-by
In the bend at the back of the dam,
And now at the camp-fire I lie by
And smoke while I listen to Sam :
From a piece of an old Australasian
They gave him back there at the station
He’s giving the boys Federation —
But who cares a d ?
The rents and the taxes they levy—
Resumptions of lease on the run—
Have long made the burden too heavy
For sad hearts and shoulders so young ;
And we’ve always had bad legislation
Hand-in-hand with a drought on the station —
The sheep die of thirst and starvation
’Neath a blasted hot sun.
You battle with burr, scrub, and thistle ;
You dig up and stack prickly pear ;
And curse, when the eaglehawks whistle,
The sight of a rabbit and hare.
Then at night, when the sky’s calm and clear,
The song of the dingoes you hear,
And you find when you muster and shear
The number’s not there.
All prices go down on to bed-rock,
Except for the things we require ;
The wool-market’s always at deadlock,
The bank’s putting interest up higher ;
Then comes the beloved selector,
The stock-tax-assessment collector,
The double-paid O.P. inspector—
A fat, greasy liar.
Then it rains—things look just a bit better ;
With luck in, you’ll surely get round ;
But the coach brings a mortgagee’s letter,
Which says you must transfer the ground.
The new man, all shirt-front and collar,
Says, “ Chappie, don’t fwet, have a swallah ;
You’ve always got dwoving to follah ”
The pigeon-toed hound !
In my mind now they march by like cattle—
The shuffling bank-boss in the rear ;
The C. P. inspector does battle
With Stock-Tax for place—and just hear
How the pockets of all seem to jingle
One tune ! while the voices all mingle
In a song called “ We lift the last shingle—
And God wipes away the last tear.”
Then fight on, ye beer-bloated bummers I
You may federate Heaven and Hell,
But you can’t cool the back-country summers,
Or keep the supply in our well.
May God in his self-renowned pity
Make Oobar or Louth Fed’ral City—
May Reid’s tongue get hard, dry and splithy—
The boko-eyed swell 1
N.S.W.

“ Leading'.’’
PRACTICAL MINER INTERVIEWED.
While in Perth, a special reporter had a chat
with Mr. Patrick Biggs, an intelligent and
experienced miner, well-known to masters and
men in Broken HiII(N.S.W.) —that mining centre
which contains the largest silver-mine in the
world. In the course of conversation, lead poison-
ing, or “leading,” as the miners call it, was
referred to, and Mr. Biggs freely mentioned it as
the curse of a miner’s life.
“ I was engaged in mining pursuits at Cobar
and Broken Hill for years,” said he, “and have
had a personal experience of ‘ leading.’ It was in
’97 that I became seriously ill with it. The doctor
was called in, and he unhesitatingly pronounced
the case one of lead poisoning. The symptoms in
my case were chronic constipation with violent
internal pains, and an inability to freely use the
hands. As time went on I did not progress as
favorably as was wished, so I went to Adelaide
for treatment. ”
“Were you successful there?” asked the re-
porter.
“ No ; the relief I obtained was only temporary.
I felt very disappointed, and decided to go on te
W. A., thinking the sea voyage might benefit me.
But the trip seemed to make me worse, and on
arrival at Perth I was almost unfit for work.
Although I had the treatment of two more doc-
tors, and from time to time tried nearly every
patent medicine advertised, I still remained un-
cured.”
‘ ‘ You could find nothing to work the poison
out of your system ? ” asked the reporter.
“No, I could not,” said Mr. Biggs, “till I
began using Dr. Williams’ pink pills for pale
people. As I continued taking them f gradually
regained good health. These pills worked the
poison out of my system. My blood became pure
and enriched, and I felt ever so much |stronger.
At present I feel better than I have ever done
before, and my recovery is solely due to Dr.
Williams’ pink pills. It will always give me
pleasure to make known the merits of so reliable a
specific. lam off to the Phillips River goldfields
shortly, and should I have sickness of any kind,
I will certainly use Dr. Williams’ pink pills
again.” Mr. Biggs is at present residing at Mary-
street, Perth.
In all cases of blood-poisoning and vitiated or
impoverished blood Dr. Williams’ pink pills will
be found invaluable. They free the blood from
impurities and increase the red corpuscles. At
the same time they tone up and repair the nervous
system. By their action on the blood and nerves
they cure rheumatism, neuralgia, sciatica, lum-
bago, bronchitis, effects of fevers, influenza, and
loss of manly strength, etc. Sold by chemists
and storekeepers and by the Dr. Williams’ Medi-
cine Co., Queen’s Place, Sydney, two and nine per
box; six boxes, fifteen and* three, post free.
Ladies will find them an ideal medicine. They
never fail to restore regular health, and so pro-
duce a brilliant and beautiful complexion. Sub-
stitutes are useless.*

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buver of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.

“S S. Concerning snakes in shafts
Me that snake imprisoning the Halhday Brothers in a
shaft for two days, one cannot help wondering that the
& oners 4 id " ot /° up ’ arld deal it out to the serpent
with a pick-handle. Snakes very often get into
shallow workings, and when grubbing in alluvial on a
Tas. field, self and mates were always most careful to
look out for snakes and scorpions when going below.
tasa
windlass him up to save my life. One warm Monday
morning had got Charley pretty well bottomed in our
Lift, shaft, when a frantic howl from below caused me to
hang to the handle all I knew. “ There’s a snake on the
bottom ! ” yelled the man in the shaft. “ Heave up, for
Cod’s sake—heave!” I heaved till my eyes stood
out like nobs on a tin trunk, but never an
inch could 1 lift fat Charley. On the contrary, he was
gaining on me bit by bit, and every inch he dropped he
fetched a wilder yell than before, and filled 64ft. of shaft
with linked profanity that spat out at the surface in vivid
lengths. I cried aloud, but no help came, and human
endurance could bear no more. Charley went to the
bottom with a rush and a bump, and for ten seconds I
liung over the mouth of that shaft with a drv tongue and
a heart thumping like a plunger. “ Ho-o-ow-how is it,
Oh-ch-charlej-?” I wailed. Then arose softly a jet of
limpid cursing that was sweetest music to my innocent
ears. “ And the snake, Charley ?” I said. “ Biowed if I
know,” replied Charles ; “ I’m on top of him ! ” Charley
did not stir a peg till we let a man down, and then only
with the greatest circumspection. But the snake was a
mangled and impotent ruin. Fat Charley had bumped
the life out of him

The Gippsland wild dogs which disinterred
and mangled the bodies washed up from the
wrecked s.s. Federal are a powerful cross-breed
between the dingo and the mastiff. They breed
freely, and grow almost to the size of a Newfound-
land. They are rapacious and fierce, and the
dingo strain in them alone prevents them from
being as dangerous as wolves.

Ticket-Taker,
The clearing of the Western country
(N.S.W.) by drought and over-stocking, and the
consequent shifting of sand, has laid bare at least
two aboriginal burying-grounds. In one, near
Wilcannia, several skeletons are exposed, along
with what apparently served for memorial-stones.
A peripatetic scientist has built up from the bones
and their disposition a paper for the next Science
Association Congress.
* * * •
This is a true bill from Barmah (Vic.)
where James, and William his mate, are sleeper-
hewing. James left his grub in a small leather
bag wrapped in his coat on a log, and towards
noon a howl from William drew his attention to
the actions of a predatory gohanna of great size.
When James rushed, the go. slid up a tree with
the strings of the leather bag hanging from its
mouth. James felled the tree, slew the go.,
withdrew the bag, and, having dined, expressed
himself blanky grateful for a warm meal.
Tinonee, Manning River, N.S.W., has
produced a Cliartier radish weighing 7flb.

kiln about two miles away from where Dunn escaped,
when a man crawled out from behind a log and begged
a “drink of water for God’s sake.” It was Dunn. He
told the brickmaker who he was, and asked him for a
horse to get away. But the brickmaker refused, went
in to Dubbo and brought out the police, who recaptured
the fugitive. The brickmaker was rewarded. Dunn was

Characteristic first news-item in Otago
Daily Times :
The individual Communion-cups were used at the
Morning-ton Presbyterian Church yesterday for the first
time, and gave every satisfaction.
Quite a formidable monument, erected by
Vic. Govt., now marks the spot where the Wel-
come nugget was found at Mt. Moliagul. The
finder’s name is engraved in big “caps,” and
trimmed with laurel “to encourage the others.”
S.S. Penarth, employed by the Admiralty
to take M.L. coal to the warships in China,
steamed back with Japanese coal in her bunkers,
and very bad material it was. “Jap coal,” says
the skipper, “ gives 24 buckets of ashes to a watch
of four hours, and Westport coal only three.”

The British aristocracy loses its dignity
badly at times. Australia’s recent acquaintance,
the Duke of Manchester, has only escaped this
disaster by never having any dignity to lose.
Manchester’s father made life a long and consider-
able splash, and left his estates bankrupt, and the
present dignitary is a promising young man with
champagne tastes and colonial beer pockets —or no
pockets at all. However, he has made provision
for the future by marrying the daughter of Cin-
cinnati millionaire Zimmerman, and papa Z. has
paid his debts—a little item of £37,000. At first,
the Yankee parent-in-law was not over friendly.
Manchester bought a couple of prize bulldogs in
N’York, on his way back from collecting the
gilded ’Murkan gal, at a cost of £3OO. The tykes
were placed on board, in kennels that were good
enough for York and Manchester themselves
respectively, and the common person wlio’d bred
them called round for his money. Manchester
promised to send a cheque from Ireland, but the
Yank guessed he’d have it there, right now.
Manchester referred him for his dog-money to the
new father-in-law, and Zimmerman flatly refused
to pay £3OO for “ dogs’-meat.” So the person
took his bulldogs home again, and Manchester
was left saying, “Bai jawve ! ”

Wily old W. H. Groom’s self-praise in liis
own paper, Toowoomba Chronicle, is quaint
reading. Advantage is taken of the ancient’s
transfer from State to Federal Legislature to once
more point out the world’s debt to Groom for
existing 39 years in one place, an immovable blob
of red mud.
Thirty-nine years ago ! Why, the German Empire of
to-day was not founded, and the veteran Kaiser, William
I, had only just been crowned at Konigsberg King of
Prussia.
Me and the other Kaiser—we started together.
But William I has gone, and William Henry I is
still on deck. Watch this spot !
The “ Man of Mystery ” was in the full blaze of that
meteoric career that ended so disastrously at Sedan.
So much for Nap ! But see me ! I’m still in the
full blaze of my career, and it isn’t meteoric.
Walk round me and admire me —there’s no
charge !
The United States writhed in the throes of civil war,
and the very day that Mr. Groom first addressed the
electors of Drayton and Toowoomba, Abraham Lincoln is-
sued his celebrated proclamation of the confiscation of the
property of the rebels, and on the very day the poll
was taken M'Olellan was being beaten back from Harri-
son’s Landing after the loss of 70,0H0 men. .. . Lincoln’s
immortal proclamation of freedom to the slaves had not
even been issued until five weeks after the Drayton and
Toowoomba electiorf.
Groom has had trouble with an election petition
lately; doubtless old Abe waited to make sure
Groom wouldn’t be unseated before doing his
emancipation act. Yes, Groom lias seen all that
(and lots more in the ChSONICLe’s leading article),
and still he lives 1 How can you account for it?
Only because Drayton and Toowoomba are the
centre of the earth, which revolves round Grooms-
axis every 24 hours by distinguished permission.

Tlie Independent Order of . Fore 5 ters.
What a distinguished Insurance man in London, England, thinks of the Independent Order of Foresters.
Mr. William Schooling, known to most insurance people as the eminent editor of Bourne, the great insurance journal, shows how he
feels with regard to the financial stability and the wisdom of the methods pursued by the Independent Order of Foresters, in the following
letter written to Oronhyatekha, Supreme Chief Ranger of the Order, on the first day of the new century: —
BOURNE’S INSURANCE INSTITUTE LTD.
Wm. Schooling, Editor and Manager. Granville House, Arundel Street, Strand, W.C.
January 1, 1901.
ORONHYATEKHA, M.D., J.P., G.C.R., Toronto, Canada.
Dear Sir and Brother. —It is, with peculiar satisfaction that, on the first day of the new century, I write to tell you that
I have become a Beneficiary Member of the Independent Order of Foresters. My pleasure is only mitigated by one circum-
stance, which is that, Isy becoming a member now, I cannot avail myself of your very gratifying offer to personally
initiate me into the Order.
You will readily understand that my work leads me to criticise pretty keenly all matters connected with insurance, and
I have not formed my present high opinion of the T.O.F. without looking very carefully into the facts.
Permit me to express the great pleasure I derived from your al!-to-short visit to me a few weeks ago, and to express the
hope that I may have some longer talks with you when you are next in London.
I have told Brother Cronyn that I want to help the Order in every way that I can, and have asked him to let me know
when any opportunities of doing so arise. To-day I had a visit from Brother Reynolds, with whom I had a very interesting
chat, and I am already beginning to experience the pleasant results of fraternal member ship.
Please accept my heartiest good wishes for the happiest prosperity for the Order, and for yourself personally, and
believe me, yours fraternally in L.B. and C.
(Signed) WILLIAM SCHOOLING.
The above letter, from so eminently distinguished an insurance expert as Mr. Schooling, is the best possible evidence that the Independent
Order of Foresters is an institution of the first class.

£900,000 of surplus assets. £1,800,000 already paid to widows and orphans. Surplus reserve increasing monthly at the rate of £12,000 per month.
Representatives wanted in every community. For full information and literature, address G. A. FRASER, Head Offices for New South Wales, 283 George
Street, Sydney. ORONHYATEKHA, Supreme Chief Ranger.

The monster public deputation in favor of
Co-Operative coupons (introduced by Mr. Duncan
Gillies, M.L.A.) established an Australian record ;
over 500 people attended. The speakers were
limited by Mr. Peacock to five, but they rep-
resented an enormous number of the public. __ M**,
Nelson (representing 2000 workers in the railway
service) said : “This coupon system has taught us
thrift and brought pleasure to a great many
homes.” Mr. Davis (President Brickmakers’
Association) said that “ The public realised that
it is the only system by which they can get dis-
count on all small purchases.”*

Total Transvaal war-deaths to June-end
arc 774 officers, 15,900 men—16,674. And sick
and wounded probably six times as many—say
100,000 —total of casualties upwards of 120,000.
With the defeat of Potato Young for the
Manning the number of members of the old Reid
Administration in N.S.W. Assembly is reduced
to four—Carruthers, Hogue, Lee, and Sunday
B ranker.
TO FINDLEY.
Go, thank your feeble foeman who
Did cast his honor down to give
You precedence, and made of you
A People’s representative !
What a hideous scene of decrepitude is
afforded by the Victorian Leg. Councillors !
Looking down on their crowns it makes one
think of a political convocation of tortoises.
What impudence it is for these to pretend to
exist with the example of the Senate before them !
Ed. Bulletin. — Be much-discussed re-
lations between squatter and selector, let me point
out that the selec-
tor'has only him-
self to blame for
most of the dis-
abilities hesuffers.
True, the squatter
loathes the selec-
tor, and putsevery
obstacle in the way
of his making a
living (with much
success, too —
members of land-
board ° being gen-
eral!} squatters
and giving notori-
ously class-deci-
sions in disputes
between the big
and the little man).
Also, the squatter
is at continual
loggerheads with
the selector over
fencing, bound-
aries, gates, &c.,
and appears to
consider him, so-
cially, as beneath
contempt. But,
for all this, the
selector ranges
himself on the
squatter’s side in
all industrial dis-
putes, votes
solidly for the
squatter’s
man at elec-
tions, and
says, speak-
ing of a can-
didate belong-
ing to the
only body that
has the power
or the will to
lighten his back-
crushing burdens,
“ Oh, yes ! —decent chap enough, no doubt —but I
->ould never vote for a Labor-man.” —A.T.H.
l'he Queensland constituencies of Charters
Towers and Bundaberg return two new Labor
members to the Assembly, in place of Common-
wealth Senators Dawson and Glassey. The Bun-
daberg victory, in the centre of the chief Q.
sugar-farming district, gives further and emphatic
confirmation of Q. belief in “White Australia.”
The Federal Senate rejected Senator
Staniforth Smith’s motion that the territory ac-
quired by the Commonwealth for the seat of
Government should consist of not less than 1000
square miles—which was a foolish and unlucky
thing for the Senate to do. The only thing that
was wrong with Smith’s motion was that it didn’t
go far enough.
“They fancied that if they could only get
a new deal they’d beat us,” said N.S.W. Premier
See of his opponents, the other night. “They
can’t win,” exclaimed an elector —“they’ve lost
the joker.” True—humorist G. H. Reid was to
the N.S.W. Calico Jimmies even as Samson’s
hair was to that man of might. It is the fellow
with the jokes who scores.
The value of Parliamentary institutions !
The uses of a Cabinet Minister! A Sydney daily
seriously urges N.S.W. Works Minister O’Sulli-
van to —clean-up the mud on Circular Quay pave-
ment ! Good old Local Government! ” Mean-
while, of course, there are hundreds of pioneer
settlements in the backblocks where there are no
pavements at all—not even roads !
Some interesting statistics : On April Ist,
the population of Thursday Island ran thus : 705
Euro eans ; 304 Japanese ; 83 Filipinos ; 113
aborigines ; 115 Chinese ; 55 Malays ; 81 South
Sea Islanders ; 54 Cingalese; 7 Javanese ; 22
Hindoos; 29 Papuans; 121 other mixed races.
Total, 1689. Can any equally small patch of
country on God’s earth show a similar variety ?
“ Occy ” writes from Melbourne :
I bought a Tocsin on the day it published the alleged
libel. Next day my newspaper-man offered me, first, 6d.,
next Is., then 18d., and finally 2s. for it; the Argus had
made that paper worth 24 times its original price. What
the newspaper man would have sold it for, Heaven knows.
1 have lent it to 20 friends already, just to show how
silly the whole reference to Royalty was. Queerly
enough, thousands are furious without knowing what to
be furious about.
On the evening of N.S.W. polling day
George Reid told Lvne in the House of
Reps, how a disgusted State was going to cast
the remnant of his discredited Ministry into
oblivion. For the rest of that night an angry and
globular prophet curled up in unwonted silence on
the Opposition benches, while a long man jeered
him at intervals, and asked him what weather we
were going to have next week.
Melb. Age, the day after Findley’s expul-
sion from Vic. Assembly, paraded the views of a
Ballarat stock-jobber that not only was Findley’s
departure a joyous circumstance, but that the
whole 17 who voted against his expulsion should
also be thrown out violently. Talk about
Russia’s press-censorship and suppression of free
speech !

probably what lost his party the seat for King
Division of Sydney. His own party candidate
was a Roman Catholic. Reid evidently forgot
this, and in his memorable speech to the electors
gratuitously threw large stupid bricks at O’Sulli-
van’s religion. Those who believed
in Reid went away with the fixed

and to feel that it will be your own fault if you do
not find your way into their affections, is a position
which any man in the world may be proud to hold.”

Gympie (Q.) election afforded the Philpites
an opportunity of getting in some really fine work.
Some 52 names were ready to go on the Gympie
roll and the authorities had expressed an opinion
that the owners of the names were entitled to vote
at the recent election. All of a sudden official
opinion underwent a change, and the gallant 52
found they couldn’t vote —simply because the list
hadn’t been printed. Labor won all the same.
S.M. Herald’s otherwise valuable report
of proceedings in the Commonwealth Parliament
AFTER THE BATTLE.
At the recent N.S.W. State elections the candidates com-
plained of the uncongenial weather for electioneering.
The member for Bunkumville indulges in luxuries.
is spoilt by its grandmotherly addiction to stereo-
typed forms. What’s the use of citing “ The Vice-
President of the Executive Council,” and soon?
Why not name the man and his territory ? Add
his office, if necessary. But citing his office with-
out his name is hotel-waiter business, where the
man is known by the number of his room only.
The Federal House of Representatives did
a wise thing in deciding that a Minister shall
draw his Ministerial salary and also his salary as
a private member. Even when the two are added
together the country expects to get a man who
can run the financial affairs of a continent for
about half the salary of a man who runs a big
bank —and the bank man has practically a life
billet, and probably a pension, and he isn’t abused
in the newspapers.
Sydney Telegraph, its party having been
licked with great enthusiasm at N.S.W. State
elections, says it has gained “amoral victory,”
anyhow. There never was an individual sitting
on the floor of the ring with a broken nose and a
black eye and a head six times its usual size, nor
an individual carrying home the remaining half of
his dog for decent burial after the dog-fight, who
hadn’t a vague idea that, somehow or other, his
licking was a moral victory.
Many old members of N.S.W. Assembly
who went up for re-election based their claims for
a fresh lease on the amount of Govt, money they
had managed to get expended in their electorates
during their term of office. Some gave detailed
statements, running up to £50,000. John Nor-
ton’s little bill was a modest £25,000. Either
these sums were fairly due, in which case no
special credit attached to the member, or they
weren’t due, in which case it was plain Thievery.
Anyhow, bring along the I/ocal Government Bill!
The most experienced and clearest-eyed
of the N.S.W. Labor-members are doubting
whether their party will be able to exercise so
great a power in proportion to its numbers as
it has done for years past. Chairman of Commit-
tees Cann puts it that the Labor-men’s power has
been the result no more of their own solidarity
than of the solidarity of the other two parties,
and that with the latter solidarity gone the Labor
party will be as strong only as it is strong in num-
bers.
N.S.W. Opposition leader Lee says that
the See Government “ appears content to hold on
to office at the caprice of the various sections of
the Assembly.” Well, it does appear that way.
And the oidy time Lee had a Ministerial billet the
Government he belonged to held office at the
caprice of the Labor section. And now Lee is
going to challenge the Govt, in the hope of getting
a chance to hold office by the caprice of the same
sections whose caprice is keeping See in office at
present. What a silly hypocrisy is the whole busi-
ness of party politics !
Senator Stewart has unearthed the fact
that Reynolds’s Newspaper is not admitted into
Victoria. It only differs from any other great
British weekly by reason of its republican senti-
ments and its want of adequate crawl to the
British Cro\yn, and the British people object to
that so little that Reynolds’s has one of the largest
circulations over there. But Victoria is much
more loyal to Britain than Britain is to itself, and
it won’t stand Reynolds’s. Senator Jingo Zeal,
by the way, said he had never heard of Rey-
nold’s. How on earth do men of Zeal’s ignorance
get into politics ?
In Snowy River district (Gippsland, Vic.),
there are now hundreds of Hindus —ploughing,
milking, scrub-cutting, etc., but mostly maize-
picking. They work interminable hours for the
meanest wage, camp in squalid gunyahs, and live
on maize and damper till they’ve saved enough to
start as hawkers. An instance is given of a scrub-
cutting contract for which the lowest “ white”
tender was £4. Eight Hindus came along and
cleared the ground to the last leaf for 30s. ! The
maize-grower hasn’t yet the cheek to assert that
his industry is doomed unless he gets coolie labor,
but he’ll presently furbish up some such theory
unless his enterprise is nipped in the bud.
Senator Barrett, to justify direct
taxation, and especially an absentee-
tax, lately quoted the familiar instance
known as “ Nicholson’s Corner.” This
Melbourne property was purchased 57
years ago for £1072. Now it is worth
£5,331,000; and the various owners
have already drawn upwards of
£7,000.000 in rents. Principal owner
has been an absentee for some score of
years. “Nicholson’s Corner” is an
example of the advantages of “ bring-
ing in foreign capital to develop the
country ” ; £1072 of British capital
came into Australia, and by reason of
it two or three millions have already
gone to England, while Australia still
owes England the best part of
£5,000,000. And the local rich men,
like Zeal and Sargood, are too dull to
see that an absentee-tax on the outside
rich man would go to lighten their
own burdens.
Edward Braddon, who for five
and a-half years was Premier of Tas-
mania and ran that State on Protec-
tionist principles, sits with the Free-
trade Party in the Federal House of
Reps., and makes long, soulful orations
about his Freetrade principles. And,
like all the rest of his tribe, he ex-
plains that he was driven to practise
Protection by his State’s financial
necessities. If a Freetrader, as soon
as his State gets into trouble, is driven
to practise Protection to get it out
of trouble, then it is a poor advt. for
Freetrade. A policy that is of no use
in bad weather is a leaky and disma.stedpolicy
without a rudder.
It is announced that Vic. Attorney General
Gillott (front name Samuel) is trying to find out
“if the libel on the King in the Tocsin was
written by an officer in the Vic. public service.”
Yet Samuel is perfectly aware that the “ libel ” in
question was written in Ireland. All the matter
written in Victoria was a complaint that the
suppression of the Irish People was illegally con-
ducted, and a statement that the language used
about the King was a foolish piece of abuse. If the
pallid Gillott finds that the Tocsin’s commen-ts on
the Irish libel were written by a public official, he
will probably foam at the mouth a good deal, and
also sack the official without trial in an under-
hand, “ un-British ” fashion. But it is dead cer-
tain that he won’t dare to take legal proceedings
against the writer, and let the courts decide fairly
and squarely whether his remarks really were
libellous.
Westralia has on hand a railway strike
which threatens to cause the closing-down of the
Kalgoorlie mines. The men want better pay, and
they wouldn’t mind having also the scalps of one
or two overbearing officials. But the better
pay is the main point. The employes had many
grievances under the old Forrest and Throssell
regime, and they seem to have given the new
Leake Government very little time in which to
remedy them. Premier Leake says he made
a perfectly bona-fide offer to submit the
whole difficulty to arbitration, the men being
fairly represented on the arbitration board, and
his offer appears to have been refused with need-
less emphasis. Seemingly the men didn’t wait to
see if Leake’s arbitration was anything different
from the old Forrest variety. On the other hand,
the wages are so wretched that it is asked why
it should be necessary to arbitrate on the small
increase asked for.
N.S.W. general election of 1901 will be
long remembered as the defeat of Sydney Daily
Telegraph. Never was that versatile journal
more cocksure of victory, and never did it fail
more utterty. Not only in the total result did it
fail, but in its own specific appeals. In Gipps-
division, its candidate (one Johnson, an old-time
single-tax bore) was, day after day, eulogised as a
brilliant and magnificent genius, whose victory
was certain. Johnson polled 197 votes, his
opponents 1636. Donnelly, whom the paper
specially pleaded for, in Balmain South, was
over 1000 *votes behind the winner. That
eloquent and heaven-sent orator M‘Naught,
who nightly drew fascinated crowds to Red-
fern, didn’t make M‘Gowen fight hard enough
to get warm. Premier See whose seat was in
jeopardy, polled 9(hper cent, of the votes cast.
O’Sullivan, “fighting with his back to the wall ”
won by hundreds at Queanbeyan. The D.T.
selected 86 candidates, 65 at least absolute cer-
tainties. Not 40 were returned. By 10 a.m. next
day, all the polling-record arrangements in front
of the Telegraph office were dismantled, though
many seats were still in dispute. The D.T. was so
narked that it sadly removed the testimony of its
failure, instead of allowing the admiring public
to inspect it for a week as heretofore.

Dear Sir, —Having' been a sufferer for 16 years from
chronic dyspepsia, enlargement of the liver, and other

9 herbalists and specialists, and receiving no relief what-

large bottles, to my surprise, I found myself recovering
from the above dreaded complaints. I am a painter by

known in and around this district, and all my friends
congratulate me on my speedy recovery, as they all ex-
pected me to succumb, after being so unsuccessfully
treated by some of the best medical faculty that Sydney
could produce.
For the sake of suffering humanity I present you this
grateful testimonial, trusting that some poor sufferer

dear Sir, yours ever grateful,
SAMUEL SEARLE.

The latest English geographical gem is
from London Daily News, which tells that a cer-
tain Mr. Rudd is living in Queensland , Westj Aus-
tralia.
A juvenile “ grass-widow,” who applied
for maintenance against her hubby, at Christ-
church (M.L.), lately, said that he had been guilty
of false pretences ; he told her that he was getting a
whole pound a week, and it was on that repre-
sentation that she married him. After six weeks
of wedded bliss he took to drink, and had been in
beer ever since.
“ Count B.” : That Philmayism (8.,
l/6/’G) reminds me of an incident in a Sydney
printing establishment long since defunct. Most
of the comps, were taking holidays, biz. being down
below zero. One of them, however, with a perti-
nacity that became monotonous, called three and
four times a day on the chance of work. “ Look
here, Jimmy,” said the boss at last, “ don’t be so
very sudden. When I get any work in I’ll
send for you.” After that Jimmy kept away for
three days, then again reappeared before the boss.
“Ah, good morning, sir. Anything in, sir?”
“ Yes,” said the boss, curtly. “ Ah, then I sup-
pose I’ll get a start. What is it—wool cata-
logue ? ” “No ; two bailiffs and a stray dog and
a man come to cut off the gas.”

“ Christian Science ” is coming rapidly to
a head in England, and the craze may be ex-
pected to strike Australia hard at an early date.
Meanwhile, the story of a case heard in Vookers-
ville (U.S.), is bursting with significance. Miss
Kate M'Culloch went to Rev. Geo. Tompkins to
be souled and healed, and, reckoning she was
merely sold, sued for the return of her 4000
dollars. She declared that Rev. Tompkins’ cure
consisted mainly of kissing, and Rev. Tompkins
retorted that plaintiff admitted after the kissing
that she experienced a peaceful and restful feeling.
He undertook to complete the cure if plaintiff
would undergo another course of “scientific
kissing. ” The kiss cure is a promising idea, and
the tall, soulful-looking healer who starts the
game here will drive a big business amongst the
submerged tenth of the sex—the frostbitten
spinsters. Hitherto it has been the belief that
men caught dangerous ailments through indis-
criminate kissing. Writer know a few who caught
wives through it anyhow.
