An indignant artist writes to The Bulletin re
a Victorian art-move :
In slavish imitation of old-world forms, the Vic. Artists’
Society has taken the ridiculous step of creating “ Acade-
micians.” Considering that there is no Academy, these
persons (naturally comprising the little crowd which runs
the show) resemble the so-called Rose of Denmark lifeboat
crew, which exists in the inland suburb of Collingwood.
The coterie of “Academicians” has also decided to ap-
point “ associates,” but these will hold their titles only
from year to year, so that any associate who seems to be
attracting too much public attention and cutting into the
business of the Academicians can be conveniently
omitted from next year’s list. This endeavor to
create an aristocracy of art is worthy of a body of
ham-and-beef merchants or the organisers of a suburban
corner in tripe. It is manifestly an attempt to gull the
public, who know nothing about art, into “ dealing off ”
the duly-qualified academicians. Admitting that the
first list is artistically strong, the principle is entirely
vicious, and the whole trend of the distinction will be to
exalt one particular coterie at the expense of other, and,
in time, probably more meritorious, painters.
The cold frosty weather down South has
persuaded all sensible men to pay more attention
than usual to the best brand for supplying nourish-
ment and warmth. This, by common consent, is
D.C.L. The man who partakes judiciously of
that genial whisky doesn’t shiver with cold and
become dull and heavy at the very moment when
he is anxious to appear bright and cheerful—nor
at any other moment. Nor does he gather up
every available influenza or cold fiend that stalketh
abroad. Because D.C.L. prevents these things it
is known as “ the whisky of the wise.”
During a Saturday afternoon ride it is no un-
common sight to see cyclists riding their tyres on the
rims, or sitting on the roadside awaiting the arrival of
some good-natured fellow cyclist, who will explain and
help to repair the leaky tyre. An ordinary puncture can
easily be repaired, and the machine ready for riding in 10
minutes if the cyclist only knows how to go about it, and
has the necessary repair materials at hand. There is no
excuse for ignorance in this matter, when cyclists can ob-
tain gratis one of the Dunlop Co,’s beautifully illustrated
booklet--" All. about Dunlop Tyres.” This hrochure
contains hundreds of little wrinkles useful to cyclists,
and apart from its artistic merits is well worth keeping
as a standard work on pneumatic tyres. Ask .your cycle
agent for a copy, or send an application to the Dunlop
Company’s office.*

A Critical Case.
A SENIOR-INSPECTOR OP POLICE SUBMITS HIS
EVIDENCE.
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills add one more
name to the long list of their wonderful cures. It
is for those once placed on the verge of the grave,
and who have been given a longer term of life by
the timely use of this wonderful remedy, to give
the Pills their due of praise; All who use them
give them a good name, but it is only those who,
after a long and dangerous sickness, feel through
their use the rich blood of perfect health in their
veins that can bring warmth and conviction and
the clear ring of manly and vigorous truth to their
words. Such a man is Senior-Inspector W. T.
Langworthy, of Yass, New South Wales, who
writes as follows : —“Dear Sirs, —For many years
I have been a sufferer from rheumatism, constipa-
tion, giddiness, violent pains in the head, and a
‘ liver,’ colds, chills, and, worse than all, lumbago ;
of course, I got temporary relief from doctors and
chemists. At last I read your pamphlet on Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills, and I considered the
vegetable ingredients would not hurt, and took
four, then three, and no pain. The change was
wonderful. Instead of leaning against the- wall
while putting on my trousers, as usual, through
giddiness, I was able to stand upright, and 1 felt a
desire to turn a somersault upon the bed as a boy.
You will laugh. Well, sirs, it is a fact. I have
used them now occasionally for three years, and
carry the little bottle with me on the road when
travelling. I recommend them to everybody,
with good results. Whenever I look at the little
bottle, I feel I owe one debt to you, and now I
will repay it by expressing my gratitude.”- 1 (I
have no objection to my testimonial being used
relative to the benefit I derived from Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills.—W. T. Langwokthy ) Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills are a perfect blood
purifier, and a positive cure for biliousness, indi-
gestion, constipation, headaches, sallow com-
plexion, liver and kidney troubles, piles, pimples,
boils and blotches, and female ailments. Sold by
chemists and storekeepers, Is. per bottle, or
six bottles 6s. 6d. Sole proprietors, The W. H.
Comstock Co., Ltd. (Australasian Depot), 58 Pitt-
street, Sydney. Packed in amber bottles, and the
full name blown thereon.*
Among the most desirable new members
of N.S.W. Assembly, is that excellent speaker,
Mr. Dan Levy, a young barrister, elected for
Fitzroy Division of Sydney. Mr. Levy has, so
far, made his way in the world solely by his
personal merits ; during a brilliant Sydney Uni-
versity career he fairly staggered under the weight
of his prizes.
The fight of N.S.W. general election was
that for the Grenfell seat, won after a desperate
battle by the late member, that eloquent young
Radical, Holman. Out of all the members of
N.S.W. Assembly (about 38) who had originally
openly expressed themselves as opposed to Aus-
tralian interference in S’Africa, he was one
of the little remnant of three or four who
through the crisis remained unbounced by Jingo
threats and blow, and had the courage to stand
to their guns. So at the election he was
venomously attacked above-ground and from
under-ground by every method, fair and foul. He
fought with marvellous vigor, and gained many
votes by his final speech before a great audience
of Wyalong gold-miners. Voting : Holman,

The highest recommendation ever given
to Australian wine has been scored by the Coolalta
wines of Mr. J. A. Wilkinson. In addition to
being appointed wine-merchant to Hopetoun, the
first Governor-General, Mr. Wilkinson has re-
ceived a very flattering compliment from H.R.H.
the Duke of York, through the A.D.C., Lord
Crichton. The A.D.C.’s letter, which the writer
of this paragraph has perused, states that the
Coolalta wines were very much appreciated by
His Royal Highness.
From this it would seem that Findley’s
chances are not quite Buckley’s :
Fint'ley, the expelled of Vic. Legislature, looks a dead
sure thing for E. Melb. at present date. 200 votes, nearly
all strongly labor, have been added to the roll since the
seat became vacant, and there are many candidates in the
field, one and all of whom command sufficient following
to impel them to go to the poll. M‘Clean, bosom friend
of old John Anderson before his death, commands the
personal-popularity vote of the late meml er and a big
section of the R.C’s. L. L Smith has been rounded out
by old-time cronies, and has met with a reception too
tempting to let slip. Shakesperian scholar Diegan is the
chosen Govt, candidate, and Willy Watt, ex-Postmaster-
General, has a record which will command enough votes to
give him a great show in the fight. Brodzky, the
Polish gentleman who runs Table Talk, is out-Jingoing
the most ultra-conservatives, and consequently has the
support of people who would not otherwise look at him.
Lastly, there is Renfrew, who came, out at the eleventh
hour at last election and was beaten by hut a few votes.
To each of the candidates the chances of return are
sufficiently rosy to make “loyalty” a secondary con-
sideration ; and, when one divides the votes on the roll
by seven (even remembering that all the Melbourne Club
members have votes, wherever they reside), Findley has
a far better chance of being returned than he had last
time in Melbourne City.
There was a great disturbance in Vic.
Assembly when “ Jack ” Murray, in fiery criticism
of a slobbersome stock-jobber who had recom-
mended terrible penalties for those 16 members
who refused to support the Govt, in its expulsion
of Findley, uttered scornful remarks about the
morality of sharebrokers and the whole herd of
bulls and bears. Murray might have libelled the
Host and all the archangels without producing
half the effect. H Proputty,” which is first
cousin to Stocks and Shares, was furious, and
Premier Peacock wildly slapped his brisket and
drew' attention to the fact that he was a dealer in
stocks and shares, and he defied the world to say
that he was not the incarnation of all the virtues.
At this Murray apologised. It’s a dangerous
thing to revile a sharebroker of the Empire. Is
he not the modern War God?

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Vic. Grand National Hurdles (Saturday) proved
another boil-over similar to that supplied by Aquarius
last year. Winner (Shoddy) was very much an outsider
(labelled by many allegedly cute people as well nigh an
impossible chance), in face of his work immediately
before ; still Mo whines that he lost on the race, as is his
custom. Shoddy is by Trenton from the First King mare
Chintz ; bred by Mr. John Crozier. He used to be
raced by “ Jimmy ” W .Ison, who leased him to Mr. Hind-
haugh, of the VVestern District, whence sprung last year’s
surprise. His time, smins. 52Jsec., equals the record put
up by Aquarius. On April 23 Shoddy annexed the 2m.
hurdles at Warrnambool, humping 9.10, and, a day later,
gathered in the Amateur Club’s hurdle event with'll.s in
the saddle.
* * • •
Shoddy carried 9.11. Six winners in the
last nine years have carried less than 10st., and
one other was on the lOst. mark. Seeing that the
heavier-burdened horses suffer unduly for their form, the
question of a 9.7 minimum and a 12.7 maximum is worth
considering.
• * • •
Shoddy and Pirate King held the lead from
beginning to end of the three-mile journey, and it
was not until they reached the distance-post that the
little ’un was beaten by his weight. Shoddy is bigger and
more seasoned, so the difference of 191 b. in his favor was
actually greater than it looked. Although he started at
an outside price, Shoddy was quite a popular pick among
punters, who looked for a light-weight victory. With
Pirate King out of the way, it would have seemed a one-
horse race, probably. The live-year-old King must be
a very game bit of stuff, for he insisted on leading the
field up to the last hurdle, and then, after being collared,
he got his head in front once again. Considering the fast
time and the close fight he made of it, Pirate King,
at list. 21b., ranks as one of the best performers in the
history of the G.N. Hurdle Race.
Pirate (without the King) should have some-
thing important to say in the Steeplechase next Satur-
day. Emma seems to be fancied, also Freedom, but it is
hard to see how the latter can be expected to beat Laceby
ou their recent running. Given four trustworthy legs
when the big event falls due, Laceby, in present scribe’s
opinion, is likely to beat Emma and Pirate, and every-
thing else.
• • • •
Last year the three places in G.N. Hurdle Race
were filled by Aquarius, Rackstraw and Goldfinder.
Winner and runner-up were regarded as hopeless chances
before the event, whilst Goldfinder started first favorite.
This year the same three horses, not being handicapped
“ out of it,” were obviously “ well in it ” by virtue of their
previous running, but within a week of the race the trio
came to grief on the track and were scratched. Ikey Mo
looks at racing in the light of such experiences as this.
The punter talks of his fancy gee-gee as bound to win
next Melbourne Cup, and Ikey thinks of the animal as
likely to be broken-down or dead by the first week in
November.
• * « •
Field forG.N. Hurdles, Saturday, was the biggest
since ’95, but not a single fall occurred.
* * » *
Among the 21 winners of V.R.O. Grand Na-
tional Hurdles there have been 10 aged nags, three six-
year olds, four five-year olds, three four-year-olds, and one
three-year-old.
» * * »
Gondolier mare Emmalea was the only one of
her sex who succeeded in Grand National Hurdles, which
she won with 11.6 six years ago. That was her sixth con-
secutive victory in Australia. Emmalea went ’Ome soon
afterwards, but has never notched a single win there.
* * * *
First V.R.C. Grand National Hurdles, 20 years
ago, was appropriated by Martin Loughlin’s Sir Peter,
with the late Tommy Corrigan up. After ’B6 the journey
was lengthened to three miles, and Mr. S. G. Cook (per
medium of Yeoman) landed the first subsequent event
In ’BB the stake (£500) was doubled, when Malua, with his
owner (J. O. Inglis) in the saddle, scored a soft win.
Martin Burke piloted the winner (Crusoe) in ’9l—a 20 to 1
chance—and, though he secured two Nationals over the
same course, this was his only success in the Hurdles.
Tim Swiveller, who subsequently lost Caulfield Cup
on Sainfoin’s protest, won. in ’93—J. E. Brewer had the
mount.
* * * *
The good thing (on paper) for the Epsom and
Metrop. double is Ferryman. The mile (with 9.2 up)
shouldn’t cost him much of an effort; it probably wouldn’t
were the burden 2st. greater. In the Metrop. he has
7.13, and though he did shape badly in a mile and a-
quarter race at Randwiolt after running well in the
Doncaster, it must not be forgotten that Ferryman is
bred (by Gozo from a half-sitter to Seaweed —dam of
Merman) to stay.
+ * • *
The old complaint about the dragging-out of
the Y.R.C. programme is always a specially bitter grum-
ble at the winter meetings. A great man}' members and
the whole crowd of non-members object to the club’s con-
servative policy of starting the first race at 1 o’clock,
instead of 2 o’clock, and fixing a luncheon interval of 75
minutes between the second and third races. Only a few
people want to feed at Flemington after 2.45 p.m. in July.
For the sake of these few, stewards and others, the public
in general have to spend nearly four hours on the course,
with only five events to interest them. A slow, chilly
entertainment for the men, and even more so for the
women who shiver in the stands. And the railway fare
to Flemington by race-train continues to be exactly' eight
times the ordinary charge, although the rival V.A.T.C.
has got the fare to Caulfield reduced to normal propor-
tions, and is now running the show to suit the conveni-
ence of the gate-money crowd.
* * * +
Haut Brion colt Hautboy, who nine months ago
promised much, but was withdrawn through leg trouble,
recently made another start, but the bother showing
again, his preparation stopped, and Mr. R. M. Fitzgerald,
of Tongy (N.S.W.), secured him for stud purposes for
£5OO. Dirt-cheap for such a noted and well-bred per-
former, who ran 13 times last season, and won V.A.T.C.
Mona Nursery, V.R.C. Nursery Handicap, Flemington
Stakes, A. J.C. Nursery, and December Stakes.
The sons and daughters of Little Bernie
keep winning every week up North. He is easily first as
regards winners, and his stock seem to inherit good under-
pinning and stoutness of heart. Little 8., it will be
remembered, won Sydney' Cup for his owner, Bookie Joe
Abrahams.
» • • *
A curious case was heard at Sydney Central last
week. A man was prosecuted for laying a wager in a
King-street betting-shop. Two policemen and an in-
former swore to his identity as the man who issued the
betting-ticket produced in court. Their story was that
the informer entered the shop and made a bet. Next day or
so the police were waiting outside, and arrested accused
as the man who issued the ticket. His story was that he
was only a casual customer “ taking a double ” on his
own account, who happened to walk in. It is only the
layer of the odds who can be proceeded against, and as a
£soor£loofine was the penalty accused was in a tight place.
After the two police and their agent had sworn to his
identity accused called his defence. The shop-owner
swore he was not in his employ. Four witnesses proved
an alibi, and the writing on the betting-ticket was shown
to be widely dissimilar from that of defendant. Still the
“ beak ” looked doubtful, for there were three witnesses
who absolutely swore to his identity. Luckily', the shop-
keeper then played a trump card. The real offender was
produced, who wrote for the magistrate a facsimile of
the betting ticket. On being confronted with the two
men the police quite failed in deciding which was the
right one, and the agent straight out admitted that he was
uncertain. But it cost some £2O to clear the wrongfully-
accused man, and, having sworn to the identity of one
man, the police could hardly muster face enough to pro-
ceed against the real culprit. How many people have
been sent to the gallows or to prison on erroneous identi-
fications?
* * * *
During the protracted debate on the Tattersall
clause of Che Postal Bill in the Senate, some of its
supporters dwelt Strongly on the allegation that many
embezzlements were due to sweep investments. People
who follow racing are aware that this is seldom or never the
case. The man who invests in sweeps knows perfectly
well that his chance of pulling off a big prize is almost in-
finitesimal, and 19 out of every 20 subscribers to Tatt.
probably regard the money as gone as soon as they post
it. The prospective embezzler, on the other hand, is a
man who possesses the sanguine, not the philosophical,
temperament. He is the close student of form, the
acquirer of mysterious and valuable information from the
jockey’s cousin. He steals his employer’s cash, not to
flutter on the off-chance of scooping a fortune, but with
the absolute certainty a certain horse will win. Speaking
generally, the sweep investor recognises that the chances
are overwhelmingly against him. The backer of horses
with other peoples’ money is as sure in his own mind as
he is of the morrow that he will be able to replace the cash
and have a substantial lump for himself through the win
of his fancied nag.

• * • *
Australian bowlers are not scoring so well at
'Ome as a Week or two since. To date they’ve had seven
matches in Scotland, winning three and losing four.
Victorian manager Oudden’s rink seems to be the weak
point now. In the last match against Crosshill, at Glas-
gow, he went under 7 to 28 ; previous one saw the oppo-
sition score 2 to 1 more than him. At Perth, Cudden
was beaten by 21 to 12 ; at Edinburgh, 24 to 23 ; and at
Galashiels, 28 to 15—all successive defeats. Yet, just
before the sluiiip set in he pulled the combination through
the Howick match, notching 41 to the Scots’ 7. M.L.
players have so far lost 7 out of 12 matches played.
Apropos the secession of M.L. section. They wanted to
form two rinks of the Australasian four, wear a distinc-
tive uniform (which had already been ordered), and be
exempt from contributing to the manager’s ex’s.
* * * *
Of the much-lauded New Crystal Palace bowl-
ing-green, run by champion cricketer, W. G. Grace, Mr.
Hanigan (Manly 8.C., Sydney) says, “ You might just as
well play on the road. The clubs are all at loggerheads ;
there is no association, consequently our welcome in
England has been far different from what we expected.”
This is the biggest set-back of all, for the team went away
dreaming of junketing and picnics galore, with a sort of
open house right through the land, securing the maxi-
mum of joy at the minimum of expense.
* * * *
“No ‘lambed-down ’ shearer” (writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent) “ was ever chucked
out of ‘ The Traveller’s Rest ’ into the dusty road in a
limp heap as the Maorilanders have been shot out of the
Antipodean bowling team. They refused to fall in line
with their Austral brothers and sling in their bit to the
manager’s expenses, so the past participle came into play
and they were ‘ slung.’ Looks as if the Meat-freezers
were becoming thoroughly Seddonized, and the whole
Southern Pacific will be necessary to reduce their
ballooned heads."

Amateur Aus. golf supremacy, to be contested
on Australian G.O. Links (Sydney), this Thursday and
Friday, drew 23 entrants, all Australian top-notchers, bar
the Geelong player and ex-British premier (Mr. P. O.
Anderson), Mr. Arthur Duncan (the M.L. champion), and
Mr. Balfour Melville (an ex-owner of the coveted distinc-
tion). Present holder (Mr. Whyte) will also compete ;
also Mr. Gillies (M.L.), who has a win of the title on
Bondi (Sydney) links to his credit.
• » * «
A team from U.S. cruiser Brooklyn romped
over a N.S.W. baseball team on polling-day. The
Sydney men, who numbered among them M. A.
Noble, Jansen, Diamond, and other good cricketers, quite
equalled their opponents in fielding, but had a lot to
learn in base running. The “ diving ” for bases when
four or five yards off by the'tars was astonishing. But
the match was won by the “pitcher” of the Brooklyn
team. His command over the ball was a revelation.
Talk of Noble “breaking in the air”! In baseball the
ball is delivered on the full, yet the pitcher could swerve
the ball apparently at will. Also, he made it “droop,”
or fall lower to the striker than its apparent motion
would lead him to expect. He disposed of 15 batsmen
before a decent hit was made. Good pitchers command
high salaries in the States, and after seeing the Brooklyn
man one can easily understand it.

Sydney’s big football attraction to-day is the
annual opening of inter-State Rugby matches between
Q. and N.S.W. Choice of N.S.W. team has raised the
usual howl, but writer thinks it a fair selection. . Mac-
kenzie, Finlay, Conlon, Beaumont, Averne, Lamb, and
Garry are inter-State reps, for the first time. Four rural
players have been included—one of them (West) operated
against Rev. Mullineux’s English combination in a test
match two years since. Finlay (half-back with pugilist
Jack Conlon) is only 18, but his selection is in every way
warranted by his excellent shaping during “ country
week.” Wet weather will probably bring about eleventh-
hour changes in several positions.
» * * •
Personnel of Bananaland inter-State toe-ball

M’Cowan, Hindmarsh and Boland.

“ Scrum ” : Usual grumblings at team selected
to represent N.S.W. v. Queensland on 13th June. South
Sydney, at present leading for first-grade premiership,
has not a solitary representative. Critics object most to
M’Kenzie, the ’Varsity man, as full-back, and White (N.
Sydney) as three-quarter. Though quite possible that
the selected team will win, and the men objected to
distinguish themselves, the selection committee might
have strained a point and included a pla3’er from the
leading club.
* * * *
Saturday’s round of Sydney senior Rugby was
decided in rain and mud. South Sydney and Eastern
Suburbs and ’Varsity and North Sydney matches ended
in pointless draws. Previous meeting between the first-
named teams brought a 1 point win (9 to 8) to South
Sydney. Glebe downed Western Suburbs (5 to nil), but
only after a tough struggle all the journey. Newtown
romped away from Balmain, and finished 2 against
nothing.
Last ’Ome mail told how Towns had given up
hope of meeting Gaudaur (a match between them
has now been made) and issued a sweeping chal-
lenge to any other scullers in the world, particularly the
’Frisean Harry Petersen, who had just reached England.
Petersen’s record embraces one victory and one loss with
Gaudaur —the latter by a few feet only. The ’Murkan
was after Britisher Sam Emmett, but Towns chipped in
with an offer to give the ’Ome product lOsec. start over
the Thames championship course. M.L. sculler Sullivan
(Towns’s particular pal) waits for either Emmett or Peter-
sen if they don’t relish Towns.
* * * •
But it has just been cabled that the Canadian
and New South Welshman have been matched,
and will row on the Lake of the Woods at the Canuck’s
retreat, Rat Portage, Ontario, during’ the third week of
August. Safe to assume that Towns has given way to
Gaudaur in everything rather than let Jake get off. Gau-
daur insisted that the stake money should be held by a
Rat Portage bank—which, until Gaudaur mentioned it,
English pressmen were absolutely ignorant of. The
course (with a turn) is over a sheet of water that would,
under other circumstances, be among the last places
chosen for the decision of the world’s sculling supremacy.
Conditions are in many ways against the Australian, but
given a fair deal, the title that was Trickett’s, Beach’s, and
Searle’s, looks a good thing for Towns, provided that the
match eventuates, which is not certain yet if the money
be held by Gaudaur’s people.
* * * *
“Chip”: Gaudaur-Towns sculling-match may
not prove so soft a snap for Towns as some think. The
French-Canadian is 44 and has followed the callings of
miner, hunter, farmer, and seaman—the sort of life likely
to keep all his physical powers unimpaired for a year or
so vet. Towns must journey 1200 miles by rail after
reaching Montreal, and then row on a lake with one or,
perhaps, two turns, which is a practice Yankee and
Canuck scullers are adepts at, while it will be new to
Towns. Toronto press, discussing the probabilities of a
match, assert that Gaudaur is still as good a man as w'hen
he met Bill Beach in ’B6. That was a memorable race.
At the last moment Beach had his “ work ” in the boat
altered, with the result that after showing Gaudaur the
W'ay for two miles he was pumped out, and the Canadian
passed him. By sheer force of will-power Beach came
again, and Gaudaur responded as best he could. First
one would show in front and then the other. Excite-
ment reached white heat. The Australian stopped twice
but lay-down to it afresh each time, and ultimately
barely won one of the most gruelling struggles ever con-
tested. Odds of 5 to 1 were laid on Beach at the start,
and 20 to 1 on Gaudaur by the time 2J miles were
covered.
• * * *
Kaffir Essler and Melbourne “feather’' Silver
supplied vimful bruising at Sydney Golden Gate A.C.,
Saturday night. Essler was aggressive, and though Silver
jigged energetically to dodge rushes and swings, he
could not avoid stopping a few. Up to the seventh round
the colored “corner” was jubilant, but thence forth
their weighty underlips fell, as it became painfully plain
that Essler couldn’t sustain the pace. Appeals against
Silver for alleged hitting in clinches were frequent, and
the referee once cautioned him to desist. “ The Kaffir ”
was sprawlingall over the ring in the 16th round— dead and
done, but he faced up for the next and, half way through,
appealed vainly for a foul, and refused to go on, so Silver
got the money. Hitting and clinching that Silver was
accused of are no offence against M.Q. rules. He didn’t
hold and hit, but simply punched directly the clinch was
broken. Latter day referees’ absurd command “ break
clean ! ” is responsible for the false notion chat hitting at
close quarters is foul work. It’s the manner of fighting
allowed in Victoria, and it’s the right and the best style
too.
* m * *
Herb. M‘Kell, fresh from S’Africa, and Snowy
Sturgeon will meet at the Gaiety A.O. (Sydney) Monday
night. Probably Snowy' will find Herb, his toughest
customer, and one he can’t work those wrenching,
wrestling dodges on without risking being hurt himself.
Conditions exact that if either exceeds 10.7 he shall
forfeit a tenner. It’s certain M‘Kell won’t exceed the
weight, and, on appearances, it’s equally certain that
Sturgeon will; latter also may be many pounds over, for
which big advantage a tenner is a trifling sum to pay.
What was M'Kell doing when he made the match thus-
wise?
• • • »
The Sturgeon crowd recently professed anxiety
for a third match with Otto Cribb, and talked hundreds,
but when the Cribbs put up the coin ’twas all over.
There wasn’t a Sturgeon man in sight. This scribe won’t
have Sturgeon against Cribb any r more. Their first fight
was as hot a thing as the Sydney ring ever saw, but
Snowy made the second a mere sprinting match. Stur-
geon’s latest form wouldn’t justify anyone risking money
in backing him to do what he couldn’t when he seemed a
better man.
* * * *
Famous pugilist Jackson is just barely holding
his own at Roma (Q.). Weight has decreased since his
arrival, but not during the last fortnight. Taken all
round, indications point to an alleviation of suffering.
Digestion is better, cough relieved, and he can now sleep
at night. If he can be kept so during the colder months,
Dr. Le Strange may be able to assist him to some benefit
during the spring and summer.
» * * *

they can lave me out av their reckonin’ altogether. O’im
not afther will-o-the-whisps these toimes.” Ryan and
No'an would put up just the sort of bullocking scraps
that miners glory in.

Selections from the printed electoral
address of Geo. Howarth, the Lee party’s selected
candidate for Willoughby (N.S.W.) : —
The increase in population during the time I have re-
presented the electorate has been shown by the constant
addition to the electoral roll. When I was first returned
the number of electors was 2300 ; now it is 4000. A
better criterion, however, is the census, which gives an
increase of 130 per cent. The increase in population must
of necessity have increased. . . . Now, as I worked in the
past to secure the satisfaction of the wants of the people,
so I shall work in the future, and when I have deter-
mined a work it shall be carried out, the easiest plan is
to carry it out with as little trouble as possible.
And he was actually returned.
Two days before the N. S. W. State elections
Syd. Teoegraph came out with a fearful sectarian
bellow, and put mustard on the tail of the Yellow
Pup to rouse him to the last extreme of fury. It
gave about a column to the report of an Orange
Service, and decorated it with big cross-headings :
“A Warning” ; “Too Much Romanism” ;
“Advice to Protestant Electors”; “Romanism
in the Government” ; “A Scandal and a Shame”;
“ Protestants should Rule,” and the like. And the
Yellow Pup promptly slewed round and bit his
owner. The Lee party appealed to King
Division, its old Freetrade stronghold, on the
Freetrade ticket, and King Division threw out
the Lee party’s R.C. candidate and put in the See
party’s Protestant representative instead.
Unique Medical Discovery.
In the year 1879 it was first made known to the
world that a certain medicinal combination of
vegetable extracts possessed such a remarkable
therapeutic action upon the kidneys and liver that
any disease arising from a deranged condition of
these vital organs would speedily yield to its
influence, and the kidneys and liver become again
perfectly healthy.
When it is understood that rheumatism, gout,
neuralgia, biliousness, jaundice, indigestion, dys-
pepsia, gravel, anaemia, debility, most blood
disorders and Bright’s disease all arise from im-
purities retained m the blood, owing to an in-
efficient action of the kidneys or liver, or both, it
will be realized how important such a discovery
is. Warner’s Safe Cure is the name given to this
medicine, and since its introduction thousands of
persons are now in the enjoyment of health, who
would otherwise have gone through life martyrs
to one or other of these complaints.
2 Dunn-street,
Richmond, Vio.,
28th March, 1901.
Dear Sirs, —
I have decided to send you a few lines in refer-
ence to the great benefit I derived from taking
Warner’s Safe Cure.
For some years I have been gradually but
steadily declining in health ; I was subject to
severe pains in various parts of my body, shortness
of breath and palpitation of the heart. I seemed,
indeed, to be on the eve of a constitutional break-
up. I consulted physicians, one of whom pro-
nounced me to be far gone with liver complaint.

“Pyalla” to The Bulletin about the Sydney
Harbor lifeboat (non) service, in connection with
the recent disaster to the steamer Manly at Port
Jackson Heads
The Bulletin has frequently severely criticised the
existing arrangements, and has pointed out the urgent
necessity for the establishment of a really efficient ser-
vice. Special reference was made, about 10 years back, to
the wreck of the Centurion barque, off North Head. On
that occasion a lifeboat crew could not be obtained until
some Artillerymen from the local battery volunteered.
These did their best, but, from lack of experience, had
to put back. The crew of the barque was meanwhile
rescued by the pilot-steamer, but, nevertheless, the use-
lessness of the “volunteer” system was powerfully
demonstrated. Yet the authorities made no change.
As regards the recent Manly episode, much abuse
has been lavished upon what is called “ the life-
boat crew.” Indeed, Captain Edie, Superintendent
of Navigation, said, according to the Sydney Daily
Telegraph that “ the only question to inquire into is
why the crew failed to respond to the signal when called
upon.” Now, who are the crew ? Does Captain Edie
know ? He simply cannot, because, as a matter of fact,
there is no crew. The “ system ” (save the mark !) is this.
At Camp Cove, near Watson’s Bay, is housed an
antiquated so-called lifeboat (design of 25 years back)
which isn’t even “ self-righting” and has scarcely room
for the crew—to say nothing of rescued passengers. And
the supposed crew of this antique argosy consists really—
by way of nucleus—of a coxswain, who receives about
5s. a week as caretaker and repairer as well as coxswain.
As for the other 12 men required to man the boat, the
expectation is that when, at intervals, a signal is made
for a crew, 12 anybodies will turn up for a wage of 10s.
each. So, of course, immediately the gun is fired (for
practice only), there is a wild rush for the half-sovs. by
men (and boys) of various degrees of efficiency, who
scramble, and sometimes fight, for seats in the boat.
As the coxswain is an excitable and only partly intel-
ligible Portuguese, what ensues is generally a “ survival
of the fittest.” Present writer knoivs, because he has
personally witnessed the “circus.” Also, this little ex-
periment, besides occurring only at very long intervals,
is generally made in fine weather, and in daylight.
As for the signal-gun, which mostly fails to go off
when required, it is an 18th - century piece of
ordnance, which nobody appears to know how to dis-
charge, even if the key of the powder magazine is obtain-
able. And, in any case, when wind and sea are raging,
the miserable pop-gun can’t be heard. Captain Edie, in
blaming the crew, followed out the official traditions of
passing on the blame to subordinates ; though, as it hap-
pened, in this instance he landed nowhere and on nobody.
What is needed is a picked crew of men living close at
hand, who should be paid, say, £12 a year each, and
should be required to practise at least once a month, and,
when possible, in darkness and storm. This is the custom
elsewhere, and would certainly long ago have been the
system here but that Sydney Harbour has hitherto
suffered from too much safety. ' No East of England port,
for instance, could have tolerated such despicable arrange-
ments for a single month.
+
Accidentally drowned, on Saturday night
last, at Milson’s Point, North Sydney, journalist
Charles Junor. Mr. Junor, who was for some
time connected with the laboratory-department of
Sydney University, was a fairly frequent con-
tributor to The Bulletin, and had latterly been
concerned in a Press Agency business. He was—
though somewhat irascible in discussion —a real
good fellow, and his sudden taking-off in the
prime of life (37) is sincerely regretted by a large
number of friends.
The pathetic end of one of M'Douall
Stuart’s first exploring party, told in his last
letter: —
Wandi (Northern Territory), May 17. Dear Steve—No
doubt you will be surprised to find my corpse to-morrow
morning. I must tell you that I am tired of life. I do
not know that I can get anything to do. I am getting too
old to work and am too proud to beg. If an investigation
should occur you will state that drink had nothing to do
with this affair. I have taken the loan of your pistol to
do the deed. I am sane at this moment." I pray that
God will forgive me. This is the end of 35 years’ ex-
perience in the N.T. Good-bye to all friends, and may
God bless you.—Yours, &e., J. A. Ewart.

When the recent census was taken in Ire-
land, many R.O.’s showed their indignation at
the Coronation Declaration against Transubstan-
tiation by stating their religion as “Idolatry.”
Even N.S. W. Prisons Department is find-
ing itself compelled to move with the times. It
i arted some time ago with its incredibly brutal,
but dearly-loved, gag ; and now the inhuman dark-
cell system has been abolished, while the still
more iniquitous solitary confinement is to have
its term reduced—for first offenders—from nine
months to one month ; the period for pre-
viously-convicted persons being made vari-
able according to the individual case. Better
late than never, certainly — but what a
multitude of victims this solitary-confinement
fetish has already claimed ! How many persons—
gaoled for the first time and belonging in no real
sense to the criminal classes bave by this accursed
“separate-treatment” been driven demented, or
practically slain ! But even these unfortunates
will not have perished in vain if a tardy public
recognition of the fact that they did so perish is
helping to make the barbarous Botany Bay
Tradition pc • ■'-d>le by the light of rational
humanitari r ; sm.
Gilmour, the mate of burglars Mont-
gomery and Williams (hanged at Sydney for
using their jemmies—though without fatal effect
—on the arresting policemen), got clear away at
the time of the memorable robbery at the Union
s.s. Co.’s Sydney office, but has fallen in at last.
He lately killed a Frenchwoman whose house in
Paris he was burgling, and is very likely indeed to
be guillotined.

Pis an acknowledged fact the world-wide over that no
single individual or combination of men can be ab-
solute masters of every phase an . branch of art, science,
medicine, or other professed avocation of a limitless
sphere of operation and magnitude. At best the greatest
scientists of the day—men like Lord Kelvin and Edison,
Tours truly . . .
HOWARD FIVE KM AN,
Sydney’s Leading Specialist.
Professor of Electricity as applied Medically, Member
Dr. Harmon's College, Washington Cor., Cal., TJ.S.A.
Director and Founder of the Institute.
for instance, whose fame is universally known—class
themselves as infants in scientific invention and research,
and modestly tell us that they have but a smattering of
knowledge of the possibilities, extent, and development
We have only to look at the fine buildings that occupy
the corner of Elizabeth and Bathurst streets to gain some
idea of the extent and importance of their professional
work. But to have the privilege of being shown through
the building by the Specialists themselves, and to see and
learn something of the real character of their wide prac-
tice, is a treat that only comes the way of a fortunate in-
dividual like the writer. .
The instrument-room was, as usual, one of particular
curiosity. Laymen always have a penchant for eyeing
and examining the polished steel articles with which so
many extraordinary acts are performed upon the human
body. Th; collection in this room is in every way up-to-
date. Whenever a new instrument conies to the front,
and its worth is proved beyond question, it is added to
this rare assortment. Another room full of absorbing in-
terest is that devoted to the galvanic batteries, of which
there are several magnificent kinds. The noted Gaitfe
batteries, the finest French makes, are here in perfection,
irrespective of their high cost. If any article or mechani-
cal and electric contrivance is found necessary in main-
taining the fame of this institution, th* price is never
allowed to stand in the way.
The correspondence department is .. veritable eye-
opener. Its sacredness is a bar to a stranger’s admission,
but a full view of the work going on is had through the
glass doors. Half a-dozen typists are seen driving their
machine-writers at electric speed. To the principals only
are known the names of the persons throughout Australia
who are being treated as skilfully in their homes about
the metropolis and away in the interior as if they were
present in personal consultation at the institution.
The packing-rooms are likewise shut off from curious
and prying glances, and the work of one department is a
mystery to the others. No better system for holding
secret the nature of sufferers’ troubles, tor safe-guarding
their interests, and giving due heed to the feelings and
sensitiveness of patients, could be imagined. Packages
are not plastered with advertising labels, they are put up
in such a way that only the names and addresses are in
view.
What a practice is here revealed and what a consola-
tion must it be to these eminent specialists to know that
they are permanently curing and restoring to health and
happiness thousands of people whose lives were a burden
to them. The young, the middle-aged, and the healthy
are treated with the same amount of success. The pro-
digious nature of the work accomplished by these
specialists necessitates —not the stereotyped morning and
evening two hours’ consultation ticket, but their constant
attention at the institution from early morning till late
into the night. “I call assure you,” said Dr. Wallace,
“ we don’t put in these long hours because we are fonder
'
'
x&x
Premises = Gor. Elizabeth and Bathurst Sts (Opp. Hyde Park, Sydney
(All Trams Stop at the Door).
of electricity, and only a fringe of imagination of the un-
told stores of strength and wealth wrapt up in this
phenomenal power.
Therefore it requires no argument to convince the
most pronounced of optimists that the field in any one of
the great and honored professions of the day is far too
vast for the thorough comprehension of the most per-
fectly brain-endowed man, or body of naturally-gifted
men. Yet in respect of the most delicate and difficult of
all professions—that of prescribing for the ills and
diseases of mankind—we find doctors, scattered over the
globe, off-handedly and without careful, minute, and
searching examination and diagnosis—without any know-
ledge of the past lives of patients—flippantly dashing off
prescriptions for every class of complaint, as if a cure was
as simple as falling over the proverbial log.
Thanks to the march of civilisation, culture, and edu-
cation, people are getting to know that modern medical
men who are worth consulting confine their practice to a
certain class of cases. Realising the utter hopelessness of
mastering and curing everything under the sun, and find-
ing that “gullibles” are not so plentiful as they used to
be, the wise and progressive men of medicine devote
themselves to one or other class of cases, and work out
the remedies for them to the best of their skill and
ability.
What better and more striking illustration of the suc-
cess of Specialists in Skin Diseases, Heart, Kidney, Liver
Affections, Rheumatics, Sciatica, Nervous Diseases, could
the people of this magnificent continent, Australia, have
than the enormous practice of the famous practitioners of
the Electro-Medical Institute. Their skill, attainments,
position, and renowned reputation stamp them as the un-
doubted masters of fhe classes of diseases and complaints
which have been the study of their professional lives.
When we speak of electricity we think of Edison. Why ?
Because he dwarfs other men in this branch of science.
In the same way, when we talk of specialists, we at once
associate the names of the Freeman and Wallace Insti-
tute. So it is in every branch of. scientific medicine;
some one or two men stand head and shoulders above
their fellows.
It is the aim, object, and devotion of certain doctors to
be pre-eminent in some defined class of disease rather
than to tinker with a host of complaints about which
they can have but a hazy notion, and superficial know-
ledge. To use a hackneyed but expressive phrase, they
are “Jacks of all trades and masters of none.” Never
since the time of Ricord, the noted French Specialist,
have any men conducted so gigantic and successful a
practice as that cone acted by the Freeman-W'allace
Electro Medical and Surgical Institute.
of hard work than any other medical men ; we do it in
the best interests of sufferers. We don’t keep them
waiting- longer than we can help, and we suffer many in-
conveniences for our patients’ sakes.”

for his departure, and the Specialists returning to their

same.

At St. Arnaud (Vic.), t’other day, a little
girl was arrested for stealing some pork, and it
was proved in the police court that she was one of
five children whose mother, a widow, had been
gaoled for selling wine without a license. When
the mother was convicted it was pointed out that
she could not pay the fine, £25, and if sent to
gaol her children would be left entirely helpless.
She was sent to gaol, and the children were quite
forgotten till the eldest stole the pork in a pitiful
attempt to feed her starving sisters and brothers.
Law has a forcible method of inculcating virtue
amongst the lower orders.
Lucknow, 14th June, 1901.
g J. POLLITZER, Esq., Sydney.

have knocked through from the D’Arcy to the Proprietary

“ Cockney ” : Susie Vaughan’s coming with the Brough
O. sets one thinking of old times when she was one of
the four Sisters Vaughan—golden-haired Kate being the
“star"—who danced the most fetching and artistic can-
can that Condon had ever seen. Susie was an unmistak-
able sister of Kate, and the other two members of the
quartette were said to be also in the family. They danced
as though they loved it. Saw them first at the old Surrey
Gardens. Then Chatterton, the lessee of Drury Lane,
heard of them, and gave the slim, tall sisters an engage-
ment to do a turn after the spectacular drama, which was
an annual autumn-to-winter production at the Lane in
those days. As the result of the Drury Lane success Kate
Vaughan went on her own, and became the dancing idol
of Gaiety Burlesque, what time Royce and Edward Terry
(with Nellie Farren, of course,) were the other principals,
and Maud Hobson, the handsomest of the chorus girls,
was unknown by name except to the initiated. Susie
Vaughan dropped out of notice for a while after the dis-
bandment of the sisters, but presently she came to light
in small comedy parts ; and now—here she is with the
Brough co.
The Gearl of Yarmouth, as plaintiff in a libel action
against a New York journal (he landed £5OO damages),
was cross-examined re the “ decency ” of his skirt-dancing
in Australia. Poor young Gearl! His antics were often
absurd, but never objectionable. As for his Serpentine
dance, it was an interesting illustration of the fact that a
tall, thin young man, armed with a couple of sticks and
arrayed in gauzy draperies and a flaxen wig, could make
a more effective flutter than the ordinary female who
performs the same easy trick. Yarmouth had another
negative virtue. He said of himself that he supposed he
was “ a bit of a joke.”
A Phil May was announced to open “ a season of musical
and lyric entertainments ” at the Bechstein Hall, London,
on June 15th. It can’t be that our Phil May has taken to
that sort of thing.
Editor Bulletin— Apropos your recent critique on my
performance with the Empire 00. at the Empire Theatre,
I wish to remark that the falling of the lady off the bell
was quite unusual, and occurred only once during my
engagement with Mr. Fuller; it happened the identical
night your writer “Happened to be there.” I did not
wish to appear that night as I was suffering from the
result of a sprain occasioned on the previous Saturday ;
so it was only because I did not desire to break faith with
the public that I went through my performance. In
regard to the quivering of the muscles, that is done inten-
tionally, and if your scribe knew anything of muscular
development he should be aware that that particular part
of the exhibition is very difficult and only to be attained by
hard and rigid training. In regard to the last sentence —
would that my crust were earned as easily as your
reporter’s.— Herr Pagel.
War-correspondent Macdonald is on his way back from
London, frozen- out by the cold. In a private letter, he
says it was possible to keep out of the way of the shells
in the early stages of the Ladysmith siege, and to get
used to them in the later stages, but he could do neither
the one nor the other with the cold of England. After
making an encouraging start with his lecturing tour, he
had to abandon it to get back to his native land to thaw.
Some recent operatic happenings in Brisbane. First,
in “ Faust,” a trap failed to work, so that Mephisto
couldn’t descend and was hailed by a small boy in the
family circle with : “ What ho, Nick ! Have they shut
your own door in your face? ” Next, in “ Carmen,” one
of the ponies engaged in the bull-fight scene refused to
go off and had to be bodily humped away by six supers.
Again, in “Lohengrin,” at the end.of one of the acts, the
curtain was raised and revealed an attendant disrobing
one of the female songsters.
An Argus paragrapher has discovered that Bernard
Shaw was once a pugilist. There is a very early novel of
his lurking in the back shelves of the second-hand book-
shops, called “ Oashell Byron’s Profession,” the hero of
which is a prize-fighter ; but Shaw was never more than
an elegant amateur. He learned to put his ha_nds_up,
considering pugilism an accomplishment essential in a
dramatic critic, and that was about all.

Madame Tussaud’s Waxworks, in London, adver-
tises as its principal attraction a “ Lifelike portrait-model
of Commandant Christian De Wet.”
John Anderson, the lately-deceased member for East
Melbourne, was, in his young days, a stonemason and an
amateur actor—most unusual combination. His theatrical
tendencies shaped his course in life. Anderson took to
the hotel business and flourished.
Handsome Miss Elliott Page, assisted by Julius Knight
and others, tried a Danish play—“ The Jensen Family ”
at a recent matinee in London. The play was spoken of
as an unpleasantly foolish story, having a showy central
character for Miss E.P. She took the part of a beautiful
wench, living with her impecunious parents, and rever-
ently beloved by the lodger, the one admirer who didn’t
make improper proposals to her. After a while her dis-
gust for the proposing sex prompted her to sell herself to
a married barrister, who put her upon the variety stage
and made her fortune. Then the heroine turned up
again, like Magda, in the old home, and every-
body, except her young sister, was glad to see
her on account of her splendid jewellery and things.
She provided the family with a desirable mansion
to live in, and arot rid of her benefactor, the married bar-
rister, on hearing that the man had lost all his money.
Also she scared her good sister’s fiance off the premises by
appearing in the drawing-room in tights, after which she
herself married the lodger of her innocent days. The
lodger was still devoted to the lady, and he couldn't
afford any scruples. These modern advanced stage
heroines, who go wrong and prosper, and return to their
unsophisticated parents, seem to be more inhumanly im-
possible than Formosa in Boucicault’s cheerful old melo-
drama, which was adapted more or less from the French,
and which gave H. A. Jones the main idea for “The
Dancing Girl.” £


Found by Accident.
THE GRIP OF LA GRIPPE.
The Expulsion of the Enemy.
The Case of
Mr. ALEXANDER HISLOP.
(For the "News.”)
of the best known chefs in New
oouth Wales is Mr. Alexander Hislop,
residing at No. 34 Burton-street, Darling-
hurst. That gentleman has been engaged
as chief cook in some of the leading hotels
and restaurants in Sydney, and also in
several important country towns in the
mother colony. His popularity was account-
able for our representative hearing of a
visitation of illness whilst at business in
Albury, the details of which were supplied
in the course of the following interview :
“A few years ago,” began Mr. Hislop,
“I was at work on the Albury railway
station, and whilst there I had a very severe
attack of influenza. It came on like an
ordinary cold, and I treated it lightly for
some time ; but it eventually got me down
altogether. first indications were a
tingling, dry sense of fullness in the nose,
and I used to sneeze nearly the whole day.
The pains in my head I shall never forget-
partfcularly across my forehead. 1 could
nob breathe through my nose at all, and my
eyes yere red and watery. Thiough a
harassing dry cough, which came on at
regular intervals, my throat got very sore.
My lungs were quite clogged up with the
cold, and I became so hoarse that I could
not make my voice heard. I was unfortunate
in having aching pains all over my body,
but intensely acute in my back and legs I
was never free from thirst, being always
feverish. My temperature increased every
day, and caused me no little alarm
Whenever I was exposed to air a little
cooler than what I was accustomed to, I felt
flashes of heat and chills one after the other.
I was always wanting to get near the fire.
My windpipe was inflamed and seemed
swollen, and provoked considerable pain.
During the first stages of my attack I forced
myself to eat a little food, although my sore
throat made the task a difficult one ; but in
a few days all appetite had forsaken me
entirely, and I could only drink large
quantities of water. It was a risky and
foolhardy thing for me to do, bub many a
night I got out of bed and tramped the
station platform from end to end because I
was too restless and feverish to sleep.
Luckily for myself I have a constitution
like a horse, or else those walks in search
•f relief would have caused my death. I
was too weak to stay out long, and on
returning to bed I had to lie in a feverish,
burning state till morning, As the disease
laid a firmer hold upon me I had water
running from the nose and eyes, and my
cough became a little more moist. There
was also a slight discharge from my throat.
My pulse had attained a rapidity which I
did not think possible, and I was so reduced
m flesh that the complicated symptoms
occasiondme distressing forebodings.”
“ What did your doctor say ? ” asked the
repeater.
.-® e said I had influenza in its most
serious form. I came down to Sydney for
treatment, and remained three weeks here.
During those three weeks I took every care
of myself, with a view to getting better as
soon as possible. My doctor’s -directions
were closely attended to, and I did every-
thing that common sense could suggest to
assist in my recovery, j I did not waste one
teaspoonful of his medicine all the time;
but at the end of the third week I thought I
might just as well suffer at Albury as in
Sydney. My d—tor had not given me any
relief with his physic, so I went back again,
feeling every bit as bad as when I went
away. I thought I would never be well
again, so depressed and low-spiiited had I
got. However, on returning to the country
I engaged a furnished house, away from the
noise of the station, as I was too worn out
and irritable to stand it, and I made up my
mind to lie up till I was either killed or
cured.” . ,
“And what sort of progress did you
make ? ”
“ I made very rapid progress indeed.*
The taking of that furnished house was the
luckiest thing I have ever done in my life.
Through an oversight on the part of the
previous occupier, a bottle of Clements
Tonic was left on one of the mantelpieces,
and I read the label. ‘Everything else has
failed to cure me, so I will give this a trial,’
I said, and right glad lam that I did. A
few doses eased my chest of a thick, yellow
phlegm, and my breathing became much
easier. The soreness of my throat was
cured simultaneously with my cough, and I
soon had a longing for food, which 1 ate with
a relish. The splitting frontal headaches
went as quickly as they came, and the
pains all over my body left me as I daily
grew stronger. I could walk about without
feeling as though my legs would collapse
under me, and without feeling light headed
or giddy. My eyes, which had looked so
bleary red and w.'tery for weeks, now
looked clear and bright. As I continued to
get along so well, I knew I had hold of the
right remedy, so I sent to the local store for
a couple more bottles of Clements Tonic.
By the time they were exhausted I had not
a sign of influenza about me. I could eat
well, sleep well, and work well. I had not
been able to smoke for weeks, but now I
could enjoy a pipe as much as anybody. I
do not know what would have become of me
at that time if it had not been for Clements
Tonic, as it cured me of the most severe
attack of influenza when my doctor and his
medicine had failed.” ~.«s»*. -
“Do you ever feel any ill-effects of your
illness now?”
“No;none whatever. But I frequently
work long hours at a stretch, and get a
little run-down. I am never without a
bottle of Clements Tonic in the house, so I
soon pick myself up again.” ~
“Do you consent to your experience
being made known?” %-••**• **•-
“Most certainly. I tell all my friends
what I am indebted to for my present good
health, and you are quite welcome to publish
my case in any way you like.”

Dear Bulletin, —You say of Pastor Fit-
chett’s “Wellington’s Men ” (8., 15/6/’01); “It
is the personal, dramatic, and literary value of
these accounts that Mr. Fitchett has exploited . .
contributions to knowledge of the human animal
in warfare. They are, in fact, contributions to
human psychology.” Granted ; but let the picture
of “the human animal in warfare” be complete.
Why this perpetual repetition of battle-pictures,
when we know that battles are only occasional
episodes, with, between them, long months of
marches, bivouacs, picquet, and guard-mounting ?
Let Mr. Fitchett give not merely the high lights,
but something, say, like the “Conscript” of
Erekmann-Chatrian. There are plenty of materials.
Let him “analyse the human animal in warfare”
by all means ; but let him take the animal under
all the conditions of warfare —suffering want, and
oppressed with toil, as well as when warmed up
in battle. The pictures of enthusiasts like Fit-
chett are absurdly untrue to nature. They are
not studies from life, but copies ; and bad copies,
despite the technical skill expended on them. —
Veteean.
A Narrabri (N.S.W.) firm has imported a
three-barrelled gun. Two barrels are for shot,
and the other, for bullets, is fixed beneath the
first two.
Heard, recently, at a Sydney debating
society: “A man may overcome great natural
disadvantages. For instance, I have heard of a
case where a man born without arms learned to
write a beautiful hand with his feet.”
Decided in an English Appeal Court (prac-
tically) that a man may break open a box and take
letters belonging to his wife, if he supposes them
to show (and they did) an improper intimacy be-
tween her and the writer. The lady had plenty
of nerve, and fought the matter right through.
First, in the police-court, for stealing ; charge
dismissed. Then before a Judge, who ordered the
return of the letters. Finally the Superior Court
refused to let either party have them, and handed
them to the Divorce authorities ; so the husband
finally scored. Presumably this decision cuts
both ways.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
After dinner. Dave and Joe at the barn waiting
for the horses to finish feeding. Dave sitting
with his back to the slabs, his hands embracing
his knees, staring thoughtfully from under his old
felt hat at the stubble-field, upon which a million
grasshoppers sported themselves in the scorching,
simmering heat. Joe lying flat on his stomach,
supporting his chin in his palms, digging earth up
with the toes of his boots, and dexterously spit-
ting at ants that passed within range.
“ Don’t know wot you think,” Dave said,
gloomily, “ but it ain’t good enough for me. I’ve
told the old chap, too, over and over again, that I
won’t stand it any longer. Slog away as much as
y’ like, an’ look after things all the year, then
when y’ want a few bob you’ve to ask him for it ”
(a pause); “an’ when y’ do ask he growls like a
bear ! ”
“That’s so,” Joe said.
“Y’d think,” Dave continued, “a feller was
on’y a kid, th’ way
Just then Dad appeared, using strong language
on Cranky Jaok, for heaving a shovel at one of
the mares.
“Notyoked-up yet?” he growled; “y’ll tek
all day, soon ! ”
“ Oh, give th’ horses time to finish I ” Dave said,
sharply.
“ Time t’ finish 1 ” Dad snorted; “ time for y’
to yarn and idle!”
Dave fixed his eyes on Dad.
“Wot ’re y’ talkin’ about ? ” he said.
“ Wot’m I damn you, feller, will y’ sit there
givin’ impudence t’ me very face ? Get up, and
go on with th’ ploughin’ tli’ two of y’ ! ”
“ Not another turn ’ll I do,” Dave said, the tears
starting into his eyes; “not anotherd—n stroke !
... Y’ ain’t satisfied mooching round the place
all morning, pokin’ in everybody’s road, but y’
must come here ’n’ meddle with things y’ know
nothing about ”
“Know nothing about?” and Dad shook with
rage.
“ Yes ; know.nothing about.”
“ Confound you, you insolent (Dad was lost for
a word) —Clear out of this— clear!” And up
went his big right hand, after the manner of a
railway-guard.
“No need t’ tell me,” Dave said; “ I’ve stayed
here too long as it is—for all thet ever I get from
y\”
“Get from me? What th’devil do you want,
you hound ? ”
“ Something more than the few miserable shil-
lings I get once a year.”
“ D—n y’, feller; d’ y’ think I’m a million-
aire ? ”
“No —nor anyone— —”
Here mother came and intervened, and tried to
make peace.
But Dave was determined to leave home. He
went into the house and put on a coat; then he
saddled a horse and rode away. He went as far
as Delaney’s—five miles up.
Dad began to think, and discussed the situation
with mother.
“Well, you know,” said mother, quietly, “the
boys is men now, and I suppose they think as it’s
time they had something to themselves.”
Dad thought some more, then went down to the
17-acres, where a man we had engaged and a boy
called Regan were pulling corn and carting it in.
Dad went over their tracks, and, finding a small
cob with scarcely any corn on it, brought it along
and threw it into the dray, and lectured the man
for missing it.
The man offered no explanation, but young
Regan grinned.
“You imp ! ” Dad yelled, “ get on outer thet;
wot th’ devil ’re y’ standin’ for ? ”
The boy waded-in.
Dad then lent a hand and worked hard. The
dray was nearly full. The man mounted it to
square the load.
“Wot ’re y’ lookin’ at now?” Dad shouted
again to the boy, who. was watching the man on
the dray.
“I’ve finished me side,” whined the boy.
“ Lead th’ horse on, then.”
“How kin I, till”
“ Lead th’ horse on ! ”
The boy ducked in time to dodge a cob Dad
aimed at his head.
“ You whelp! ” and Dad went off round the
dray after him.
Regan dodged. Dad pelted more cobs at him,
and roared: “Stand, you young devil, or I’ll
knock your head off.”
Regan gathered cobs as he ran, and returned
Dad’s fire over the back of the horse.
The man on the dray sank in a lump on the
load and laughed.
Regan left the dray, charged into the corn and
disappeared.
Dad threatened the man on the dray with
violence and the sack, then left and went across
to Cranky Jack and a traveller who was putting
in a day or two for tucker. They were filling the
shed with hay and must have been doing it all
wrong, because as soon as Dad set eyes on it he
started swearing and dancing round.
The traveller stood, holding an immense forkful
of hay above his head, and listened a second or
two; then he said : “Be d dto you !” and
threw all the hay on top of Dad, and smashed the
fork on the ground, and heaved the pieces down
the paddock, and walked off, cursing.
Dad threw the hay off himself and spat and
shouted, “ You coward ! ”
“Go ’way, Boss; go ’way,” Jack said —“an’
don’t insult the gentleman. Jim’s from Ireland.”
Dad scowled at the half-witted man and went
away. He harnessed Dave’s pair of plough-horses
and joined Joe.
“ I’ll show th’ feller he can be done without, I
know,” he said to Joe.
Joe smiled and said: “Giterp ... Jess .. .
Jally.” And when he stopped to clean the plough
again and looked round there was Dad walloping
Dave’s horses with a shovel.
Joe ran back.
“ What th’ deuce is up, n-now ? ” he said.
“ Confound th’ feller,” Dad answered—“he’s
got th’ horses completely spoilt.”
“They’re all right,” Joe said, approaching the
plough-handles.
“They’re not all right—they’re all wrong; stand
aside, sir.” And Dad took the reins again.”
“ Now then,” he shouted, shaking the plough,
“get up !. . . Horses!” They jerked and
swerved, and shoved each other. “ You d——n
rubbish ! . . . Get up 1 ”
“No wonderl” said Joe, making a discovery.
“They’re not in their right places—put th’ b-
black horse in th’ f-furrer.”
“He’ll go where I put him. Get up , . . you
pair o’ dogs ! . . . Gee back . . . way ! way J Wa-
ya! ” Dad dropped the plough-handles, and
slipped up beside the horses, and brought the
shovel down on them again so suddenly that they
both bounded off before Joe could seize the reins,
and the next moment were bolting across the
paddock with the plough flying behind them.
Dad turned to Joe: “Dammit, why couldn’t
y’ take th’ reins ? ”
“W-well,” and Joe grinned, “there’s some
things a feller can’t do.’’
“ There’s a d n lotter things y’ can’t do ! ”
Dad snorted out, and went after the horses. He
found them, and a portion of the plough, stuck in
the fence near the barn, and surrounded by
mother and Sarah and the dogs and the man who
was pulling corn.
Mother wanted to know what had happened,
but Dad was uncommunicative. Next day he
ordered a new plough, and for months afterwards
the black horse hopped about on three legs.
Dave came back after tea, but scarcely looked
at anyone. He rolled some clothes in a blanket,
hung about for a minute or two, as though he felt
sorry, then said “Good-bye” to us all, and went
back to Delaney’s, leaving mother crying on the
verandah.
“Mark my word,” said Dad, pacing up and
down, “he’ll be glad to came back, yet.”
• • * *
Dave wasn’t away a week when everything was
going wrong. Three cows burst on the lucerne ;
a mare and foal lost; the chaff-cutter smashed in
two places, and every ounce of a bullock that Dad
salted went bad, and was thrown out.
* * * *
A hot day at Delaney’s. Dave ploughing. He
had scraped the plough, and was standing,
reflecting. He felt lonely—’twas the first time
he had ever been away—and couldn’t help think-
ing of home and mother —crying on the verandah
—and of Joe and Sarah. “ Poor old Sarah !” he
said aloud, when a form he knew well rode up to
the fence and greeted him cheerfully.
“Hello, Dad ! ” Dave said with a glad grin. It
seemed like old times to Dave to see Dad, though
it was only eight days since they parted.
Dad dismounted and crawled through the
fence.
“ What’s he puttin’ in here ? ” he asked, survey-
ing the ploughed land.
Dave told him. Then there was a dead silence;
even the birds about and the horses’ tails kept
still. Dave played with some mud he had scraped
from the plough ; Dad with his trouser-pocket.
Dad spoke.
“ Better come home, Dave ? ” he said.
“Dunno,” Dave answered, coloring-up and
throwing the mud down.
“ This is no place for you, man ” (a pause) “no
place at all! ”
Dave gazed in silence at his boots.
“If there’s enerthin’ y’ want, say s’, lad ? ” Dad
went on, knowing when he had an advantage.
“ Here,” dragging his hand out of his pocket with
a jerk, “here’s a fi’-pun’-note for y’, nowl . . .
An’ goodness on’y knows if ever y’ want t’ go t’
town, or enerwhere, yer can alez take a day, or two
days, or a week for that matter — can’t yer ? ”
“ Yairs ... I s’pose s’.”
“ Wull; come ’long?”
And Dave came. And two days later Dad called
him a useless dog.
STEELE RUDD.
Hoisting the Flag at Dingo Flat.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dingo school stands a few paces from the winding
main-track to nowhere. The building is an un-
painted weatherboard structure, with here and
there a board loose and hanging down. Over the
door two boards are gone altogether, but a bag
partly stuffed in the opening, the end dangling
inside like a suicided swagman, keeps the wind,
or some of it, out. The only evidence to this
effect is that you meet much wind outside, and
the bag must be the reason why it isn’t all inside.
The chimney, also of weatherboards, bears evi-
dence of sundry accidents in the shape of large
burnt holes. While ample enough _as to size,
it has the disadvangage of being useless
unless the wind is from the south. When the
wind is from any other direction the smoke inside
school gets so dense that either the fire or the scholars -
have to go out. Some Samaritan has started to
line the internal walls with bags, bub tiring, or
running out of bags, has left it unfinished.
Months ago the scholars were let loose on the
neighboring selectors with subscription-lists to
get money to buy a flag to hoist when the Duke
arrived.
Tuesday was the day appointed for the cere-
tnony, but the teacher had not got back from his
holiday, so Thursday (Dingo is a half-time school)
saw the scholars, eight in number, marshalled in
line ready to cheer. It is a dull, cold, grey day,
and eight insufficiently-clad children, one with no
boots, stand shivering in the raw air. The
teacher, a tall, thin, bony, elderly man, with
stooping shoulders and a chronic cough, holding
in his hand the long-expected flag, stands at the
staff, a long stringy-bark sapling, the bark
stripped off and lying in a yellow heap
on the ground, and tells of The Glory
and the Grandeur of the Empire on which the sun
never sets, and explains, in a dreary, nasal voice,
punctuated by coughs, how the Empire should be
loved and its rulers revered. The children shuffle
and shiver; the flag is run-up, and hangs de-
jectedly ; there is a feeble whisper of a cheer, and
one little girl waves her father’s red handkerchief
violently. The rest stare at a passing bullock-
team crawling along the track, and industriously
count the bullocks —fourteen and a loose one fol-
lowing. A little boy breaks away from the others,
dodges through the ferns, and “peppers” the
passing team with stones. The teacher sighs and
goes inside.
On enquiring why the flag was hoisted, I am told
—because the Duke is here. A further enquiry as
to who the Duke is, meets with the reply that
“ He’s the man who made the war.” Well, per-
haps he is ! T don’t remember myself. Facts
appear to slide off your mind at Dingo.
FRAULEIN.
The imported Inspector-General of Wes-
tralian schools is encouraging a new idea —the
interchange of letters between the top-class pupils
in some of his schools and the ditto in English
schools.
It is about time the N.S.W. Attorney-
General put a stop to trivial cases coming before a
judge. It took a judge, several barristers and
lawyers, a dozen jurymen, and Heaven knows
how many policemen, a whole day, recently, to
decide whether two boys had stolen a shilling’s
worth of corks found in their possession.
