The Hunger-Track.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ They are praying for rain in the farthest West,
and grass is scarce as can be ”
And the daily paper then goes on to news from
over the sea:
How Germany’s king has caught a cold, and
Britain has won a fight,
And Crete and Greece have at last agreed to bury
their feud and unite.
But hidden away in the “ country news ” is a
letter from Yandaroo —
“There isn’t a blade of grass for miles, and the
stock will never pull through.”
A word or two, but between the lines, in letters
of red and black,
Is a world of history they may read who have
trodden the Hunger-Track.
Six hundred leagues from north to south—
chocolate, black and red,
And women and children hungering for lack of a
crust of bread;
Six hundred leagues from east to west —chocolate,
red and black,
And the womb of the grey land barren and her
dry dugs wrinkled and slack.
The drover through to Wodonga yards with
“ stores ” from the Queensland side,
The traveller camped on the Ninety-Mile where
the mirage waters glide,
The teamster in with the season’s clip from a
hundred miles out back, —
They have paid their toll in Thirst and Sweat at
the gates of the Hunger-Track.
They know the reaches of black despair when the
starving stock go by ;
They have pitched their camps on the bounds of
hell where the bleaching bone-heaps lie ;
They have crossed where long-deserted homes—
lone sentinels of the plain—
Stretch out their hands to the passer-by and cry
to heaven in vain.
They are fighting away on the Hunger-Track as
the weary years run out,
Silent and grim they are battling on with famine
and heat and drought.
One for the sake of a girl down east, one for a
weekly dole,
And one with Hell for the right to live—the prize
of a white man’s soul.
With teeth hard set they grapple with Want and
grip at the throat of Fate,
And laugh as they rush at the last redoubt to
clutch at the goal—too late;
With the pangs of the grey years hot at heart,
they are striving for gold years yet,
And planting a ladder to phantom hope in the
ashes of long regret.
There’s never a day comes out of the east but a
man goes down in the fight
Who has blazed the track that his sons may win to
the heart of their land aright;
There’s never a red sun sinks in the west but a
soul goes out to its God,
That has fought and fallen, as strong men may,
with Death in the track he trod.
But the grey Bush-Mother has reared them well,
• and her children know her moods ;
How she scourges most when she loves them best,
how she hungers and frets and broods,
And they turn to her when the work is done, with
“ Mother, we long for rest! ”
And she gathers them into her outstretched arms
and hides them away in her breast.
They are hoisting a flag in the city streets, with
music, and cheer, and gun,
To tell the world of Australia’s birth and a Nation’s
life begun ;
But out where the desert ends loom wide, that
her worth may be understood,
They are writing Australia’s history and sealing
it deep in blood.
Q.
VELDT.
Ah Ling’s Religion.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ah Ling was short and fat. Moreover he was
religious. He sang hymns in a peculiar falsetto
that made you feel uncharitable towards your
fellow-man and be tempted to heave things at the
singer.
He also went to church twice every Sunday. It
was a treat to see him on that day. He was so
chookful of goodness, he seemed to ooze sanctity
all over. Ah Ling was my servant. His air of
sorrow and commiseration for my ungodliness was
vast.
It is a common saying in China that “ it is im-
possible to convert a Chinaman.” No matter how
genuine the conversion may seem, it invariably
turns out that, unless there is some direct gain in
the matter, John backslides in the end. I was
loth to think this of Ah Ling, but I was sus-
picious, nevertheless. In the first place, I knew
he was robbing me, but I also knew he took good
care that no one else did. This in itself was
serious, but then I knew that plenty good Chris-
tians did the same thing.
His devotion to my interests (apart from that
little “squeeze” I have mentioned) was touching.
At this time I was a bachelor, but was going to get
married.
How to break this news to Ah Ling was hard.
I would fall lower than ever in his estimation, I
felt assured. Eventually I did not break the
news at all. I decided to let him find out for him-
self. I was married in Hongkong, and arrived at
Shanghai at night.
When I knocked at the door, it, was Ah Ling
who opened it. He lifted his eyebrows when he
saw my companion; and, when she came in, I
thought he looked excessively uious. Happening
to steal a side-glance at him, I saw him smile m
himself, and there was a swift Asiatic wink in
that oblique eye. Turning on him, I said,
sharply—
“ What are you grinning at? You wooden-
faced image, take those traps up to my room.”
“ Takee box belong girl ’long your loom, allee
same ? ”
“Yes.”
“Aim.”
I fancied I saw a wicked leer on his face as he
spoke, but the next instant it looked guilelessly
innocent again. What did the beggar mean ? I
determined to have a talk with him in the morn-
ing-
Later that night I happened to go past his
room, and hearing him speaking, stopped and
listened. He was praying :
“ O Lord, wha’for you lettee Missa Blown do
likee allee same Missa Smiff ? One time I wolk
long him ; I sabee he plenty bad man.
“ Allee time he have got plenty sisiter. Some-
time sisiter come stop two, tlee day ; go ’way.
He talkee me.
“* Ah Ling, my sisiter come look see me to-
mollow. You sabee—sisiter ? ’ Hiyah ! I sabee.
“Wha’for he makee too muchee love-pigeon
long he sisiter ?
“ Missa Jones he catchee girl to-night. Bime’bye
be say : ‘ Ah Ling, my sisiter come stop long me
two, tlee day.’ O Chli! ”
I waited to hear no more. Shades of morality !
that was what the beggar meant. I decided that
Ah Ling and I must part. We parted next day.
I have not seen him since.
Also, about two hundred dollars’ worth of our
jewellery and silver have not been seen since.
ALEC. ZANDER.
The Two Wrecks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Smoking-room, Melbourne tavern. Returned
contingenter, almost bent double, the wreck of a
fine man, totters in on arm of a red-cross comrade
and crawls on to couch. They swap yarns. “De
la Rey,” says the Red Cross, “is a fair blanker.
If his crowd catch a bushman, they cut his throat.
That’s ’cos of that young Hollander at Klipfon-
tein. He shot a captain, a lieutenant and a
trooper, also eight horses. They couldn’t make
out where the trouble was cornin’ from. Sud-
denly they spotted him —he was behind a sand-
hill. When he seen they’d spotted him, he slings
up his arms. But they was narked—real furious.
They riddled him with bullets, then bayoneted
him all to pieces ; never saw such a mess in
my life. One gash started just ’ere (bending
over and touching his hip-bone) an’ come
out ’ere (indicating the back of his neck).
The Colonel wanted ’em to carry the carcase
down and bury it. The whole troop point-
blank refused.”
Then the man with a twisted spine told
his tale, with many cheerful oaths: “The
worst thing I seen was after Commando
Nek. We got an old Boer that we called
John, for short. He was a silly old rabbit. We
was ordered to march, and, of course, John was
expected to march too. But ’e didn’t want to shift.
‘Come.on,’ sez a trooper, ‘shift!’ But the old
Boer shook his head and sat down. ’E was a silly,
stubborn old rabbit. ‘ Well, you can blankin’
well stay where y’ are,’ sez the trooper, and he
give’m the bayonet; jobbed it right through ’im
into the ground. Funniest thing ever I seen in
me life.” They both laugh, though the wounded
wreck gets a fit of coughing in consequence.
Then Red Cross tells of a Maorilander who was
“copped” by De la Rey’s commando. “The
Boers stripped him bare, but gave him a ragged
pair of moles. Prisoner asked for his hat, as the
sun was very hot. De la Rey happened along,
and the Maorilander sez, * Are you the
general?’ ‘Yes,’sez De la Rey. ‘Well, surely
you won’t let your fellers take a man’s ’at in the
sun like this.’ ‘ Give ’im ’is ’at,’ sez De la Rey—
so they give it to him. ‘ Now, you just clear out,’
sez De la Rey. ‘ But I can’t travel like this,’ sez
the Maorilander, showin’ his nakedness. ‘You’re
what I call a blanky lot of blanks,’ he sez. With
that, De la Rey, who had a shooter in one hand
and a sjambok in the other, made a welt at him
with the sjambok. And the Maorilander
cleared across the veldt like ’ell.” The wounded
man started off with another cheerful bayonet-
story ; but this scribe didn’t stay for the finish.
J. DRAYMAN.
Writes a Queensland contingenter : .
We are living on fresh mutton, as there are thousands
of sheep (splendid ones, too). Starve the Boers out!
Why, they have beef and mutton to keep them going for
20 years. The only thing that will stop them is want of
ammunition, and they capture enough of that to keep
them going almost, what with capturing trains and
convoys.
The testimony of English Tommies seems to bear
this out.
A. J. Fischer writes to The Bulletin
from London (whence he is returning soon): —
I have spent most of my time here at the Westminster
Art School under Mont Loudan. It is considered one of
the best on this side. Loudan teaches on very broad
lines, and tries to develop the art in one, not drive his
own in. . . . Paris, I find, is trading on its past reputa-
tion. Hatherell says so; and Heysen, the Adelaide
student, thinks so also. He is coming to London next
year (that is for the term which starts in September)
to study under Mont Loudan. He was very much
taken with L’s work in last year’s Academy. French
art of to-day, with few exceptions, is on a level
with the London music-halls (if I might draw the
comparison)—loud, clever, suggestive, and emptv ; while
English art ranks with the highest drama and comedy—
has strength, quiet passages, fine feeling, good colour
&c. And English artists recognise the beauty of women,
and paint them as in Ralph Peacock’s “Sisters” in the
Tate gallery. A visit to the Tate gallery, after a stay in
Paris, gives one a great respect for English art.
I met Fullwood on a ’bus in Piccadilly the other dav
He could not get on with the Yanks, so is going to tr.\
London. He has managed to get a picture in the RoyaJ
Academy as a start. Spence and Hopwood also hav<
pictures in. Spence’s looks like a postage-stamj
among all the large stuff. Clever Streeton has had
bad luck so far as the Academy is concerned.
I could see by the Royal Academy students'
exhibition that the R.A. wants correct tone,
good drawing, and a certain amount of finish. . . .
I think Mahony would get along here ; for there are very
few good animal draughtsmen and painters. Cato'n
Woodville gets a lot of dash in his pictures, but his paint-
ing is very much off, and his color is bad. There is not a
good animal painting in the Academy, and I dare say it
is Spence’s animal painting which helps him there, for he
is as good as any of them. Abbey and La Thangue are
the colorists of the R.A. Abbey’s “ Crusaders Sighting
Jerusalem ”is a marvellous piece of color. I met a clever
Australian here—Reg. Robinson, of Newcastle, N.S.W.
He holds several art scholarships, and promises to do
some good work in the future. Heysen, the Adelaide
student, is a modest fellow, who will go far, although this
is his second year here. He has some pictures in the
Salon. I heard before I met him that he was considered
one of the cleverest students in Paris at present. Minns
has not quite got over his long illness yet, but he is doing
good work. I swore all through the winter, and longed
for Australia; but London is not a bad place in summer:
Just now it is delightful.
